The author of this book, having completed for you, my children, this book, which is a complete part of the science of medicine, and having reached its ultimate clarity and explanation, has seen fit to complete it for you with this section, which is a part of practical application.
Because practical application is completely lacking in our country and in our time, so much so that the science of it is almost forgotten and its trace is lost. But only a few traces of it remain in the books of the ancients, which have been corrupted by hands and distorted by error and confusion, so that its meanings are obscure and its benefit distant.
Therefore, I have seen fit to revive it and compose this section on it through explanation, clarity, and summarization, and to present the images of cautery irons and other instruments of practice, since this is an addition to the explanation and contains what is needed.
The reason that there is no skilled practitioner with skilled hands in our time is that the craft of medicine is lengthy, and its practitioner must first practice the science of anatomy, as described by Galen. So that he may understand the benefits of the organs, their nature and humors, their connections and separations, and the knowledge of the bones, nerves, and muscles, their number and exits, the pulsating and non-pulsating veins, and the locations of their exits.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are numerous in name but few in deed, especially in the craft of the hand.  We have mentioned a part of this in the introduction of this book.
Because whoever is not knowledgeable about the anatomy we have mentioned is liable to commit errors that kill people, as I have witnessed many who imagined themselves knowledgeable in this field and claimed it without knowledge or understanding.
I saw an ignorant physician who incised a swine's-nose tumor on a woman's neck, and severed some of the neck's arteries, causing the woman to bleed to death before his eyes.
I saw another physician who advanced in removing a stone from an old man, and the stone was large.  He acted rashly and removed it with a piece of bladder tissue. The man died about three days later.  I was called to remove it, and upon seeing the size of the stone and the patient's condition, I realized that I could not have done it.
And I saw another physician who practiced medicine for hire among some of the merchants of our city.
A black boy, with a fracture near his heel and a wound, came to him.  The physician, in his ignorance, quickly bound the fracture to the wound tightly with splints and bandages, not leaving any breathing space for the wound.
Then he yielded to his desires, and left him for days, ordering him not to loosen the bandage until his leg and foot swelled, and he was on the verge of death.
So I was called to him, and I quickly loosened the bandage. He found relief and was free from his pain, but the corruption had already become firmly established in the limb, and I could not prevent it.
The corruption continued to spread throughout the limb until he died.
I saw another physician treat a malignant tumor.  After a few days, it ulcerated, and the patient's condition worsened.  This is because if the cancer is purely composed of black humors, it should never be treated with iron.
Unless it is in a limb that can be entirely removed.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual work is divided into two categories: work that is accompanied by safety, and work that, in most cases, results in injury.
I have warned in every instance in this book about work that involves risk and fear, and you should avoid it.
So that the ignorant do not find a way to speak ill of you, be firm and cautious, and be gentle and steadfast in your treatment.
Use the best path leading to safety and a good outcome, and avoid serious and difficult-to-cure illnesses.
Keep yourselves free from what you fear might cause doubt in your religion and your worldly affairs, for it is more lasting for your reputation and raises your standing in this world and the next.
Galen said in some of his precepts: Do not treat a disease poorly and thus become a poor physician.
This article is divided into three chapters.
Chapter one.
In cauterization by fire and cauterization by a sharp medicine, a chapter is presented, arranged from the heel to the foot, and showing the instruments, cauterizing tools, and all that is needed for manual work.
And the second chapter.
Concerning incision, lancing, phlebotomy, cupping, surgery, extraction of arrows, and the like, all presented in an organized manner, with illustrations of the instruments.
And the third chapter.
Concerning fractures, dislocations, treatment of sprains, and the like, presented in an organized manner from the heel to the foot, with illustrations of the instruments.
The first chapter.
Concerning cauterization.
Before describing its application, it is necessary to discuss its benefits, drawbacks, and in which temperament it is used.
I say that discussing the benefits and drawbacks of cauterization, its detailed science, and hidden secrets, is a lengthy discourse and a subject of deep knowledge which many wise men have discussed.
I have condensed the limited amount of their discourse, fearing to become verbose.
I say that cauterization is generally beneficial for all bad temperaments with or without material, except for two temperaments: the hot temperament without material and the dry temperament without material.
As for the dry temperament with material, they differed. Some said that cauterization is beneficial, while others argued the opposite, that cauterization is not suitable for an illness resulting from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
It is impossible to be cured of a hot, dry disease with a hot, dry remedy.  The one who argues the opposite said that cauterization with fire might benefit from a hot, dry disease that occurs in human bodies. Because when you add the human body and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the human body cold.
And I say with him, because experience has shown me that repeatedly, although it should not be imagined in this matter except by one who has diligently practiced and trained in the art of cauterization to a great degree. And he must understand the differences in the temperaments of people, the nature of diseases themselves, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for the other temperaments, there is no fear from them, especially cold, moist diseases, for all physicians have agreed on them, and there is no disagreement concerning the benefit of cauterization in them.
Know, my children, that the secret of treatment by cauterization with fire and its superiority over cauterization with a burning medicine is because fire is a simple element whose effect is limited to the organ cauterized and it does not harm another connected organ except in a minor way.
Cauterization with burning medicine, however, may extend its effect to other organs and perhaps induce a disease in the organ that is difficult to treat, or even kill it.  Fire, owing to its nobility and purity of essence, will not do this unless it is excessive.
This has become clear to us through experience, due to the length of service, care in the craft, and understanding the truth of the matter.
Therefore, I have dispensed with lengthy explanations.  Were it not unbefitting this book of mine, I would have presented to you the secret and method of fire's action on bodies and its removal of diseases using a precise philosophical and demonstrative discourse beyond your comprehension.
Know, my children, that there is disagreement concerning the appropriate time for cauterization, and they consider spring to be the best time.
I say that cauterization is suitable in any time, for the harm caused by the time is absorbed by the benefit brought about by the cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for a strong, necessary ailment, a deferred one that cannot be delayed, lest a greater harm results than the minor damage caused by time.
Do not, my children, consider what the common people and ignorant physicians imagine, that cauterization, which cures a disease, never returns and treat it as a curse.
This is not as you have imagined, for cauterization is like a medicine that alters the temperament and dries the humidity which is the cause of the ailments.
However, cauterization is preferred to medicine because of its speed of success, strength of action, and intensity of its power.
The disease may return sometime in the future, depending on the patient's temperament, the strength and severity of the disease, the accumulation of harmful elements in the body, and the patient's neglect in acquiring appropriate nourishment, and similar causes.
Unless the disease for which cauterization is used is a mild disease in a part of the body with few harmful elements and humidity. For example, cauterizing a tooth for pain, and similar cases, the pain may not return, and this will be at least the case.
Also, the common saying that cauterization is the final stage of medicine is a correct statement, not in the way they believe, for they think there is no treatment that will be useful, with or without medicine, after cauterization, but the matter is different.
The meaning of "cauterization is the final stage of medicine" is that when we have used various treatments for a disease and those remedies have not been effective, and then we use cauterization as the last resort, and it is successful, then in this case, cauterization is the final stage of medicine. Not in the sense understood by the general public and many ignorant physicians.
The ancients stated that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron.  They said this because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its substance, and they said that the cauterized area does not fester.  However, this is not universally true.
Because I have experimented and found that this is only true in some bodies and not others.  Cauterization with gold is better and superior to iron, as they said, except that if you heat a gold cautery in fire, you cannot readily determine when it has been heated to the desired degree of gold's redness.
Because cold quickly reaches it, and if you increase its heat in the fire, it melts and fuses, causing the craftsman to be involved in extra work.  Therefore, cauterization with iron is, in our opinion, quicker and closer to correct for practical application, God willing.
I have organized this chapter on cauterization into sections, arranged from head to foot, to make it easier for the student to find what he needs, God willing.
The First Chapter.
Cauterization of the head: one cauterization.
This cauterization is beneficial for excess moisture and cold in the brain, which are the cause of headaches, frequent colds running from the head down to the eye and ear areas, excessive sleepiness, toothaches, and sore throats. Generally, for all diseases caused by cold, such as paralysis, epilepsy, and stupor, and similar ailments.
The procedure for this cauterization is that the patient should first be made to vomit with a purgative medicine which cleanses the head for three or four nights, depending on the patient's strength, age, and usual habits.
Then, order him to shave his head with a razor, and then have him sit before you, squatting, with his hands placed on his chest.
Then place the base of your palm on the base of his nose between his eyes.  Wherever your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-oil cauterizing iron, which has this form.
If the bone is exposed to the extent of a needle's head or the size of a carbuncle, raise your hand; otherwise, reheat the iron, or use another if the first has cooled. Until you see the bone as I described, then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a piece of cotton in it. Place it on the affected area and leave it for three days.
Then apply a cotton swab soaked in clarified butter and leave it on until the burning sensation subsides.  Then treat it with the four-part ointment until it heals, God willing.
They said that the longer a wound remains open, the more pus it produces, and this is better and more beneficial.
Some have said to cauterize the skin down to the bone, holding the cautery until some of the bone's thickness is burned.  Then, to remove the burned bone, and then to treat the wound.
Others said that it is necessary to use excessive cauterization, affecting the bone strongly, until it falls off like a small piece of charcoal or a small particle. They claimed that this releases head vapors from the area and leaves the wound open for a long time.
Then treat until it heals. I do not at all see these two types of cauterization as beneficial, except for some people, and in a reckless manner.  Leaving it untreated is better in my opinion, and with safety, because the head weakens when its natural connections are disrupted, as we have observed in other organs.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cauterization is safer and better in my opinion.  Use it, and you will be safe, God willing.
Second Chapter.
Concerning cauterization of the head also.
If a chronic pain arises throughout the head, afflicting the patient, and if applications, fomentations, whippings, ointments, and dressings have been used, especially if a single cauterization, which we have described, has not helped, then examine. If the patient's head is strong by nature and not weak, and if he experiences intense cold, cauterize again slightly above the previous one. Then cauterize each horn of his head with a cautery until the thickness of the skin is gone and the bone is exposed to the extent we have described. And cauterize with a cautery at the back of his head, in the area known as the "axe" [spot].  Be gentle here, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience intense pain unlike the pain of the other head cauterizations. I will mention this cautery in its proper place.
The cautery used for the horns and back of the head should be gentler than the cautery used for the middle of the head.  This is its image/form.
Chapter Three.
For the non-chronic sister's cautery.
If pain occurs in the head's fissure, accompanied by a headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then the patient should be purged with drugs that clear the head.  Use all other treatments that I have mentioned in the categories of illnesses. If that does not succeed, the cautery should be applied in two ways. Either by cautery with a sharp, burning medicine, or with iron.
As for cautery with a burning medicine, take one clove of garlic, peel it, and cut its ends from both sides. Then, slit the affected area of the temple with a wide scalpel, until a space is created under the skin that will accommodate the clove.
Insert it into the skin until it disappears. Then, apply firm pressure with a bandage. Leave it for approximately fifteen hours, then remove it and take out the garlic. Allow the wound to heal for two or three days, then apply a dressing.
Apply a cotton ball soaked in ghee until the area becomes suppurated.  Then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If you wish, you can accomplish this with some of the burning medicines that I have presented in the eighteenth article on burning medicines.
And as for cauterizing it with iron, it is done in this way: Heat the cautery iron, which has this shape:.
It is called a nail-shaped cautery because its head is shaped like a nail, with some ridges, and a small projection in the center.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while gently rotating the iron.  The amount of skin burned should be about half its thickness. Raise your hand so as not to burn the artery beneath it and cause bleeding, then soak a cotton ball in salt water and place it on the area, leaving it for three days. Then coat the cotton with ghee, and treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
If you wish, cauterize this wound with the sharp, knife-like end of the cautery, and take care to avoid cutting the artery in this particular chronic wound.
Chapter Four.
Treating chronic wounds.
If you have treated the wound with the aforementioned treatments and the treatments mentioned in the sections on diseases, and the treatment is ineffective, and you see a cause that does not respond to the initial cauterization with medicine or fire cauterization. You should heat the knife-like cautery until it glows white, after tracing the painful area with a line about half a finger in length or similar.  Hold it once, tightening it until the artery is cut and you reach the bone.
However, you must be careful not to touch the jaw that moves during chewing, lest you burn the muscle or nerve that moves it, causing spasm.
Be cautious and vigilant regarding the bleeding from the artery you cut, for there is danger in cutting it. Especially for someone unfamiliar with the procedure and lacking proven experience; abandoning the treatment is preferable.
Treatment of accidental bleeding from the artery will be discussed in its proper place in the book, God willing. If you see a condition that this cauterization will not address, then...
And if you see the patient's body capable of tolerating it, cauterize the scalp in the center as we described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
If you wish, you may use the cauterization we described in the chapter on treating arteries with the two-bladed cautery; it is a better and more effective cauterization than this one.
Chapter Five.
On cauterizing earaches.
If an earache occurs due to a cold, and it has been treated with laxatives and other remedies that we mentioned in the classification, and the pain does not subside, cauterize the ear with the cautery called the "point," which is depicted in this image.
Then, after heating it, apply it around the entire ear, or around both ears if the pain is in both, as you would rotate it around. Move the cautery slightly away from the base of the ear after marking the spot with ink. The cautery should be about ten points in size for each ear, or thereabouts. Then, treat the affected areas until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Six.
On cauterization with the cautery.
The abscesses that are treated with cauterization are of the type that arise from phlegm, as I mentioned in the classification of diseases.
One should avoid cauterizing the type that arises from dryness and nerve spasm.
If, having treated this type of abscess with poultices, massage, and gargles, your treatment has not been successful, then the affected area should be cauterized three times. One at the base of the ear, the second slightly below the temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
Make your cauterization on the opposite side of the affected area, because relaxation occurs on the side that appears healthy.
The cauterization should be performed as follows:  One cauterization should be applied opposite the upper part of the ear, slightly below the crown of the head, and another in the temple, and its length should be the same as the length of the thumb.
Bring your cauterizing implement down until you have burned approximately half the thickness of the skin.  This is the image of the cauterizing iron, a type of blade previously described, but slightly gentler as you see. The blade should have a slightly increased thickness, then treat the area as previously mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
On cauterization for chronic stroke.
If the stroke is chronic and you have treated it as we have described, and your treatment is unsuccessful and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize four times. One cauterization on each temple, one cauterization in the middle of the head, as we mentioned, and one cauterization at the back of the head, as previously described.
Using the cautery as previously described, and a cauterization might also be applied to the mouth of the stomach, which would be more effective, then treat as previously described.
Chapter Eight.
On cauterization for forgetfulness.
One afflicted with phlegmatic forgetfulness should first be given large doses of emetics and purgatives that clear the brain.
Then his entire head is shaved and a mustard poultice, as described in the chapter on dressings, is applied to his back repeatedly; for it is a form of cauterization.
And do this in exactly the same way as mentioned there. If he is cured by this, then stop. Otherwise, cauterize three times on the back of his head, arranged in a line from the top of his head to the base of his neck.
Leave a finger's breadth between each cauterization.  Then treat the cauterization as previously described. If you want to increase the treatment and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle cauterization.
If you wish to increase the treatment, cauterize the temples. Then treat it until he is healed. The cautery should be olive-shaped, in the manner previously described.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterization for paralysis and relaxation of the entire body.
One should proceed with cleansing the head with the remedies previously mentioned. Then shave the patient's head and cauterize a mark in the center of the head, a mark on each temple, and a mark on the back of the head.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck.  If, in a case of relaxation of the body, more than that is required and the patient can tolerate it, and the disease is strong and firmly established, cauterize four marks on the vertebrae of his back. Cauterize until most of the skin is burned, then raise your hand. Then treat it as previously mentioned until it is healed, God willing.  Use an olive oil-based cautery.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterization for epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is due to phlegm should be cauterized.  First, his brain should be cleansed with large cupping glasses and with all the other treatments we have described in the classification, if the patient is large and can tolerate medication.
However, if he is a child who cannot tolerate medication, then he should use gargles and cleansing mouthwashes for the brain many days before, while improving his diet.
Then his head should be shaved, and one cauterization should be performed in the middle of the head, as previously described in the description, and another cauterization in the back of the head. And a cauterization on each temple of the head.  If the patient is strong and can tolerate it, cauterize with the cauterizations mentioned in the case of paralysis and weakness of the body on the cervical and dorsal vertebrae.  The cautery should be olive-shaped, in the manner described previously.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery gentle in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
In cauterizing melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is putrid moisture and thick phlegm, cauterize with the cauterizations we mentioned in the case of paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is excess tending towards black bile and the patient's body is moist and corrupt, evacuate what purifies the brain as previously described in the division.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tight cake of linen like a circle, then place it in the center of the head while the patient sits upright and holds it from all sides. Then take one pound of old sheep's butter.
Then heat it over a moderate fire, to the point where a finger can tolerate it when inserted.  Then pour it into the circle on the patient's head, and let it cool. Do this once a week with the rest of your good treatment until, God willing, he is cured.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small, scattered points, without holding the cauterizing iron in your hand, but rather by a light touch, for this type of cauterization moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a linen cloth soaked in butter or chicken fat.
Chapter Twelve.
Concerning cauterizing water that flows from the eye.
If you perceive the initial signs of water flowing from the eye as described in the classifications, then promptly treat the patient.  Clear his head, protect it from all moisture, and sweat him in a bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave his head and cauterize with a cautery in the center of the head, and then cauterize twice on the temples if the water is flowing from both eyes, or on the temple of the affected side if only one eye is affected.
Then, with the cautery, cut all the veins and arteries under the skin, and let the cauterizations be long and wide over the temples. Take precautions against bleeding.
If you see anything of it [the affected area], cut it at the site with any treatment possible.  We will discuss the wisdom of dealing with arteries, and their cutting while preventing bleeding.
He may be cauterized twice deeply on the back of the head, beneath the bones.
Chapter Thirteen.
On cauterizing chronic tears.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and persistent, and originate from veins and arteries on the exterior of the head, and you are certain that it is due to thick, cold, phlegmatic excess, cauterize it using the cautery method described for the initial, descending water. One cautery in the center of the head, two cauteries on the temples, and two cauteries on the back of the head beneath the bones. If further cauterization is required, cauterize on each side of the eye's tail at the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
On cauterizing foul odors of the nose.
If treatment with the methods described in the classification is unsuccessful, promptly cauterize the patient's temples for three nights.  Then shave his head and cauterize the middle area with the olive-oil cautery iron. Then cauterize with the nail-shaped cautery iron, two cauteries above the eyebrows, slightly under the hair.  Protect the artery; do not sever it.  This is the image of the nail-shaped cautery.
Chapter Fifteen.
On cauterizing relaxation of the eyelid.
If the eyelid relaxes due to disease or moisture, cauterize the eyelid once with this crescent-shaped cautery iron.
And if you wish, cauterize above the eyebrows, two cauteries on each side, spaced apart from the temples.  The length of each cautery should be the length of the eyebrow.
Do not overdo the cauterization, but only to the point where one-third of the skin is burned.  The cautery iron should be of this shape.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If its eyelashes turn inward, injuring the eye, then there are two types of cauterization for it. Either cauterization with fire, or cauterization with a burning medicine.  As for cauterizing with fire, first order the patient to remove his eyelashes, if he is one who plucks them, so that they grow long and even.
If he has been injured while they are growing, then bind his eyes with a bandage so they do not move until they grow.  When they have grown and evened out, place the patient's head on your lap. Then mark the eyelid with ink, in the shape of a willow leaf.
Begin the mark near the eyelashes. Then place a cotton swab soaked in egg white or in the saliva of a badger under the eyelid.  Then heat an iron, this is its image.
Then cauterize in the shape you marked, little by little, in many applications, until the surface of the skin, which is entirely like the shape of the willow leaf, is burned.  A sign of your successful work is to see that the eyelid is rolled back and the hair is raised off the eye's flesh.
Then raise your hand and leave it for three days. Then apply a cotton ball with ghee until the scab is removed, then treat it with ointment until it is healed, God willing. If any hair grows back after a time and the eyelid relaxes.
Then repeat the cauterization in that place as you did at first. If the hair is in the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and does not prick the eye.  As for cauterization with the burning medicine, instruct the patient to leave the eyelashes until they grow long and even.
Then make a picture of a willow leaf from paper. Then take from the usual soap and quicklime, each one a weight of a dirham or the like.  Grind them both well, and do this quickly, so they do not dry. Then spread some of this mixture onto the paper you made, shaped like a willow leaf, and place it on the eyelid, either one or both. Place a cotton ball soaked in egg white under the eye and the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger on the medicine and press it gently, moving it as the patient feels the burning of the medicine, because it causes a burning sensation like fire.  As long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine and move it with your finger. When the burning subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And see if you find the eyelid has risen, as it rises with cauterization by fire or by cutting. Otherwise, apply more of the medicine to the area where it hasn't taken effect and hasn't blackened, until you have completed your work and the eye has been cauterized.  Then place cotton with oil on it until the burning subsides.
Then treat it with palm ointment or another, until it heals. You should take utmost care during the procedure that no medicine falls into the eye.  If the eyelid loosens after a few days and we fear something from the hair in the eye.
Then reapply the medicine to that loosened area of the eyelid, specifically, as you did initially, and treat it until it heals. This is an illustration of a willow leaf.
Know that people's eyes vary in size and age.  Therefore, adjust your cauterization accordingly.  The correct method is not hidden from one who has experience in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the fistula that appears in the eye's eyelid.
If you have treated the fistula with the methods we described in the sections on diseases, and your treatment has not been successful, then it is necessary to cauterize it in this way.
Instruct the patient to place his head on your lap, and have an attendant hold the head firmly in your hands, without allowing any movement or shaking of the head.
Then place a cotton swab moistened with egg white or with the juice of the prickly pear on his eyes, and then heat the cautery, which is pictured as follows.
It should be hollowed out, like the shaft of an eagle's feather on one end, where the cautery will be applied; if you prefer, the other end can be pierced or solid, like a cylinder; however, the hollowed-out type is better for your procedure, God willing.
Then, if the fistula is open, squeeze it to remove the matter, and dry it; or if it is not open, open it and remove the pus.  Then, place the extremely hot cautery on it, holding it with your hand until you reach the bone.
Keep your hand slightly away from the eye toward the nose when applying the cautery, so that your hand does not miss or the patient becomes agitated, causing the cautery to fall onto the eyelid and damage it.  If the bone is reached on the first application, then do nothing more; otherwise, repeat the process with the cautery if needed.
Leave it for three days, then apply cotton soaked in oil and treat it with a dried ointment until it heals. If forty days pass without healing, apply a strong, corrosive medicine to expose the bone and treat it as described in the appropriate section, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing a fistula, mentioned by some early scholars, involves approaching the fistula site, incising it, and then placing a thin funnel within the incision – this is its image.
Pour in melted lead weighing a dirham, holding the funnel firmly and steadily in your hand.  The patient must remain absolutely still so that the melted lead does not drip into their eye.  A cotton pad soaked in egg white or water should be placed over the patient's eye.
The lead burns the fistula, curing it remarkably.  If the fistula heals with the described cautery and treatment, then do nothing more; otherwise, the nasal passage must be opened and the fistula returned to its proper nasal channel, as will be discussed in its specific section.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing a cleft lip.
A cleft in the lip, often called a fissure, frequently occurs, especially in the lips of boys.  If the cleft, treated as described in the classification, does not yield, then heat a small, knife-like cautery iron in this manner...
Make its cavity as thin as a knife, then place it, hot, quickly into the cleft itself until the cauterization reaches the depth of the cleft.  Then treat it with qirrot, so that it may heal, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing a fistula occurring in the mouth.
If a tumor appears at the base of the gums, or in the palate, or at the roots of the teeth, then ulcerates and bursts, forming a fistula from the pus, and treatment is unsuccessful, then a cautery iron should be heated to a size appropriate for the fistula.
Then, insert it, hot, into the fistula's opening, and hold your hand until the heated iron reaches its bottom and end.  Do this once or twice, then treat it subsequently with the remedies mentioned previously, until it heals, God willing.
If the material is severed and it heals, then well and good. Otherwise, the area must be examined and any corrupt bone removed, as will be described in its respective section, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Loose teeth and gums.
If the gums have loosened due to moisture, and the teeth have shifted, and medication has been ineffective, then place the patient's head in your lap, and heat the cauterizing iron, whose image will come later, after placing the tube on the tooth.
Quickly insert the hot cauterizing iron, hold your hand for a short time until the patient feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth, then remove your hand, then repeat the procedure with the cauterizing iron as many times as desired, and then the patient should fill their mouth with salt water.
He should hold it for an hour and spit it out. Then the loose tooth will become firm, the relaxed gums will tighten, and the putrid moisture will dry up.
Chapter Twenty-one.
On cauterizing toothache.
If the toothache is due to cold or worms, and medication is ineffective, cauterization can be performed in two ways: either with oil or with fire.
As for cauterization with oil, take cow's ghee and boil it in an iron ladle or a shell. Then take a piece of cotton, wrap it around the end of a probe, dip it in the hot ghee, and quickly place it on the aching tooth, holding it until it cools.
Then repeat this several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth.  Alternatively, you can dip wool or cotton in cold ghee, place it on the aching tooth, and then apply a heated iron on top until the fire reaches the tooth's base.
As for cauterization with fire, use a copper or iron tube, somewhat thick-walled to prevent the heat from reaching the patient's mouth. Then heat the cautery iron, place it on the same tooth, and hold it until it cools, repeating this several times.
The pain will subside either on the same day or the next.  Following the cauterization, the patient should fill his mouth with good ghee and hold it for an hour, then spit it out.  This is the form of the cautery iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you choose, as appropriate.  This is the form of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On cauterizing pigs.
If pigs are affected by phlegm and cold humors, and do not respond to maturation with medicines, and you want rapid maturation, heat the hollow cautery, the form of which is depicted here, with two pierced ends.
To allow smoke to exit from the other end during the cauterization, apply the protected cautery to the same tumor once and again if needed, until it reaches the depth of the tumor. If the tumor is small, make the cautery the same size as the tumor.
Then leave it for three days and apply a cotton pad soaked in clarified butter to remove what was burned by fire. Then treat it with ointment and bandages until it heals, God willing.
Chapter twenty-three.
On cauterization for hoarseness of voice and shortness of breath.
If phlegm predominates in the trachea, especially if accompanied by a cold temperament, the patient should first be purged with laxative remedies, then cauterized with a cautery at the notch of the neck, near the base of the throat, at the lowest point.
Be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, and burn only half a finger's breadth of skin. Then cauterize another time at the joint of the neck, at the last bead of it, vigorously.  Use a nail-shaped cautery as described previously.
Then treat him with what was previously mentioned until he recovers, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On cauterizing lung disease and cough.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold humors, and there is no fever or consumption in the patient, and the illness is chronic, cauterize twice above the collarbones, in the soft, empty, lower areas, and once more in the center of the chest between the breasts.
And the cauterizing iron should be pointed, as described previously.  If you prefer, let your cauterization be by dripping with the cauterizing iron called the "point," and its image was previously given in the section on cauterizing ear pain.  The points should be approximately thirty in number.
Then treat him with what has been previously mentioned until he is cured.  A cauterizing iron with three prongs may be made in this manner, and it is used for quicker cauterization because you can apply three cauterizations at once; this is the image of the cauterization iron.
Chapter Twenty-five.
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus is dislocated due to slippery moisture or if it is not properly secured after reduction, so that it habitually relocates and then dislocates at the slightest movement, as we have observed, then the joint should first be reduced. Then the patient lies on their back or on their right side, and the skin inside the armpit is lifted upwards with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint is dislocated inward.
Then the double-bladed cautery, whose image is this, is heated.
Then the skin is cauterized with it until it penetrates to the other side, and the shape of the cauterization is four cauterizations.  It may be cauterized with a three-bladed cautery, and the shape of the cauterization will then be six cauterizations, with the blades placed along the thickness of the tissue.
This is the image of the three-bladed cautery.
And one may be added to this number, making the cauterizations eight. Then, the crushed balls with salt are applied to the cauterization, and the patient must keep the affected area still and not move the limb for a time until it strengthens.  If the dislocation is upward, it is rare.
Cauterize above the shoulder with one good, nail-like cautery, or several small dots, then the joint will strengthen, the moisture will disappear, and the patient will recover.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On cauterizing the stomach.
If the stomach is afflicted with cold and excessive moisture, to the point of deranging its temperament, and frequent discharges occur, and various treatments have been ineffective, then the patient should lie on their back with legs and arms extended, and three cauterizations should be performed. Cauterize below the breastbone, to the extent of a finger, with a nail-like cautery iron.  And cauterize two more points, each below the sides of the first cautery, so the cauteries form a triangle. Maintain a distance between them, lest they merge if they become infected.  The depth of the cauterization should be about two-thirds the thickness of the skin, and the shape of the cauterizations should be…
In this manner and to this degree, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with one large cautery in the middle of the stomach, in this manner.
And the cautery iron used for this single cauterization should be circular in shape, in this manner.
The stomach may also be cauterized by點, for those who fear this cauterization.  Mark points on the stomach to the desired extent with ink, then cauterize them with a pointed cautery iron, and then treat with the previous treatment until healed.
Chapter Twenty-seven.
Concerning cauterization of the cold liver.
If a cold and moist ailment or a hot wind, which has exceeded its natural state, arises from the liver, and the patient has been treated as described in the classification, and that treatment has not been successful. Then the patient should lie on their back, and three cauteries should be marked with ink, precisely this shape and size, on the liver, below the collarbones, where the human elbow ends.
And after each cautery, there should be a finger's breadth between each cautery.  The cauterization should be along the body, straight, and do not press the iron down hard.
Let the burn mark on the skin's thickness be no more than half of that, no more.  The patient should be standing on their feet, or if not able to stand, lying with legs extended and arms raised.  This is the shape of the cautery iron.
You may cauterize these marks with a knife-like cautery iron, if you have skill and dexterity in the craft, and be careful not to overdo the cauterization, burning all the skin, burning the abdomen, and reaching the intestines.  The skin there is thin, so know this.
Chapter Twenty-eight.
For cauterizing a tumor of the liver.
If a boil appears on the liver, and you wish to know whether the tumor is in the liver's flesh or its membrane, if it is in the liver's flesh, the patient will experience heaviness and pain, but without intensity.
And if it is in the liver's membrane, the pain will be accompanied by intense sharpness, and if you see that the physicians have exhausted its treatment, then the patient should lie on his back, and the swollen area should be marked with ink.
Then, the cautery, which resembles a shovel, should be heated in the fire; this is its image. Cauterize it with a single cauterization, until all the skin is burned and the cauterization reaches the membrane, so that the entire pus is removed; then treat it with the treatment for boils until it heals.
This type of cauterization should only be performed by someone who has extensive experience in medicine and has treated these diseases repeatedly; only then should one attempt such a procedure.  To leave it to someone else is better.
Chapter Twenty-nine.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients recommended cauterizing a cold fistula with the roots of the wild rue.  Take one of the longest, dried roots of wild rue, as thick as a finger, dip it in oil, and hold it in the fire. Then cauterize once between the collarbone and the neck, and two small cauteries slightly angled towards the underside of the chin, and two larger cauteries above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two more cauteries between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly angled backwards; another cautery in the middle of the chest, and one above the stomach; and three cauteries from behind, one between the shoulder blades and two on the sides of the lower ribs, below the cautery between the shoulder blades.
One should not insert the cautery deeply, but rather apply it superficially to the skin, as a cauterization.  Some ancient authors mention that certain individuals used a heated iron implement resembling a plowshare, heating it and using it to reach the tumor itself, by passing it between the ribs.
The pus will drain, as described with a liver tumor; and in this case of using cauterization for a tumor, there is a risk that the patient will die immediately or that a fistula will develop at the site, without healing.
Chapter Thirty.
In cauterizing the spleen.
If, after treating the spleen with the methods previously described, your treatment proves ineffective, cauterization may be applied in three ways, all of which are correct: one is to cauterize it three or four times in a row along the length of the spleen.
In the shape of the liver cauterizations previously described, with the interval between each cauterization equal to or slightly greater than the thickness of a finger.  Use the same cauterizing instrument as described for liver cauterization.  Do not insert the cauterizing instrument deeply.  The patient should be lying on their back.
The other method is at the elbow.  Lift the skin opposite the spleen, where the patient's left elbow ends, lifting the skin across the width of the body so that the cauterizations fall along the body's length.
Then, insert the cauterizing irons, very carefully, until they pierce the skin on the other side. Then remove the cauterizing irons, resulting in four cauterizations.  If desired, use the three-pronged cauterizing iron instead.
Then treat the cauterized area after allowing it to fester for many days, for this is more effective than any of the previously mentioned treatments.
Chapter Thirty-one.
On cauterizing dropsy.
Cauterization is helpful only in ascites specifically. If you have treated the dropsy patient with the various treatments that we have described in the divisions, and your treatment has not been effective, then you should cauterize four times around the navel and once on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver, one on the spleen, and two behind the back, one opposite his chest and the other opposite his stomach.  The depth of the cauterization should be close to the thickness of the skin. Then leave the cauterization open to fester for a long time.
Do not neglect the patient's treatment after the cauterization as is appropriate to bring together the two issues so that healing is rapid. The shape of the cauterizing iron used for the back is pointed, as previously described, and that used for the chest is ovoid.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If they swell in dropsy and fill with yellow water, one should cauterize on the back of the foot at the point between the little finger and the ring finger.
Hold your hand with the cauterizing iron straight, do not bend it, then lift your hand and do not return it; this will draw out the yellow water, and the cauterizing iron should be of this shape.
Then cauterize the legs, two cauterizations on each leg, using the sharp edge of the cauterizing iron.  Cauterize once just below the knee, and another below that, towards the middle of the leg.  Make two cauterizations on each thigh as well.
Leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time, so that the water drains out. Then treat it with the rest of the methods we have described.
Chapter Thirty-three.
On cauterizing diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is due to cold and dampness, weakening the digestive and binding powers of the stomach and intestines, and other treatments have failed, and you see the patient is strong enough to tolerate a cauterization, cauterize a large area on his stomach. As previously mentioned, cauterize the stomach with a circular cauterizing iron.  Apply four cauterizations around the navel with a fine, pointed cauterizing iron.  Apply one or two large cauterizations to the cotton wool above the coccyx.
If you see a large amount of moisture and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the pubis, each hip, and possibly two small additional cauterizations near the large one on the stomach.  This is a successful and effective treatment, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cauterizing hemorrhoids of the anus.
If there are numerous chronic hemorrhoids in the anus, or a single one, composed of thick, cold humors or putrid moisture, and treatment as previously described in the classification was ineffective, cauterize the patient with three triangular cauterizations slightly below the lower lumbar vertebrae under the table.
And cauterize below the navel with a distance of two fingers.  If you judge that the stomach is cold, and food is not digesting, and you see swelling in his face, cauterize the stomach with a large cauterization as previously described, and cauterize the liver. And another one on the spleen with a nail-shaped cautery. Leave the cauterization open for a time, and then treat him until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On cauterizing warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the cautery resembling a needle, then insert it hot into the same cut wart, and press firmly until the cautery reaches the mouth of the vein from which blood flows; do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as described, then treat them with suitable ointments until healed.  If a large cauterization is performed on a cotton pad, it is even more beneficial.
Chapter Thirty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula in the perineum and its surroundings.
If the patient does not respond to the incision and the procedure we described in its place, and avoids it, perhaps he will be cured by cauterization. If someone has a fistula that has lasted a long time, from which pus and corrupt humors flow, the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then ascertain the depth of its penetration with the probe. Then heat the cautery, resembling a needle, and insert it, hot, into the fistula itself, along the line of the fistula's penetration and to the depth the probe reached. Repeat the cauterization until all those corrupt substances are burned, once or twice or three times, according to your needs.
And be careful not to burn a nerve, if one exists, or a large vein, if the fistula opens into the bladder or the intestine.
Be careful of all these areas.  Only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy area and you judge it to be non-penetrating.  Then treat the area until it heals. If the area seals up, the discharges cease, and it remains so for a time, know that it has been completely healed.
And if the discharges do not cease, know that it is penetrating, or there is a corrupt bone in its depth, or something similar, which will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter Thirty-seven.
On cauterizing the kidney.
If pain in the kidney occurs due to cold or thick wind, and the patient's sexual function is thereby reduced, it is necessary to cauterize over the hips, on the kidney itself, cauterizing each kidney with the nail-headed cautery mentioned previously.  And perhaps we cauterize it three times on the same area, resulting in three consecutive cauterizations, which is more beneficial.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
On cauterizing the bladder.
If weakness and relaxation of the bladder occur due to cold and dampness, so that the patient cannot retain urine, cauterize once below the navel on the bladder, where the pubic hair begins, and another cauterization to the right of the navel and another to the left.
After cauterization on each side, the size should be equal to the width of the thumb.  Cauterize once at the lower back, or twice if needed. The cautery should be nail-headed, as previously described.
Chapter Thirty-nine.
On cauterizing the uterus.
If the uterus contains material from cold and dampness, so that conception is prevented, and its structure is impaired, and urination is difficult, or pain occurs during urination, then three cauterizations should be applied around the navel, as mentioned in the cauterization of the bladder. One cauterization on the lumbar region below the back, or two, and the cautery should be nail-headed.
Chapter Forty.
On cauterizing a dislocated hip.
Erroneous humidity may collect on the hip joint, causing dislocation.  A sign is that one leg will be longer than the other when comparing them, and a hollow will be found at the point of dislocation.  Therefore, a roughly circular cauterization should be applied to the affected area on the hip joint itself.
After drawing a circle around the affected area so that the joint itself is in the center of the circle, use the cautery shown in the illustration for stomach cauterizations.  If you do not have this cautery, apply three cauterizations with a large olive cautery.
Make the cauterization as deep as the thickness of the whole skin, then treat it until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Forty-one.
Cauterization of sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint due to cold and dampness, and the treatment described in the section was ineffective after some time, the patient should be relieved from thick humours with rotten seeds or prickly pear seeds, or similar substances.
Then cauterize.  Cauterization has two types: either with burning medications or with fire.  Cauterization with fire can be done in many ways, one of which is to cauterize the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations in this manner.
This creates a beneficial depth. The distance between each cauterization should be equal to the thickness of a finger. The cautery should be olive-shaped. One might also cauterize in the center, on the head of the femur itself, making four cauterizations in total. If desired, you can cauterize with the circular method previously described for hip dislocations, using one cauterization.
So that the circle surrounds the entire hip and burns the thickness of all the skin. This is the method of cauterization for sciatica if the pain is confined to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize two areas on the thigh at the location of the pain indicated by the patient, and one cauterization above the ankle bone about four fingers' width to the lateral side. The cautery should be knife-shaped, and the depth of the cauterization should be just the thickness of the skin.
If he indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize the area he points out using a pinpoint cautery three, four, or more times, if necessary. If he points to pain below the knee towards the leg, cauterize there with a single knife-shaped cautery.
Be careful in all your cauterizations, so that you do not burn a major nerve or artery, thereby causing the patient a serious or protracted injury.
I have observed cases of cauterization above the ankle bone where excessive cauterization resulted in gangrene extending to the foot, causing it to completely ulcerate and decay, leading to the entire leg's deterioration, and subsequent diarrhea and death. If the pain is in both areas, cauterize them in the same manner.
Some wise scholars have described a hip cauterization technique using a device resembling a cup made of iron, with a diameter of half a span. Its rim is about the thickness of a date pit, or slightly less.  Inside this cup are another, and a third cup.
The cups are spaced apart by the width of a thumb's knuckle. All the cups are open on both sides and are about one or two knuckles in height.  A handle of iron is attached to the cups, securely fastened. This is the device's form.
Then, it is heated in the fire until it glows red and emits sparks. It is then placed on the hip joint, the patient resting on the right side. Three circular cauterizations are applied in a single session.  He is then left for three days, bandaged with oil, and the wound left open for several days.
Many such procedures follow, then it is treated with ointment until healed. The author of this book stated that this type of cauterization is rarely used due to its unpleasantness and horrifying appearance, and few can tolerate it.  However, it is a good cauterization for those who can endure it, and are affected in the area.
As for cauterization with burning medicines, it is done by creating two cups similar to the throat that the rope of the door latch goes into, made of copper or iron.
They will have a height of approximately two knuckles, and will be spaced apart by the thickness of a finger.  The bottoms and tops will be open, and they are fastened together in this manner.
Then, they are placed on the hip joint, the patient lying on his right side.  You should gently hold them.  Then, pour some hot, sharp water between the circles. Hold it for a time, like an hourglass. The patient endures the burning and stinging sensation, as it feels like fire.
Until the burning subsides. Then remove the instrument and wash the entire hip with fresh water.  Then leave it for three days, and apply fat to it until the burn's blackness disappears. Leave it for several days until pus comes out, as this is most beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until healed.  If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a mold to contain water, similar to what was done for the hip. This is the description of sharp water, and it has been confirmed in the treatise on the correction of medicines.
Take one part of caustic soda and one part of quicklime. Grind them and place them in a new pot with a single small hole at the bottom, just large enough for a pestle to enter. Place another glazed pot beneath the bottom of the first pot.
Pour fresh water over the caustic soda and lime, enough to cover them with a finger's breadth, after gently pressing them with your hand. Let the pot sit until the sharp water drains into the glazed pot below. Collect all this water, then pour it onto fresh lime and caustic soda.
Then it will be extremely strong in its sharpness, useful in many medical procedures and for cauterizing other parts of the body, because it acts like fire itself.  Some medications can also cauterize the hip, such as safflower, baladi honey, and lime mixed with soap, all ground up.
Galen claimed, recounting a story about an ancient physician, that a treatment for hip and sciatica pain was extremely effective and that it needed no other treatment, claiming it cured in a single application.
Even to the point where a person might be carried into a bath and walk out cured.  To do this, take fresh green chistroge. If no green is available, take fresh dry chistroge. Grind it with some fat and place it on the hip where the pain is, or on the leg or thigh.
Tie it on and leave it for three hours, or as long as the patient feels the burning subside. Then, have them enter a bath. When their body is damp, put them in a basin.  The pain will go away and the person will be healed, God willing. If not healed, repeat the dressing after ten days.
He will be healed, God willing.  And know that no one should use these medications for cauterization until the body has been emptied. Dioscorides mentions that goat's dung, when used for cauterizing sciatica, is beneficial.
The cauterization should be done this way: Take wool, soak it in old oil, and apply it to the deep spot between the thumb and the elbow, closer to the elbow. Then, take dry goat dung.
Set it on fire until it becomes embers, then place it on the wool and let it extinguish. Repeat this process until sensation in the middle of the upper arm extends to the hip, and the pain subsides, God willing. This type of cauterization is called cauterization with dung.
Chapter Forty-Two.
On cauterizing back pain.
Back pain may arise from various causes, such as a fall, a blow, excessive vomiting, and the like. It may be due to the effusion of cold, damp matter.  Cauterization is effective only in this type of case.
After the patient has vomited, a poultice of rotten herbs or the like should be applied.  Cauterize the back where the pain is, in three rows across the table, after marking the area with ink.  In each row, apply five cauterizations, or more, depending on the patient's tolerance and strength.
Cauterize with a point cautery, or if you wish, with a medium-sized nail cautery, three or four times, in this manner.
Chapter Forty-three.
On cauterizing the beginning of a swelling.
This affliction often affects young children.  A sign of its onset in a child is shortness of breath when standing or moving, and you will find a prominent bead, protruding above the other beads, at the end of the spinal vertebrae.
If you see this and wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery, like this. Cauterize each side of the vertebra evenly.  If you wish, cauterize around the vertebra with a pinpoint cautery in two or three rows, with the points close together.  Then treat the area until it is healed, as described.
Beware of using cauterization on a swelling caused by nerve tension.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If joint pains are caused by cold dampness affecting any part of the body, when they occur in the legs, physicians usually call it gout specifically.
If the cold gout, its cause, is treated with the methods we have described in the classification, but the pain does not subside, then cauterization will relieve it.  Cauterize the area around the joints of the legs with many cauteries, using a medium olive-oil cautery in this manner.
And if it is necessary to cauterize on the surface of the leg, do so with a point cautery. If the pain ascends to the knees or other joints, as often happens, cauterize each knee with three or four cauteries on each side using the same olive-oil cautery.
If more cauterization is required, cauterize, but do not make the cauterization too deep, only to the thickness of the skin. If the pain ascends to the hips or back, use the cauterization described in its section.
If the pain is only in the hands, cauterize around the elbows, as if tracing two lines. If the pain persists in the fingers, cauterize each joint and the palm of the hand. If the pain ascends to the forearms or shoulders after a few days,
Cauterize both (forearms/shoulders) from every side.  The patient should not neglect good treatment and taking medications.  If the diet is improved and phlegm is expelled, then recovery will occur with this cauterization, God willing.
Chapter Forty-five.
On the cauterization of hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin, and some intestines and bowels have descended into the testicle, and this has happened recently, then the patient should be instructed to abstain from food that day and use laxatives to evacuate the bowels.
Then, the patient should lie on their back before you, and you should instruct them to hold themselves still until the bowels or intestines protrude.  Then, you should push them back with your finger.  Then, mark the area under the hernia on the pubic bone with ink, making a mark resembling a semicircle, its ends extending towards the upper body.
Then, heat a cautery iron—this is its description.
Until it becomes white, throwing sparks. Then, return the intestines or bowels to their place.  Then, a servant should place their hand over the area to prevent the intestines from protruding.  The legs of the patient have been separated and a cushion placed underneath.  Another servant holds the legs together.
And another servant is on the patient's chest, holding their hands. Then, the cautery iron is placed over the same mark. Your hand, holding the cautery iron, should remain straight and firm until it reaches the bone, or repeat the process if it does not reach the bone on the first attempt.
And exert yourself to prevent the intestines from protruding while cauterizing, so as not to burn them, thereby causing the patient either death or a great illness.  Know that if the cauterization does not reach the bone, your work will not succeed.  The cautery iron should be gentle for children according to their size, and for adults according to their size.
Then, treat the cauterized area with ghee after three days, until the scorching of the fire is gone.  Then treat it with other ointments until healed.  Let the patient lie on their back for forty days until the wound is closed.
His food during his treatment should be such as to soften his bowels, so that the intestines do not protrude during defecation.  Then, if he wishes to stand up after forty days, use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days.  Reduce the strain of eating, drinking, and loud noises.
If he follows this treatment, he will recover completely, God willing.  I will mention the treatment of hernias by incision in its proper place, God willing.  As for a hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is in its early stages, if you want to prevent it from worsening, cauterize a circular area corresponding to its size. Let the burned skin be about one-third of its thickness.  Then treat it as we have mentioned, for it will not worsen, God willing. Some of the earlier scholars have mentioned cauterizing the hernia with a triangular cautery iron in this manner.
After drawing lines with ink, the line across the upper side of the hernia and the other line down to the bottom, then place a single, nail-like cautery in the middle. The first cautery is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter Forty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula.
If, in any limb, a fall or blow causes pain that persists for a long time and is not relieved by medication, it is necessary to cauterize with a point cautery, and the cauterization of each limb should be according to its size, smallness, weakness, and strength.
And if the pain and fistula are overcome by it, then he will recover from the first cautery, otherwise repeat the cauterization, because it is customary for these pains to spread from the limb to those nearby; therefore, it is necessary to follow up with cauterization until the patient recovers.
Chapter Forty-seven.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
As for those afflicted with leprosy, they may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially the type of leprosy that originates from the putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize him, observe. If the leprosy is in its initial stages, and you have treated it as described in the classification, and it has not deteriorated or stopped, and you fear that the corruption will spread to all the patient's humours, cauterize the head five times. One in the usual center of the head, the second below it towards the forehead, at the end of the hair, two on the temples, and one from behind at the nape of the neck.  Apply the cauterization slightly deeper so it affects the bone to a minor degree.
And scales will peel off, making it easier for the thick vapors to escape.  Also cauterize the spleen as mentioned previously.  However, if the leprosy has persisted and is clearly evident on the patient, you should cauterize with the aforementioned methods on the head. One cauterization on the tip of the nose, two on each cheek, two on the vertebrae of the neck, six on the vertebrae of the back, one large one on the coccyx at the base of the spine, and another above it in the same area, and two on each thigh, one on each hip. Two on each knee, one on each knee, two on each shoulder, two on each elbow, two on the ribs of the chest, and cauterize each joint of his fingers and toes, and each heel of his feet and wrist of his hands.
And be careful not to burn the nerve behind the heels, and a cauterization might be applied to the pubic bone, another to the stomach opening, and another to the liver. Know that the more cauterizations are made, the more beneficial and effective it will be.
Know that the patient feels less pain from the cauterization than a healthy person because their body is numb. The size of the cauterizing instrument should vary according to the specific part of the body and the joint, as detailed in the descriptions of the instruments.
Then treat the cauterization with fine crushed dates, honey, and other remedies, until he recovers, God willing.
The Forty-eighth Chapter.
Concerning cauterization for numbness: If a body part is numb and treated with medications, ointments, and dressings, but does not heal, cauterize the same numb part with cauterizations appropriate to the size of the affected body part; make your cauterizations slightly below the thickness of the skin.
Then treat it with the ointment until it heals.  Cauterization can be used for some numbness that affects the hand or foot in the spinal column, at the exit point of the nerve that moves that limb, to eliminate the numbness.
Only those who are knowledgeable about the dissection of body parts and the exit points of the nerves that move the body should attempt this.
The Forty-ninth Chapter.
On cauterizing leprosy.
If leprosy persists and no medical treatment is effective, cauterize it with a small amount of depth, proportionate to the thickness of the skin, so that the whiteness disappears and the color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose oil,  horsetail herb, and pigeon blood or hawk blood, one part of each. Mix all of them, apply it to a cloth, and bandage the area until healed.
The Fiftieth Chapter.
On cauterizing cancer.
If the cancer is in its early stages and you want to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, moving as it revolves. Some physicians have mentioned using a severe cauterization in the center, but I do not consider this, as I anticipate ulceration, and I have seen this happen on several occasions.
So the correct approach is to cauterize around it in a circle, as we have said, or with many small cauterizations.
The Fifty-first Chapter.
On cauterizing the fistula.
If a fistula has occurred and is slow to mature, either due to the causative agent, or due to the patient's age (if they are old and anemic), or due to time, and you wish to accelerate the fistula's maturation, cauterize around it with many small, punctuate cauterizations.
Then leave it, for this will hasten maturation.  If you wish to cover it with cauterization, heat the cautery iron that has this form.
Place it in the center of the fistula, until it penetrates the skin. Let the cauterization be below, so the discharge of pus is easier.  Then treat it with what is appropriate until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-two.
On cauterizing ulcers.
Ulcers are nothing but corruption that ravages a part of the body and consumes it, as fire consumes dry wood.  If you see an ulcer in a place suitable for cauterization by fire, heat a cautery iron with many small and large nails, according to what is appropriate for the location of the ulcer.
Then cauterize it from every side until all corruption is eradicated and nothing of it remains. Then leave it for three days and apply crushed sulfur with oil to the cauterized areas until all the scab and corruption is removed.
Then treat it with healing ointments. If after three days you see that the flesh is growing soundly without corruption, then stop.  Otherwise, repeat the cauterization on any remaining corrupted areas.  Ulcers may also be treated with a sharp medicine.
It acts as a substitute for fire, providing faster relief.  The treatment of ulcers with sharp medicine is mentioned in the divisions; consult there for details when needed.
Chapter Fifty-three.
On the cauterization of inverted and non-inverted nails.
This affliction, a rough and painful condition, often occurs on the soles of the feet.  Cauterization for it can be performed in two ways: either with fire or with a sharp liquid.
As for cauterization with fire, heat a hollowed-out cautery iron, resembling an eagle's beak, made of iron and fitting snugly around the nail on all sides, with a thin rim.  Then apply it hot to the nail, and rotate your hand around the nail, until the cautery reaches the nail's depth.
Leave it for three days, allowing pus to form, then bandage it with pounded wild chamomile mixed with salt.  Leave the bandage on for a night, and the nail will detach from its roots.  Then treat the wound with a flesh-inducing ointment until healed, if the nails are not inverted.
This affliction often occurs on the surface of the body, particularly the hands and feet.  A tube of copper, iron, or eagle's beak should be applied to the nail or wart.  Then, a small amount of sharp liquid should be poured into the tube.
Hold the tube in your hand, rotating it gently, so the tube's rim affects the nail's root, allowing the sharp liquid to penetrate to the nail's root. The patient should endure the sharp liquid's sting for a short while.
Then leave it, for the nail will detach from its roots.  Do this one by one, until all those on the body are removed.  Then treat the areas where they were with flesh-inducing ointments.
Chapter Forty-five.
Concerning the removal of scabs.
If a scab forms from cold in the nerve, or from a fever of the quarter, or something else, it is necessary to cauterize it four or five times on the spine, placing a cauterization between each bead, and one on the chest. And cauterize the abdomen with the olive oil cautery, for the scab will subside and hasten the maturation of the cold illness.
Chapter Fifty-five.
Concerning the cauterization of wounds occurring on the body.
Ugly boils may arise in the body from thick, corrupt, cold materials.  Upon their first appearance, a gentle cauterization should be applied to the head of each boil with a stick whose tip has been heated in fire, or with the root of a long zera'und, or with a lens-shaped cautery.
And boils, in their initial eruption, may be cauterized in this manner.  The causative factor will not increase, but will dissipate, and the patient will recover, provided that this occurs after the patient has been bled.
Chapter Fifty-six.
Concerning the cauterization of bleeding occurring from severed arteries.
Bleeding frequently occurs from a severed artery in a wound from the outside, or in the incision of a tumor, or cauterization of a part of the body, and similar cases, and it is difficult to stop.
If this happens to someone, quickly apply your index finger to the mouth of the artery, pressing gently until the blood is contained beneath your finger and nothing escapes. Then, take several small and large olive-shaped cauteries and heat them in the fire until extremely hot.
Then, take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the location where the artery has ruptured, and apply the cautery to the same vein, after quickly removing your finger, and hold the cautery until the bleeding stops.
If the bleeding starts again when you remove your finger from the artery's opening and the cautery cools, quickly take another cautery from the heated ones and continue doing this, one after another, until the bleeding stops.
Take care not to burn a nerve, lest another affliction result for the patient.  Know that if an artery is bleeding, it cannot be severed, especially if it is large, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization, as we have said, or by amputation if it is not already severed. If it is severed, its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop; or it may be tied with strong threads, or the appropriate drugs to stop the bleeding and tighten the ligatures may be applied.
However, attempting to sever it with a ligature, or tightening with cloth, or applying burning substances, or the like, will not stop it at all, except rarely. If such a thing occurs and no physician or remedy is present, then he should immediately place the index finger over the opening of the wound itself, as we have described.
He should press it firmly until the blood is constricted and flows from above the wound and onto the artery and the finger.  The finger should be continuously kept on it with extremely cold water, until the blood solidifies and thickens, and stops flowing.  During this time, observe what cauterization or medication may be needed.
Chapter Two.
Concerning incisions, lancinations, phlebotomy, and similar surgical procedures.
Khalaf said: We have previously discussed in the first chapter all diseases suitable for cauterization with fire and corrosive remedies, their causes and reasons, and the instruments used.  We have divided that into sections, from the foot to the head.
And I will follow the same approach in this chapter, so that the student can easily attain their goal.
Before beginning this, you children should know that this chapter contains greater uncertainties than the first chapter on cauterization.
For this reason, caution must be greater here, because in this chapter, the practitioner often risks expelling the blood upon which life depends when opening a vein or making an incision on a tumor or an abscess, or treating a wound or removing an arrow or cutting a stone, and similar procedures.  All of these are accompanied by uncertainty and fear, and death often occurs in most of them.
I urge you to avoid any uncertainty in your work.  Many people with various illnesses come to you in this profession.  Some may be weary of their illness and see death as a relief.
Due to the severity of their illness and the prolonged suffering, some show signs of death.  Some give you their wealth to bring about health, and their illness is fatal, so you must not assist anyone who has this sort of condition at all.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and eagerness.  Do not undertake any of these procedures without certain knowledge that you have the best possible outcome.  In all of your treatment of your patients, use the knowledge of the situation to anticipate how the cure might go.
For in this you will gain praise, glory, remembrance, and thanks. May God inspire you, my children, with good judgment and preserve your correctness and success.  For that is in His hand, there is no god but He.
I have arranged this chapter into sections, following the previous chapter on cauterization, from the head to the foot, so that you will find what you are looking for more easily, God willing.
Chapter One.
On treating the water that collects in the heads of children.
This ailment often occurs in infants at birth if the midwife applies pressure to the infant's head without gentleness.  It may also arise from a hidden, unknown cause.
I have not seen this ailment in anyone other than infants.  All those I have seen who had it died quickly.  Therefore, I have seen fit to abandon treatment for it.  I once saw an infant whose head had filled with water and whose head was growing larger every day. Until the infant could no longer sit up due to the enlargement of his head and the increasing moisture, and thus perished.
This moisture may gather between the skin and bone, or beneath the bone upon the peritoneum.  If the moisture is between the skin and bone and the swelling is small, a single horizontal incision should be made in the center of the head, about two spans long, to allow the moisture to drain. This is a depiction of the lancet.
If the moisture is more extensive and the swelling larger, make two intersecting incisions in this manner.
If the moisture is beneath the bone, indicated by the sutures of the head opening on all sides and the water receding inward when pressed with your hand, this is clear.  Then, three incisions should be made in the center of the head in this manner.
After the incision, all the moisture will drain out. Then, the incisions should be bound with strips and bandages.  Then, apply poultices of herbs and oil for five days.  Then, loosen the bandages and treat the wound with ointments and plasters.  Maintain a moderate head dressing.  Feed the patient with all dry foods that are low in moisture.
Until the organ strengthens and heals.  Another approach to incision is to look for where the bone of the swelling and the collection of water are evident, as it may be more prominent at the back of the head, or the front, or the right, or the left.
So aim the incision where the swelling and accumulation of water appear, making an incision as you can, being careful not to sever an artery, causing bleeding that could kill the patient from the bleeding, while draining the moisture.
The Second Chapter.
Severing the two arteries behind the ears, known as the "hissans.
When someone suffers from a severe affliction of the eyes or chest, which persists and is not relieved by medication, the best treatment in such cases is severing these two arteries.
Therefore, if you wish to sever them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to reveal the artery.
