Then, pull the patient's neck taut with the aid of his clothes, then look for where the pulse is and their two locations, the two lower locations behind the ears.  They are rarely hidden, except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, then sever them with a dissecting scalpel, cutting to the bone.  This should be done with the head in a straight position.  If you prefer, you may insert the scalpel under the artery and cut upwards.  The length of the cut should be about two clenched fingers.
If the artery is severed, the blood will flow out in a pulsating manner, surging forward and in a continuous stream. If the artery is not visible, you should estimate the location from the ear, about three fingers' breadth away, then mark it with ink and cut to the bone.  And what is essential...
Approximately six ounces of blood will be released immediately.  It may be more or less, all depending on the patient's strength and the fullness of their arteries.  Then, examine the wound; if any part of the peritoneum remains on the bone, sever it.
So that a hot swelling does not appear, then bandage the wound with a linen thread, and treat it with ointments until healed.
Chapter Three.
The arteries in the temples.
If a person experiences chronic headaches or acute attacks caused by sharp humidity and heat in the muscles of the temples, or chronic, severe headaches, and similar conditions, and if treatment by various medical methods is ineffective, we have tried, in these illnesses, to dissect the arteries of the temples or cauterize them, as we have described.
The procedure for dissecting them is that the patient should shave the hair from the temples, and locate the visible artery in the temple, for its pulsation will make it clear.  It is rarely hidden, except perhaps in some individuals.
Or, if it is difficult to find in severe cold, the patient should tighten his neck with his clothing, then you should rub the area with a cloth, or apply warm water until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the lancet, which has this form. Then, gently slice through the skin with the lancet until you reach the artery, then insert a hook into it, and pull it upwards until you bring it out through the skin, and release it from the membranes underneath on all sides.  If the artery is thin, twist it with the end of the hook. Then cut a piece from it, of a size such that its ends are separated and contracted, and no bleeding occurs.  If it is not severed and not cut, the blood will not recede at all.  Then, drain from six to three ounces of blood.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong doubled thread, whether of silk or bowstrings, so that gangrene does not quickly affect it before the wound heals, thus causing bleeding.
Then, cut away the excess between the ligatures.  Do this at that time or after some other time.  If you wish, cauterize it to the bone with a knife cauterizer until its ends are severed, in which case this action will accomplish the same purpose, or even better, as we said.
However, if the patient has a fever or a heated temperament, cauterization is even more beneficial because it helps to eliminate moisture.  After ligating the arteries, the area should be packed with used cotton and covered with firm dressings.  Following the removal of the dressings, treat with dry medications that promote flesh regeneration and with compresses until it heals, God willing.
If, during your work, bleeding occurs from the artery, promptly cut it, either with cautery or by filling the area with pitch and applying pressure with your hand until the bleeding stops.  If you do not have any of these things available, then apply your finger until the blood clots.
Apply very cold water to the area until the throbbing subsides and it is properly constricted.  Something easier and less involved than ligating the artery is to cauterize the vein with this double-bladed cautery after marking the locations with ink.
Apply it very intensely until it reaches the bone and severs the vein in two places, so that the ends are spaced apart.  This cautery will not cause them to adhere.  This is a picture of the cautery.
The blades are sharp, resembling small daggers, but much less sharp than a knife.  If they were as sharp as a knife, the cold would reach them quickly, and the flesh would not separate quickly.  If there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire will remain in them.
And it quickly cuts the flesh. This method is better than all others, easier and simpler.  Allow the space between the blades to be about the thickness of a finger.
Fourth Chapter.
On treating constant hot watering of the eyes.
If the tears are constant and flow from the veins on the skull's exterior, and if no medicinal treatment helps, and you see the patient's face has reddened... And he feels a crawling sensation in his forehead, like ants crawling, and his eyes are sunken and moist, their edges worn away and their eyelids glued together due to the severity of the tears.  Early practitioners, in their treatment, resorted to this method. They call it the treatment with the sword, which is to command the patient to shave the hair on his forehead.
Then, three parallel incisions are made on the forehead, their length about two fingers each, parallel to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second one slightly off the action of the muscle in the temple, and the third on the opposite side.
And beware of cutting the arteries on either side. Keep your hand away from the jaw joint.  After each incision, leave a space of three fingers' breadth. Have prepared sponges or many dry cloths to absorb the blood.
Then, insert the sharp double-ended scalpel, which has this form. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, use it to remove all the skin and periosteum between the incisions. Then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision, and then remove the scalpel. Then, insert into the first incision another tool, called a single-sided, sharp scalpel, smooth and blunt on the other side, which has this form.
Place the sharp side of the tool upwards towards the flesh attached to the skin, and the blunt side towards the bone. Push it until it reaches the middle incision, cutting all the vessels descending from the head to the eyes, without cutting through the skin.
Then do the same from the middle incision to the other incision.  After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, press the areas of coagulated blood. Then, place a cotton wick in each incision and apply a compress soaked in a mixture of broth, oil, or vinegar and oil.
To prevent a hot swelling. On the third day, the ligaments are released.  Use copious warm water compresses. Then, treat it with basil ointment, rose oil, and other treatments for wounds until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
On treating tears and discharge from the eyes from within the head.
When someone experiences frequent, intense, persistent discharges, and the eyes appear small and weak, with diminished vision, and the eyelids ulcerated and eyelashes falling out. And there is intense, painful pain deep within the head, and repeated sneezing.  Know from these symptoms that these fluids and discharges arise from numerous deep veins.  The best treatment is this: instruct the afflicted to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the center of the forehead.
And there is intense, painful pain deep within the head, and repeated sneezing.  Know from these symptoms that these fluids and discharges arise from numerous deep veins.  The best treatment is this: instruct the afflicted to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the center of the forehead. Or, raise it slightly transversely and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple.  The incision should be into the bone. Keep your hand away from the moving temporal muscles during chewing. Once the bone is exposed and all the blood is dried with a sponge...
Then separate the edges of the incision with worn cotton or flax fibers.  Tie it shut with bandages soaked in medicine and oil, to prevent a hot swelling.  Once you have resolved it, and see that the hot swelling has subsided, you should rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with the desiccated preparation that grows flesh, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of calophon and preparing a poultice from them.  Use it for the growth of flesh in wounds like these.
Chapter Six.
For treating what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic or semi-metallic stone, like iron or glass; or a plant seed, such as chickpeas, kernels, and the like; or a liquid, like water and vinegar, and the like.
Or an animal. If a stone or a stone-like object has fallen into the ear and it does not extend into the ear canal, then expose the ear to the sun. If you see the stone, apply some violet or suruj ointment to it, and then try to remove it by moving the head or inducing sneezing with a cupping device. Close the nostrils when sneezing occurs, after having placed a bandage of cloth or wool around the ear and tilting the ear upwards.  Often the object comes out with this treatment. If it does not, then try to remove it with a gentle swab, whose form is like this.
If it comes out with the swab, otherwise try to remove it with a flexible, slightly curved, blunt needle. If it does not come out with that, then make a copper tube and insert the tube end into the ear canal.
Seal the area around the tube with softened pitch, oiled, so that air has no way to escape except through the tube.  Then pull it out with a strong pull.  Often, it comes out with this.  If it does not come out with what we have described, then take a small amount of mastic or the mastic that is used to catch birds, and place it on the end of the ear speculum.
After wrapping it tightly with cotton, then gently insert it into the ear canal after the ear has dried from moisture. If it does not come out with all the methods described, then proceed to incision before the inflammatory swelling or stiffness occurs.
The method of incision is to first bleed the patient at the temples to a degree proportional to their strength.  Then, place the patient before you, tilting their ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear, near the cartilage, in the lowest part.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone. Then remove it with any available instruments.  Immediately suture the incision and treat it until it heals.
If the object that fell into the ear is one of the seeds that swell and become inflamed, attempt to remove it using the methods mentioned. If it does not come out, then take a slender, gentle forceps, shaped like this.
Attempt to break those types of fallen seeds in the ear using this.  However, only do this if you are certain that the seed has become moistened by the ear’s vapor, so that it can be broken into many small pieces. Then remove them with a blind probe, a gentle swab, or suction.
As we have mentioned, it will be easier to remove it.  Regarding water in the ear, the patient should first use a bellows to induce sneezing.  They should also fill their ear with some used cotton, lying on the ear containing water. If this expels it.
Otherwise, take several smooth, thin stones, warm them slightly by fire. The patient should insert one into the ear canal.  The patient should stand on one leg, on the affected side, and strike another stone against the stone in the ear.
He should continue doing this with stone after stone until all the water is removed.  Water may also be expelled by taking a piece of papyrus or a feather, inserting one end into the ear, and heating the other end by fire until most of it burns, then repeating with another feather.
Repeat this several times until all water is expelled, or pull it out with a tube as previously mentioned regarding the stones.  Regarding removing an animal inside, if it is small like a flea or similar, treat it as described in the classification.  Otherwise...
If its body is large enough to be felt, try removing it with lint and forceps.  This is easier than anything else that can be lodged in the ear. Regarding removing worms that have developed in the ear, if you have treated it as described in the classification in the article on ear-insects and your treatment does not work.
You should look into the ear in the sunlight. If you see anything of the worm, remove it with lint or fine forceps. If you see nothing, take a tube, this is its form.
Narrow at the bottom, wide at the top. Insert the narrow end into the ear as much as the patient can tolerate, then suck strongly with it, repeating this several times until all the worm comes out. If it does not come out, seal around the tube with wax, as I mentioned to you regarding the stone.
If it does not come out with what we have mentioned, use the ear-insects which I have described. The ancients have tested them for killing worms; you will find this in the chapter on ear-insects. You will use this instrument to apply ointments and medications to the ear.  This is its form.
As you see, you make it from silver or brass, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, wide at the top. If you wish, the tube within the instrument can be made of tightly fitted brass, or if you wish, you take a spindle and tightly wrap cotton around one end.
Then you place the ointment, or juice, or whatever medicine you want, in the tube while it is in the ear.  Then you insert the spindle with the cotton from above and press gently with your hand until the ointment is driven into the ear cavity and felt.
Administer it to the patient internally.  Let whatever you pour into the ear be slightly warmed in the fire.  Be careful that the substance you instill is neither too cold nor too hot, for the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
In the treatment of ear obstructions.
Some children may emerge from their mothers' wombs with their ear canals unpierced.
Some people may also experience an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or a growth of flesh within it.  This obstruction may be deep within the ear canal, and thus not apparent to the eye.
Sometimes it presents slightly outside the canal and is visible.  An obstruction deep within the ear, unseen, is usually more difficult to cure.  If it is visible, the patient's ear should be placed in the sun. And examine it.  If you see the obstruction externally, open it with a gentle scalpel, which should appear like this:
Its tip should have a slight, limited projection on one side, while the rest of the scalpel should have smooth sides to avoid injuring the ear.  If the obstruction is from flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a gentle forceps and cut it with great delicacy until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep in the ear, take a smooth, gentle probe, warm it slightly in the fire, and then insert it into the ear canal.  If you feel the obstruction, along with the patient's experience of difficulty in hearing, gently remove it.
Be careful not to injure the eardrum. Then, insert a wick into the ear, sized according to the canal's width, coated in Egyptian ointment.  Do this for several days, to ensure the wound does not heal prematurely.  Alternatively, take a wick, soak it in water, and sprinkle powdered za'farân on it.
Use it. If you see the ear has become swollen and inflamed, you should remove the wick and replace it with another wick coated in qīrūṭī, made with rose oil, to relieve the inflammation. Then continue treatment until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear. Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding as described in the section on cauterization.
Chapter Eight.
On the treatment of warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  They should be grasped with a forceps or hook and cut from their roots with a scalpel. Then, powdered zāg should be applied to the area.
If blood flows from them, cauterize it with a gentle lentil-shaped cauterizing iron.  Cauterization after cutting is better.  It often returns if anything remains of its roots.  If cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and it will not return.
Chapter Nine.
On the treatment of colds affecting the eyelids.
A condition resembling a cold in its severity and hardness often appears on the eyelids.
Therefore, it is called a cold, being a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids.  If the cold is prominent on the surface of the eyelid, moving in all directions, then it is easy to treat.  Make a transverse incision on it.
Then gently peel it from all sides until it detaches. Then suspend it with a hook and cut it. If you cannot cut it without penetrating the eyelid with the cut, that will not harm the patient. If the incision is large, stitch it and treat it until it heals.
If it is small, there is no problem. The ointment will mend and seal it. If the cold is inclined towards the inside of the eyelid, towards the inner surface, turn the eyelid inside out and suspend the cold with a hook without needing an incision, and trim it from all sides.
If the eyelid is penetrated by the cut, that will not harm anything. Then wash the eye after cutting the cold with saline water and treat the area with something that will heal until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
On treating the hordeolum that appears in the eyelid.
The hordeolum is a swelling that occurs in the layers of the upper eyelid.  It most often appears in boys. It burdens their eyes, causing them colds in the pre-dawn hours and they cannot look at the sunlight. Because tears hasten to them.
Therefore, you see them constantly leaning on their faces, and sleeping on them, their eyelids under their eyebrows damp, perhaps swollen.  The hordeolum, the swelling, is clearly visible.  And if you press the area with your fingers, you feel the hordeolum between them.  The procedure is for the patient to place their head on your lap.
Then take a linen rag, prepare a wick from it, and make a circle from that wick large enough to encircle the hordeolum on all sides.  Then place it over the hordeolum and press with your fingers on all sides to gather the hordeolum in the center of the circle.
Then, with a lancet, make a horizontal incision in the middle of that dampness, an incision no larger than that made in phlebotomy.  As for the depth, you must cut through all the skin until you reach the hordeolum.
In most cases, the hordeolum protrudes from the incision as a white piece of fat.  Grab it with a cloth wrapped around your fingers, pulling it outward as you rotate your hand right and left until it heals.  Be careful not to enlarge the incision, lest the lancet injure the eye.
If the hordeolum does not appear at the initial incision, gently enlarge the incision a little until the hordeolum protrudes. Then, pull it out as we said.  Next, soak a cloth in vinegar and water and place it over the area, securing it with a bandage. Some people also crush salt.
Place it [the crushed salt] in the incision to dissolve any remaining moisture. Then treat it until it heals. If a hot abscess develops at the site, treat it with cooling compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the types of eye dressings.
If extra eyelashes grow on the eyelid, outside the natural course beneath the natural eyelashes and become thickened, they harm the eye and cause various ailments, such as constant tearing, eyelid relaxation, whitening, and thickening, even leading to the eye's impairment.
Eye dressings are of four types: either by cauterization with fire, or by a strong medicine as previously discussed in the chapter on cauterization; or the dressing may be by cutting and stitching, or by applying a reed, as I shall describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand.  If it turns, then take a needle with thread from below the eyelid and pierce the needle with the thread from above, near the hair itself.  Pull the thread upward with the eyelid.
Then turn it with a spatula, and make a slit in the inner eyelid, without cutting the extra hair, using a forceps from the greater hook to the smaller hook. Then remove the thread and place a small pad of cotton or cloth beneath the eyelid and bandage the eyelid with a compress.
It should be like the shape of a rue leaf, but the shape should be as great as necessary to raise the eyelid, because it varies among individuals. Some may require a certain amount of the eyelid to be cut, depending on the degree of eyelid relaxation.
Some require a smaller cut, all according to the degree of eyelid relaxation.  Then, slit along the two lines that you marked, starting from the greater hook to the smaller hook. Each slit should be near the natural hair, about the thickness of a spatula.
Then, insert the hook into one of the corners of the skin, and peel it all away.  Then, stitch the edges together with a needle and thin wool thread, wipe away the blood, and secure any remaining threads to the eyebrows with adhesive materials.
You may wish to do this, or not, as you see fit.  The stitches and threads should remain for about three or four days, then treat it. If you prefer, leave the wound un-stitched and treat it with something that dries and contracts, because the eyelid will rise when the wound is sealed and closed.  Stitching is preferable.
This method of lifting the eyelid, mentioned by the ancients, though it may be troublesome for the patient, is a good practice and without danger.  Another method for lifting the eyelid is to form the eyelid into a shape like a rue leaf, as described. Then lift the eyelid with three hooks, either separate or grouped in this manner:
Then cut the excess eyelid with a small pair of scissors, in this manner, making even cuts.
If you cannot hold the hooks in place or if they are uneven, take a needle with thread, insert it into the center of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger opening, and a third near the smaller. Hold the threads between your fingers evenly, then lift your hand in a controlled manner.
Lift the marked skin completely, as described, then suture the edges of the wound and treat it until it heals.  If a hot swelling arises from your cutting or incision, soothe it with a poultice or similar soothing ointment.
The lower eyelid may also have its edges turn inward.  In this case, the cutting, stitching, cauterization with fire and with a sharp medicine, and cauterization with fire or with a sharp medicine is more effective and less harsh on the patient than cutting and stitching.  In either case, use the more appropriate method.
Cauterization with a reed is done in this way: turn the eyelid over and make an incision on the inside, as described, then make two thin reeds or pieces of wood, their length equal to the eyelid's length and their width narrower than a scalpel's width.
Shape them by nibbling at their ends on both sides where the threads adhere, then gently bring them together over the remaining part of the eyelid and tightly tie the reeds on both sides. Leave it for several days; the tightened skin will die, turn black, and decay.
Until it falls off on its own.  If it is slow to fall off, nibble it with nippers and then treat it until healed.  When it heals, the eyelid will rise and the edges will not irritate the eye.  This is the shape and size of the reeds, in length and width:
Chapter Twelve.
For removing irritating hairs from the eye with a needle.
If it is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a thin needle, thread it with a smooth, fine silk thread, tie the ends in a very gentle knot, and make the thread about a span long.
Then thread another fine, shorter thread onto the needle, and knot its ends if desired.  The patient should place their head on your lap, close to the sun so the work is clear, as it is delicate work. Then, using the needle and thread, go into the root of the extra hair.
And carefully remove it through the eyelid, bringing it out above the natural hair.  Then pull the thread upward, out of the needle, and remove the hair(s) - one, two, or three at most - from the needle.
Then gently pull the needle until the hair comes out of the eyelid with the natural hair. If pulling the needle doesn't remove the hair, pull the needle downwards with the thread you've threaded through it, until the needle comes out the top hole.
Then return the hair to the needle, and pull both of them out, repeating until the hair is elevated in the upper hole and visible.  Then, remove the needle and thread, close and bandage the eye. Leave the eye bandaged for two or three days until the hair is securely in place, and the flesh has grown around it.
If the hair is short, either attach a long strand of natural hair to it so it lifts up, or leave it until it grows longer, then lift it up.
Chapter Thirteen.
On treating the sty that occurs on the upper eyelid.
This sty, called "arniya," can be either natural or accidental.  Accidental styes result from an abscess, wound, laceration, or similar burn.
The procedure is to incise the abscess, separate its edges, and insert a linen thread between them, tying it off until it heals.  Avoid using things that dry and constrict.
If you do this, the sty will return worse.  Instead, use softening things like fenugreek, moistened with steeped cumin, linseed, and diachylon ointment mixed with some oil. Apply this to the thread and treat it with this.
The key to its treatment is to prevent it from adhering in its original form by every possible means.  The method described is a partial remedy for the sty, not a restoration of the original condition.
Chapter Fourteen.
On treating styes in the lower eyelid.
This sty, which arises from below, is truly a sty, and is either natural or accidental.  The accidental styes result from a wound, cut, burn, or the like.
The procedure involves taking a needle with a doubled thread, inserting it into the flesh, piercing from the inner to outer canthus until the thread is on both sides of the flesh.  Then, the flesh is pulled upwards with the needle and cut with a broad scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper shape, well and good. Otherwise, take a pledget and place it over the cut. Turn the eyelid over, making two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid, with the ends of the incisions extending from the angles of the cut so they meet to form an angle. So that when they meet, the shape becomes similar to this shape.
Then, remove the flesh, as much as the sharp side of it is below the eyelid.  Then, gather the separated pieces with two stitches, sewing them with a wool thread.  Then, treat it with the relaxing and drawing medications we mentioned, until it heals.
If the ulcer has appeared from a cut, stitching, or cauterization, a small incision should be made, excluding the eyelashes, as described before. Then separate the lips with a drawing tool, as previously mentioned.
In summary, for treating ulcers whether located above or below, conduct the treatment according to the appearance of the ulcer.  It may vary considerably in form.  The skilled craftsman devises the appropriate method.
So that the shape returns to or approximates its natural form. The disease itself indicates the suitable treatment and tools, in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion can affect many people, causing the upper eyelid to adhere to the white of the eye, hindering its movement and natural functions.  A slender probe should be inserted under the eyelid.
Lift it upwards or pull it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a gentle scalpel, not as sharp as other scalpels, but rather somewhat blunt, so as not to disturb the patient while working, lest the scalpel slips and injures the eye with its sharpness. Your cut should be as if you were peeling off the adhesion.
Wait until the eyelid returns to its natural position and the entire adhesion heals.  Then, irrigate the eye with salt water or instill a solution of zinjari (a type of herbal extract) in water from a dropper. Then, separate the eyelid and the eye with a linen thread.
Place moistened egg white on top of the eye. After three days, use the softened poultices until healed.
Chapter Sixteen.
In cutting the nail and deep flesh growths.
The nail is of two types: either nervous, resembling a tough, thin membrane, or non-nervous, resembling a stiff, white moisture that breaks if touched with an iron tool or if one tries to grasp it with tongs.
And a tongs will not grip it. Both types, starting from the largest part, gradually spread until they cover the eye and obstruct the light and movement of the eye.  The method for cutting it is for the patient to place his head in the rock.
Then, he opens his eyes, and you lift the eyelid with your hand. Then, grasp the nail with a slightly curved tongs and pull it upwards.  Then take a needle, thread a horse or cow hair and a strong thread through it, bend the tip of the needle slightly, and pierce the middle of the nail.
Pierce it with the needle and tie the nail with the thread. Pull it upwards and scrape off the side of the nail next to the pupil with the hair, as if spreading it with the hair to its end. Then, cut the remaining part at the root of the growth with a gentle scalpel or small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the growth.
So that a constant flow of tears does not result from cutting it. The nail can be pulled with the tongs alone, or with the thread, as we said, then the smooth side, which is depicted in this way, is scraped off with a scalpel.
And be careful that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, causing a rupture and thus damaging the pupil.  Then, after cutting, apply some powdered salt or zinjari shayaf to the eye, and leave it for another day.  Then treat it as necessary until it heals.
If the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert a needle or secure it with tongs, this type of nail is often treated with medicines, and heals as described in the section. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a gentle, smooth, blunt scalpel, like this picture. And gently scrape the nail from above, if you see it becoming loose and fragmented and only the scraping has been done, then instill from your watch into the eye either zinjari shayaf, or red shayaf, or powdered salt and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the procedure until all of it is gone, except if a hot tumor interferes with the eye, then leave it be and treat the hot tumor until it heals, then resume the scraping procedure until it heals. And as for the protruding flesh...
If the protruding flesh causes severe pain to the eye, then suspend that protrusion with the tongs and cut off a portion, but do not overdo the cutting lest it cause excessive tearing. Then instill into the depths red or zinjari shayaf until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cutting the wording and any excess flesh that grows in the eye.
In some people's eyes, red, layered flesh may grow until it covers the pupil or nearly does, or spills onto the eyelids, and perhaps the eyelids turn outward, resembling a rose.
The procedure is that the patient should place his head in your lap, then open his eyes, and you should remove all of that red flesh with the appropriate forceps.
Or hold it with a scalpel or a dressing, then cut the flesh, piece by piece, until all of it is gone, protecting the eye from being harmed during the procedure.  Your cut can be made either with the scalpel we described in the section on cutting the toenail, or with small scissors. Like the way you pick thorns, as will be shown in the next chapter.
Once the cutting is complete and all the flesh is gone, fill the eye with powdered salt or apply drops of red or similar corrosive medications, and apply a bandage of egg white to the outside of the eye to prevent a hot swelling.
If any flesh remains and bleeding occurs, and you fear a hot swelling, leave the eye and treat it to relieve the swelling, then return to the procedure until healed.  Do the same with any excess flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating any weak eye through nature or by presenting it to the procedure with the iron.  Do not put your hand in anything of these procedures until you see that the organ is suitable for this procedure.

Protect the eye, avoid injuring it with the tips of the scissors.  Let your work be during the middle of the day, in the light of the sun.
Be very diligent in your work so as not to cut anything other than those veins.  When you are finished, instill into the eye green or red stibium to consume any remaining objects.  If you cannot extract all of them at that time, then bandage the eye to relieve its pain.
Leave it for several days until the pain subsides and any inflammation is gone. Then, repeat the procedure in the exact same manner until the eye is healed. This is the manner of scissors to remove the pain from the eye.

Insert the hook on a needle and pull all the strands until they rise above the membrane.  Do not cut the strands, for tears and blood will mix with you.

Chapter Nineteen.
In replacing the nose's splint.
If physicians call the splint a *nasura*, and treatment with cauterization or a strong caustic as previously described has not cured it, then the only recourse is to incise the abscess when it is ripe and remove all the moisture or pus from it.
Until the bone is exposed. If the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, scrape it with an instrument of this shape:
This instrument, called the *khashna al-ras*, is made of Indian iron.  Its head is round like a small seed; it is engraved with the fine engraving of a rasp or scraper.  Place it on the corrupted area of the bone, then rotate it between your fingers while slightly clenching your hand until you know that the corruption has been removed.
Repeat this several times, then treat the area with desiccated, astringent medicines.  If the area heals, flesh grows, the discharge stops, and forty days pass without recurrence, and without suppuration or other events occurring.
Then know that it is healed.  If not, there is no other recourse than to return the *nasura* to the nasal passage in this way: expose the bone again with an instrument or a caustic. If the bone is exposed, take a probe like this:
The tip of the iron instrument is triangular, and its shaft is a tapered cone that tapers to a point, as you see. Place it on the bone itself, near the nasal passage, and slightly away from the eye.  Then, rotate the instrument until the bone is pierced and the patient feels it.
When the patient feels air escaping from the nose when you hold your hand on their nose, then treat the area with astringent, desiccated medicines as I have instructed. When flesh grows and the area hardens, the material which was projecting outward will return to the nose, which will be less burdensome on the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
On returning the protruding eye.
If the entire eye protrudes and there is no impairment or loss of vision, the patient should be made comfortable, then positioned on their back. A cupping glass should be placed on the back of the neck, without any specific conditions, and a gentle suction applied.
Then, apply a compress made from acacia, aloes, frankincense, and henna to the eye.  The eye should be bandaged from above with many strips.  The strips should be made by taking many soft linen cloths, folding each into four layers, and creating many such strips.
Then, place these strips one over the other as needed.  Tighten them firmly with a strong cloth.  Replace the cupping glass and apply suction without limitations.  Remove the cupping glass and keep the eye bandaged for a day and a night.
If the ligament relaxes during this time, it should be tightened again. Then, remove the bandage after one day and one night, as we said.  If you see that the eye has returned to its place, then stop. Otherwise, repeat the bandage, strips, tightening, and cupping until the eye returns to normal.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In cutting the conjunctiva.
If a rupture occurs in the conjunctiva, protruding outside the eyelids as a distinct conjunctiva, and thus disfiguring the appearance, and you wish to cut it, then proceed as described.
To do this, insert a needle into the base of the conjunctiva from below upwards, then insert another needle with a double thread from the side of the tear, and pass it through. Leave the first needle as it is.
Then cut the bend of the double thread, tie some of the conjunctiva around the needle, and pull it tight.  Then remove the needle and place moistened egg white wool on the eye. Leave it until the threads fall out with the conjunctiva. Then treat the eye with something that strengthens it.
Until the wound heals.  The conjunctiva may be pulled from all sides with a circular piece of cloth, to draw the eye outward. Then, with a thin scalpel reaching the back of the eye, drain the white moisture, causing the eye to sink and wither in its position. Then pull it tight until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of the hordeolum.
This affliction, called the hordeolum, is a swelling that collects in the eye, resembling descending water but without it.  The method of treatment is to have the patient sit upright on a chair, then take their head in both hands.
and move it until you see the swelling move downward with your eyes. Then stop, and do not move it. The light will return, and the patient will see things as they used to see them. If the swelling does not descend, then it is likely water. If the swelling does not move down with the method described above.
Otherwise, have the patient sit before you. Then take a thin scalpel and make a small incision on the cornea just where the inflamed tissue meets the cornea at the limbus until the matter is discharged.
Then, after the discharge, instill into the eye warm water, mixed with honey, or water in which you boiled fenugreek and honey.  Then treat by other methods until healed.
Chapter Twenty-Three.

The cup should penetrate to a depth roughly equal to the distance from the pupil to the edge of the iris (limbus).  The copper, due to the clarity of the cornea, will be seen by the observer as though it were in the eye itself. Then the cup should be moved upward to the location of the water.
Then, press it downwards repeatedly. If the water descends immediately, the patient will see what has been opened to his sight immediately, with the cup in his eye, and then he will rest a little. If the water rises, lower it again without removing the cup.

With rose oil and egg white, and bandage the sound eye. As for us and the people of our time, we place crushed cumin with egg white on it. Chapter Twenty-Four.
In treating flesh growing in the nose.
Various superfluous fleshy growths may develop in the nose; some resembling scorpions with many legs, others a cancerous, hardened, dark flesh, and others a soft, non-dark flesh.
If these fleshy growths are soft, not malignant or cancerous, the patient should sit facing the sun, their nostril opened, and a probe inserted into the flesh.  It should then be pulled outward, and the portion grasped severed with a gentle, sharp scalpel, from a single direction.
To ensure all flesh has been removed, if any remains unsevered, gently scrape it away with a delicate instrument so that nothing is left; otherwise, bleeding or a hot swelling may occur.
If the growth is malignant, then promptly cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone.  Then, after the cauterization, apply vinegar and water or a drink to the nose. If the nose opens and moisture flows down into the throat, [the treatment is successful].
Know then that it has healed, if the moisture does not flow properly; then know that there is a fleshy growth on the upper, porous bones, which the instrument did not reach with the cut.  Then, you should take a thick linen thread.
Tie many knots in it, placing each knot a finger's width apart, or less.  The patient should maneuver one end of the thread into their nose with a probe or whatever tool is available, shaping it like a button, and pull it until it reaches the nostrils.
It will come out to the throat.  Children often do similar things in schools; it is an easy procedure for those who wish to do it.  Then bring together the two ends of the thread, one that came out of the mouth and the other that remains in the nose.
Then, use the knots in the thread to separate the flesh.  Do this until you know that the flesh has been separated by the knots in the thread. Then, remove the thread, and the thread now in the nose, after cleaning off the blood, is soaked in Egyptian salve.  Do this for three days or more.
Until the salve consumes all remaining flesh.  Then, the last thing in the nose will be a lead tube for a few days until it heals.  If further drying treatment is needed, use that.
This is an image of the applicator used to drip ointments and medicines into the nose.
It is made of silver or copper, resembling a small, open lamp, with an ointment channel.
Alternatively, you can make the tube closed like a reed, and the applicator's ointment-holding surface flat.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, to hold it when heating ointments or any other fluids or substances in it.
Chapter Twenty-five.
Concerning warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Warts often grow on the tip of the nose, increasing and enlarging over time until they disfigure the appearance.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut it off at its first appearance, removing the entire wart, and then apply either cauterization or a burning medicine, which acts as a cauterant. If it was missed until it grew large, observe whether it is hard, stony, dark-colored, and insensitive.
Do not use iron on it, for it is a cancerous tumor.  I have often witnessed that cutting this tumor has brought a great calamity upon the afflicted person. If the tumor is soft to the touch, not dark-colored, and I see that it can be completely cut away, proceed. Exhaustively remove the diseased portion and cut it without hesitation or delay. Treat the area with what dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-six.
On suturing the nose, lip, and ear. If its connection is separated by a wound or similar, know that if a separation of connection occurs in one of these cartilages, rarely will the treatment succeed, except in some people.
Therefore, if one of these things occurs, examine if the wound is fresh and bloody. If so, bring the edges of the wound together with sutures and treat it until it heals. If the separation has parted its edges and each gap is sound, then one should peel back each gap with the thickness of its visible skin until it bleeds.
Then, bring the edges together with sutures, tightening them, and sprinkle powdered shiyan and luban on them. Place a plaster of palm ointment or another adhesive ointment on top. Leave it tight for two or three days, then remove it.
And change the medicine and leave it until the threads break of themselves, then treat it with ointment until it heals.  The method of suturing is to bring together the separation of connection, either with needles as we described in abdominal suturing, or with thread, as we explained there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On removing the knots that appear on the lips.
Many people may develop small, hard tumors inside their lips, some resembling carbuncles, others smaller or larger.  It is necessary to turn back the lip and cut at each knot, then remove them with forceps.
And cut it from all sides, then fill the area after cutting with powdered alum until the bleeding stops.  Then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the areas with what contracts them until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On cutting excess flesh in the gums.
Excess flesh often grows on the gums, which the ancients called *apols*.  It should be grasped with forceps or a scalpel, cut at its base, and allowed the blood to flow.  Then, powdered alum should be placed on the area.
Or one of the astringent, dried powders. If that flesh returns after treatment, it often does.  Cut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On cleaning teeth with an iron instrument.
Rough, unsightly scales may gather on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums.  These may turn black, yellow, or green, causing decay to reach the gums and making the teeth unsightly.
The patient should sit before you, with their head resting on your lap.  Clean the tooth and the area where the scales and sand-like matter have appeared until nothing remains.  Do the same for the black, green, and yellow discoloration, and so forth, until it is clean.
If the cleaning is successful from the first attempt, then stop. If not, repeat the cleaning the next day, and again on the third day, until the desired goal is achieved.  Know that a tooth requires various instruments of different shapes and forms, depending on the task.
Because the instrument used to clean inside the tooth is different from the one used on the outside, and the one used between the teeth has a different shape.  You should have all these different types of instruments prepared.
Chapter Thirty.
On the extraction of teeth.
One should try every remedy to alleviate the toothache before resorting to extraction, for there is no recovery after extraction, as it is a noble substance, so only extract it as a last resort.
Therefore, if the patient is determined to have the tooth extracted, ensure that the toothache has resolved. Often the patient is deceived by the pain, believing it is in a healthy tooth, and has it extracted, then the pain does not cease until the diseased tooth is extracted.  We have witnessed this repeatedly in the actions of leeches.
If the toothache has subsided, then one should use a strong forceps to detach the gum around the tooth from all sides.  Then gently move the tooth, bit by bit, with your fingers or delicate pliers, until it loosens.
Then firmly grasp the tooth with large forceps, with the patient's head between your knees, making sure they do not move.  Pull the tooth straight to avoid breaking it. If it does not come out, take one of those instruments and gently insert it under the tooth from all sides and repeat the movement as you did before.
If the tooth is hollow or decayed, it is necessary to fill the cavity with a cloth and seal it tightly with a thin, smooth strip, so it does not break apart when you pull on it with the forceps.  And you must carefully detach the gum completely from around the tooth.
Exercise caution to avoid breaking the tooth, as a fragment left behind can cause the patient greater harm than the original pain.  Do not imitate the unskilled leeches, who pull teeth without using the methods we have described.
Often they inflict great harm on people, the least of which is breaking the tooth, leaving the roots whole or partially. Or the tooth may be pulled out along with part of the jawbone, as we have seen repeatedly.  After extraction, the patient should rinse with a drink, vinegar, or salt water.  If bleeding occurs at the site, which is common, immediately apply crushed alum to the area.
Otherwise, if the alum is ineffective, use fine forceps.  The forceps should have long arms and a short, thick handle, so it will not bend when you grasp the tooth.  This is an illustration of the large forceps.
The handles should be thick, so that when you grip them, they don't yield or bend.  The arms should be short.  The forceps should be made of Indian iron or tempered steel. The arms should have teeth that interlock, providing a firm and secure grip.
The arms can also be shaped like a file, providing a strong grip.

Chapter Thirty-Two.
In the eruption of teeth that sprout over others.
Teeth that erupt out of their natural path disfigure the appearance, particularly in women and slaves.  Therefore, one must examine.  If a tooth has erupted behind another tooth,
If it cannot be moved or extracted, remove it. If it is stuck to another tooth, cut it off with this instrument, whose image is this.
It resembles a small beak, made of sharp Indian steel.  Cut it in several days due to the tooth's hardness, so as not to loosen other teeth.  If it is protruding and firmly rooted, cool it with an Indian file, which has this image.
It should be entirely of Indian steel, with extremely delicate carvings, like the file used to make needles.  Cool the tooth little by little over many days, gently, so as not to loosen and cause it to fall. Then smooth it and clean it with various instruments.
If a tooth has broken off, causing pain to the tongue when speaking, then it should also be cooled until the roughness of the break is gone, it is smooth, and no longer harms the tongue or disrupts speech.
Chapter Thirty-three.
For fastening loose teeth with silver or gold thread.
If older teeth become loose and shift due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot eat because of fear of loss, and if binding remedies have been ineffective.
The solution is to tie them with gold or silver thread. Gold is better, as silver tarnishes and rots after a few days, while gold remains unchanged. The thread should be of medium thickness and thinness, appropriate for the space between the teeth.
The method of tying is to take the thread, insert its loop between the two sound teeth, and then weave the ends of the thread between the loose teeth, one or more, until the weaving reaches the sound tooth on the other side.
Then weave back to the starting side, pulling gently and wisely so that it does not move at all.  Pull the thread at the roots of the teeth so it does not slip.  Cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them, twist them with the forceps, and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth.
So that the tongue is not harmed. Then leave it fastened like this.  If it loosens or breaks, fasten it with another thread.  Enjoy it this way for all time.  This is the illustration of the teeth and the method of tying in two healthy teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
A single or two teeth may be repositioned and secured as described, and remain.  A skilled craftsman does this.  He may carve a piece of ox bone to resemble the tooth and place it in the empty socket. And fastens it as we said, so it remains.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cutting the ligament that lies beneath the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament beneath the tongue may be either natural, present at birth, or accidental, resulting from a healed wound.
The procedure involves opening the patient's mouth, placing their head on your lap, and lifting their tongue.  Then, cut the nerve ligament transversely so the tongue is freed.  If there is some stiffness or complexity, and this is from a healed wound, use a probe to pierce and cut it transversely, until the ligament is severed and the knots are loosened.
Beware of cutting too deeply into the flesh and severing an artery, leading to bleeding.  Then, after the cut, rinse with rosewater or vinegar and cold water.  Next, place a linen wick under the tongue, for the patient to hold in place every night.
To prevent re-adhesion.  If bleeding occurs, apply powdered alum to the area. If the bleeding is excessive, cauterize the area with a suitable lens-shaped cautery iron. Then treat him with other remedies until he is healed.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On removing the toad generated under the tongue.
A swelling resembling a small toad may develop under the tongue, hindering the tongue's normal function, and possibly growing to fill the mouth.  The procedure involves opening the patient's mouth facing the sun and examining the swelling.
If you see it dark-colored or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation, do not attempt treatment, for it is cancer.  If it leans toward white and has moisture, use a probe to cut it and gently split it with a scalpel, removing it from all sides.
If blood flows excessively during your work, apply powdered alum until the bleeding stops.  Then resume your procedure until the entire growth is removed. Finally, have the patient gargle with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with the rest of the appropriate treatment until healed.
Chapter Thirty-six.
On the treatment of tonsil tumors.
Any growths that sprout in the throat, including tumors, may present glands inside the throat that resemble those on the outside, called tonsils. If treatment according to the classification does not effect a cure,
If the tumor is hard, dark-colored, and insensitive, do not use the iron to treat it.  If it is red and its origin thick, do not use the iron either, for fear of blood loss.  Instead, leave it until it ripens, either causing it to shrink or to burst on its own.
If it is white, round, and its origin thin, this is what should be cut. Before operating, observe if the inflammation has completely subsided, or if some reduction has taken place.
Then, have the patient sit facing the sun, head in your lap, mouth open.  Take an assistant, and have them press the tongue downwards with an instrument, like this:
Made of silver or copper, thin like a knife.  When the tongue is pressed down and the tumor is clearly visible, take a probe and insert it into the tonsil, pulling it outwards as much as possible without pulling anything from the membranes.
Then, cut it with an instrument, shaped like scissors, but with both ends curved, both edges very sharp.  Made of Indian steel or Damascus steel.
If this instrument is unavailable, cut it with a scalpel, like this:
Sharp on one side, blunt on the other.  After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same way.  Then, after cutting, have the patient gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, have him gargle with water in which pomegranate peels or rue leaves, or similar astringents, have been boiled, until the bleeding stops.  Then treat him until he is healed.  Other tumors may grow in the throat, besides the tonsils, and these are cut in the same way as the tonsils.
I treated a woman with a tumor that had grown inside her throat, resembling a small, hard, almost completely obstructing growth.  The woman was breathing through a narrow passage.  It had prevented her from eating or drinking.
She was on the verge of death if she had lived another day or two. The tumor had two branches that extended to her nostrils.  I acted quickly and inserted a hook into one, pulling out a healthy piece.
Then I cut it where it protruded from the nostril.  I did the same with the other nostril.  Then I opened her mouth and pressed down on her tongue.  Then I inserted the hook into the tumor itself, and cut off a portion of it, with only a small amount of blood flowing.
The woman's throat cleared, and she immediately drank water and ate.  We continued to cut away from that tumor repeatedly for a long time, and the tumor would regrow in place of what we cut away.  This went on for a long time, both for me and for her.
I devised a plan and cauterized the tumor inside her throat, hoping to stop its growth.  Then I left the area. I do not know what happened to her after I departed.
Chapter Thirty-seven.
On the excision of a tumor of the throat.
If a tumor, called 'ʿunaba', descends into the throat and swells, and is white and oblong, it is called a 'ʿamūd'.  If it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called an 'ʿunaba'.  If treatment as described in the classification was ineffective.
If I see that the hot tumor has subsided and is thin, it should be excised.  But if it is a solid, round mass with no length, and is red, or the color of a bruise, or black, or without sensation, then its excision should be avoided.
There is danger for the patient. Therefore, if you see it with the characteristics mentioned, its whiteness and length, the patient should sit facing the sun, and the tongue should be pressed with the instrument whose description was previously given, then the snare should be inserted into the 'ʿunaba'.
It should be pulled downwards and cut with one of the two instruments mentioned for the excision of the tonsils.  Only the portion exceeding the natural extent should be excised, and no more, for if you cut more, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then, after excision, use what we described for the excision of the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient is hesitant about excision, then use a method of cauterizing it without fear or caution. The method of cauterization is with a sharp medicine, placing the patient's head on your lap.
Then press the tongue with the instrument we mentioned, then take the sharp water mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, mix it with quicklime, neither thick nor thin, and fill the hollow of this instrument. This is its image:.
The end of the instrument where you place the medicine should be concave like the bowl of a wooden spoon.  Place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself, and the patient should lie on his side, so that saliva flows from his mouth from inside the throat, lest something should descend from it into his throat and harm him.
Then, hold the medicine in your hand and press it onto the uvula for about half an hour until you see it has blackened and the burning of the medicine has subsided. If you prefer, take a piece of cotton, wrap it around the end of a wooden spoon, and soak the cotton in the medicine.
Insert the wooden spoon with the cotton into the tube from above so that the cotton adheres to the uvula.  Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired cauterization of the uvula. Then leave it; it will wither and fall off in three or four days. If you need to repeat the application of the medicine, do so.
After cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in ghee, and remove any remaining medicine from the surrounding area. Then, rinse with cold water and treat externally with poultices and internally with gargles until healed. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and further from fear.
The uvula may also be treated with something gentler than cauterization or cutting, using incense in this manner:  Take frankincense, zoffa, sage, sisymbrium, shih, chamomile, and qaysum, and similar herbs.  Gather them all or some of them in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
And boil, with the pot sealed with clay of wisdom, and a hole in the lid to fit the hollow instrument, made of silver or copper:
Insert the end with the pomegranate into the patient's mouth so that the steam rises to the uvula through the tube, causing the uvula to contract.  Repeat this several times until it withers.  Be careful not to perform this treatment in the initial stage of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling.  Instead, one should do this when the inflammatory swelling has subsided.  If this instrument is unavailable, take a reed and attach a hard-boiled egg shell to its end so that the patient's mouth is not burned.
Because the egg shell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth, and this is a good and safe treatment.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
On the removal of thorns and other foreign objects lodged in the throat.
A bone, fish thorn, or other foreign object often becomes lodged in the throat.  One should remove anything visible with the eye, after depressing the tongue with an instrument in the direction of the sun.
To make clear what is in the throat, and if something is hidden and not visible in the throat, then the patient should vomit before his food is digested in his stomach.  Perhaps the lodged object will come out with vomiting, or the patient might swallow a piece of lint or a piece of lettuce stalk.
Or he may swallow a piece of dry bread, or take a piece of soft sea sponge, tie it to a thread, and then swallow it. When it reaches the position of the thorn, quickly pull the thread repeatedly.
Frequently, the thorn or bone will adhere to it and come out. If it does not come out by the methods mentioned, use a lead instrument in this way:
It should be slightly thicker than a lead rod, and at its tip there should be a bend. The patient, with their head raised, should gently insert it into their throat, being careful not to touch their throat, lest it cause coughing, and thus dislodge the bone or thorn.  Alternatively, the physician can insert it with their hand.
Having the patient insert it is better, as they know the location of the lodged object.  They push it downwards or pull upward with the instrument, according to what is possible, until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-nine.
On removing leeches lodged in the throat.
If the leech, using the medication mentioned in the classification, proves ineffective, then examine the patient's throat in the sunlight, after having pressed the tongue with the instrument I described.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a gentle, firm forceps.  If this is not successful, take a hollow tube, insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, and then insert a heated iron into the tube.
Do this several times. The patient should abstain from drinking water all day.  Then, he should take a bowl filled with cool water, open his mouth over it, gargle, and not swallow a drop.  He should move the water with his hand occasionally.
If the leech feels the water, it will fall off. If it does not come out by the method described, fumigate the throat with urine and hellebore using the instrument described for the fumigation of the larynx.  Do this several times; it will fall off.
The method for fumigation is to take a vessel containing embers heated by fire.  The vessel is covered with a lid having a hole in the center.  Place the end of the instrument into this hole.  Place the medicine for fumigation into the vessel and have the patient place his mouth on the end of the tube and close his mouth to ensure the fumes reach his throat.
If the leech falls off at this stage, if not, the fumigation is repeated several times. The patient endures thirst, eats salty food and garlic, and does not drink water.  The leech must come out with this procedure.  This is the form of the instrument to extract the leech from the throat, when seen.
It resembles pincers, as you see, except that it has this curve that goes into the throat.  Its tips are like a bird's beak; there is the roughness of a file on them, so if they grasp something, they do not let go.
Another instrument is used to restrain the tongue. It is a wide ring of iron, sized to fit the mouth over the tongue.  It is inserted into the mouth, standing on its side, keeping the mouth open against the patient's will.  The patient cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
Description of the instrument used to open the mouth in this exact manner.
Chapter Forty.
In it is the discussion of the lancing and incision of tumors.
Tumors are of many varied types, as will be described individually in this book.  Their lancing and incision differ in two ways: one is the type of tumor itself and the humors it contains.
And the second type is according to the locations in the body where they occur, because a tumor occurring in the head is different from a tumor occurring in the buttocks, and a tumor occurring in a fleshy area is different from a tumor occurring in a joint. Each has its own method of treatment.
Some tumors should not be lanced until the pus has matured and is complete; others should be lanced while still immature, such as tumors close to joints.
Because if a tumor occurs near a joint and grows to the point of causing decay around it, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing the impairment of that limb, or the tumor may be near a vital organ.
Because if you delay lancing it until it matures, you will harm the vital organ, or if it is near the buttocks, lance it while still immature.  It is necessary to lance a tumor that is immature and near the buttocks so that the pus does not spread and penetrate the buttocks, causing a fistula.
It will become incurable to some degree.  You should know when to lance tumors that have fully matured; this is when the tumor's pain subsides, the fever disappears, redness and swelling lessen, the head of the tumor softens, and other such signs appear.
The incision should be made at the lowest part of the tumor, if possible, to allow the discharge to drain downwards, or at the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor.  And the incision should run lengthwise if the tumors are on the arms or legs.
And the locations of the muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries—and generally, all flat, unyielding areas—should be considered.  For areas that bend, the incision should follow the shape of the area.  If the tumor is in a fleshy area, it is best to leave it alone.
Until its maturity is complete, as we have stated. If you lance it before that, the pus will drain excessively, causing considerable harm and uncleanliness; and sometimes its edges and interior will harden. Some tumors may be incised across the body if necessary or in a way that suits the affected limb.
For small tumors, a single incision should be used. For large tumors, a wide incision or several incisions, depending on the size of the tumor, may be needed.  Some tumors may require the skin to be incised or cut, if they have become like a rag.
Some have reached a state approaching death, as often seen in many abscesses and hidden tumors. Some are incised in a three-angled manner, while others are cut like a rue leaf, like the tumor of the groin.
Some require a round or crescent-shaped incision, or similar incisions.  If the tumor has no head, like a smooth, flat tumor, a simple incision should be made. If the tumor is large and has developed for a long time, and you have lanced it, do not rush to drain all the pus at once.
Instead, drain some of it, and then compress the tumor for another day, and then drain some more pus. Repeat this gradually until all the pus is removed.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an elderly person, the animal spirit often weakens with a sudden discharge of pus.  The patient might die without you realizing it.  Beware of this point greatly.
After lancing these tumors, you should clean the wound and inspect it. If the tumor wound is small or the incision simple, use a bandage of linen or worn cotton. If the tumor is large and the incisions numerous,
You should insert a thread into each incision until they reach one another. If part of the skin is torn or raised, you should fill it with worn cotton or linen strips, without moisture, and compress it for three days.
Then remove it and treat it with appropriate ointments until healed. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar, after soaking a linen cloth in them, and apply it to the bleeding site repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the methods and treatments, and cauterization, described in many places in this book and in the sections. If it is winter and the tumor area is highly sensitive, you should wet the bandages with a warm drink and oil.
And place them on the area. If it is summer and the areas are fleshy, you should apply bandages soaked in water and oil, or in a cool drink and oil.  Once the third day has passed, as we said, you should dissolve the swelling and clean it.
And use similar treatments until it heals. This is what you need to know about treating tumors in general.  As for detailed methods, each tumor's treatment has been described in a summarized and categorized manner.
Chapter Forty-one.
On the treatment of tumors that appear on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp, a type of swelling contained within pouches, resembling a hen's egg. Their types are numerous, some fatty, others containing moisture like mud.
Some contain moisture like porridge and soup, and similar things. Others are stony and hard.  There is no danger in cutting them open and removing them, unless a blood vessel is encountered during the incision.
The procedure for opening them is to first probe them with the instrument, which will be illustrated later, called a *midas*, to know what they contain. If they contain moisture, cut them lengthwise with a simple incision, like this:
Begin the incision from line ~b~ to line ~c~.  If the moisture is discharged, remove and cut away the entire sac that contained the moisture. Do not leave any part of it, for it often returns if any remains.
Then dip a cotton swab into the *sur-i* ointment, if available, or else into salt water, and fill the wound with it. Leave it until the next day, for it will consume any remaining portion of the sac.  Then, repeat the application with the *sur-i* ointment, once or twice more, if necessary.
Until you are certain that nothing of the sac remains. Then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the swelling contains fatty matter, cut it in this way:
Make a cross-shaped incision, and place forceps on the edges of the wound. Remove it from all sides.  Strive to remove the peritoneum that contains it. If an artery is encountered, do what was previously described for it.  Do the same for the swelling if it is hardened from the incision.
The treatment is the same as previously mentioned.  The incision on a hardened swelling is easier because it has less blood and moisture. Some of these swellings in the head in some people contain no moisture at all. I incised a swelling in...
the head of an elderly woman. I found the swelling like hard, rough, white stone. It could not be broken, even if someone threw it to shatter it.  The other swellings that occur on the heads of children are not like this.
At the bases of the ears, make a simple incision across the entire area. Make the bottom part thinner to allow the material to drain downwards. Then treat it with appropriate remedies.
Chapter Forty-two.
Concerning the incision on swine that often appear on the neck.  These swellings frequently occur on the neck, under the armpits, and in the groin.  They may also appear elsewhere on the body.  Sometimes only one appears on the neck, sometimes many, and some arise from others.
Each swelling has its own enclosed sac, as do the swellings in the merchandise and on the head, as we described.  These swellings are of various types: some are stony, some contain moisture, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
Of those that I examined, some were well-disposed to touch, their surface appearing close to the color of skin. They moved in all directions, were not attached to the neck nerves, veins, or arteries, and were not deeply embedded. Then it is necessary to incise it with a simple incision from top to bottom of the body, along the line ~J~ to line ~B~.
Remove it from all sides, and stretch the skin's edges with a hook or two hooks, or many hooks if necessary, as we said concerning swellings of the head.  Withdraw it bit by bit, ensuring the neck is not cut by any vein or nerve.  Let the scalpel not be excessively sharp.
Lest your hand cause further cutting, or distress the patient, leading you to cut what is not necessary. If you cut a vein or artery, and that prevents your work, then place powdered styptic or some of the substances that stop bleeding in the wound.
Close the wound and leave it until the swelling subsides, the wound relaxes, and putrefaction begins.  The bleeding will stop at that point. Return to your work until finished.  Then, check with your index finger if there are any remaining small pieces, and if so, cut and remove them.
If a large vein is found at the root of the piece, it should not be cut from its base, but should be tied with a strong doubled thread, and left until it falls out on its own without harm.  Then fill the wound with old cotton, which has been dipped in Egyptian ointment, and treat it.
If the entire piece is cut, the edges of the wound should be brought together and stitched immediately, after confirming that there are no remnants left. However, if you see a remnant of the piece of flesh, do not stitch.
You should aim your cut at the base and root of the piece, using the suturing and what we have mentioned. If the pieces contain moisture, apply a light dressing where you see the area of ripeness. Make the dressing below, as we said.
Then, after the dressing, use a poultice with Egyptian ointment, or similar, to consume any remaining corruption.  Once you see the wound is clean, treat it with healing ointments until it heals.
Chapter Forty-three.
On slitting the throat for a tumor occurring inside the throat.
The ancients mentioned this slitting of the throat, but I have not seen anyone in our country perform it.  This is in accordance with their statements.  However, those with quinsy should avoid slitting the throat, as they will not benefit from it.
In order that all tumors and the lung be diseased, and those with a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no problem with the windpipe, the slitting of the throat is necessary to escape the harm caused by suffocation.
Therefore, if we wish to do this, the throat should be slit under three circles of the windpipe or four small slits transversely between two circles, as much as the slit would be in the diaphragm, not in the cartilage. This place is appropriate for the slit.
Because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are distant, if the surgeon is cowardly, he should extend the throat's skin with a hook, then slit the skin until, when he reaches the windpipe, he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he slits the diaphragm that we have described, and evidence for slitting the windpipe is found in the phlegm that comes out of it with what is punctured, and from the cessation of the voice.  The wound should be left open for a time.  When the time of fearing suffocation has passed, the wound's edges are brought together with skin.
Then he sews it alone, excluding the cartilage.  Afterwards, the medicines that promote flesh growth are used until healing occurs.  The author of this book says, "The explanation of this passage we have narrated is that it is applied when the patient's throat is obstructed by one of these tumors that we have mentioned.
When the patient is close to death and fears to die, they proceed to slit the throat, so that the patient breathes through the wound, and is saved from death.  They are instructed to leave the wound open until the disease's course is completed, and its duration is three days or similar.
At that point, they were instructed to sew up the wound and treat it until healed.  What I personally observed was that a servant took a knife and slashed her throat, cutting some of the lung's windpipe.  I was summoned to treat her, and I found her weakening like a slaughtered animal.  I uncovered the wound.
I found that the blood coming from the wound was slight, so I was certain that she had not cut an artery or vein.  Air was escaping from the wound.  I quickly sewed up the wound and treated it until she was healed.  The servant suffered nothing except a slight hoarseness of voice; nothing more.
After a few days, she returned to her best condition.  Therefore, we say that slitting the throat entails no danger.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On the incision of the tumor that appears in the larynx from the outside, and is called a tumor of the larynx.
This tumor, called a tumor of the larynx, is a large tumor the color of the body.  It is common in women and comes in two types.
Either it is natural or incidental. The natural one has no remedy, while the incidental one comes in two types; one similar to fatty tumors, and the other similar to a tumor arising from arterial complications, and incising it is dangerous. Therefore, one should not use iron on it at all, unless it is small. If you probe and examine it with a probe, and find it resembles a fatty tumor and is not connected to any vessels, then incise it as you would a tumor. And remove it with whatever is inside the sac if it is in a sac, otherwise remove everything completely, then treat the area with the appropriate treatment.
Chapter forty-five.
On the incision of various tumors.
Tumors are of many types, and all their types are mentioned in the classification. Here we must explain the difference between a tumor and an abscess, as it is problematic.  I say that an abscess is accompanied by heat, fever, and fearful pains until the boiling of the matter subsides. And when the pus matures, the fever and severity subside. A tumor is not accompanied by heat, fever, or pain. It is contained within a serous sac, its own special container, and is the same color as the surrounding body.  It begins like a boil and becomes like a wheal, larger or smaller.
And there are two types: either fatty or containing moisture. The colors of the moisture are numerous, as mentioned in the classification. Therefore, when you set about treating a tumor, you must first probe and examine it with the instrument called a probe, as its image will be shown in the chapter following this one.
Assess the size of the swelling, then withdraw the probe and observe what follows. If fluid of any color emerges, make a simple incision, as described in the treatment of other tumors. If no fluid follows the probe, know that it is fatty.
Incise it with a firm incision in this manner, as I have taught you.  Suspend it with forceps and carefully dissect the skin from all sides, and if possible, remove the sac containing the tumor intact.
If the sac breaks during the procedure and you cannot remove it intact, which often happens, remove it piece by piece so that nothing of it remains, for if even a small portion remains, the tumor will likely recur.
If you are unsuccessful and a small portion remains, cauterize the wound with sharp cauterizing agents when you are finished, then compress the wound and apply a remedy to soothe the inflammation. Treat it with other methods until it heals. If the tumor is large, suture its edges and treat it with procedures for healing wounds.
If a bleeding vein, either pulsating or not, arises, immediately cauterize the affected area with powdered alum and leave it compressed for two or three days until the wound suppurates and the bleeding subsides.  Then proceed to remove any remaining part of the tumor.
