Chapter forty-seven.
In the treatment of the male breast.
The breast of some people may swell at the age of puberty until it resembles a woman's breast, remaining swollen and unsightly.  If one dislikes this, one should make an incision in the breast, a crescent-shaped incision, like this shape.
From line ~b~ to line ~c~. Then remove all the fat, then fill the wound with the medicated ointment, then bring the edges of the wound together with stitches and treat it until it heals. If the breast droops and relaxes due to the bone, as happens with women.
One should make incisions in its upper sides, two incisions resembling a crescent shape, each connected to the other at their ends, so that the larger line is encircling the smaller one, in this manner. From line ~b~ to line ~c~, then the skin between the incisions is peeled off, the fat is removed, and the mentioned sutures, stitches, and necessary treatment are used until it heals.
If the incision does not encompass what needs to be cut due to the patient's distress or bleeding, then the wound should be packed with cotton soaked in the corrosive ointment and left to consume any remaining fat, then treated until healed.
Chapter Forty-Eight.
On the treatment of tumors appearing under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are of the pig-like, solid, sac-containing type.  Some contain moisture.  Those containing moisture should be incised with a crescent-shaped cut, like this.
From line ~b~ to line ~c~.  If all the moisture in the tumor is expelled, pack it with old cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat it with the appropriate ointment until healed.  If the tumor is the knotted pig-like type, incise it as we described for pig-like tumors; if the wound extends so as to discharge pus, then use cautery, as we previously described.
Chapter Forty-nine.
Incising the tumor that appears beside the artery or vein, and is called an aneurysm.
If the artery is wounded and the skin above it adheres, a tumor often results.  Similarly, a vein may also display swelling and a tumor.
The signs by which one knows whether the swelling and puffiness are from an artery or a vein: if from an artery, the swelling is oblong, compressed into the depth of the body, and when you press the tumor with your finger, you feel a crackling sensation.
That which is from the vein, the swelling is round on the surface of the body.  Incision on these tumors is dangerous, especially in the armpit, groin, neck, and many other parts of the body, and if they are very large, one should avoid treating them with iron.
And those also that are in the limbs or the head should be avoided.  For any swelling from the mouth of the artery, make a lengthwise incision in the skin, then open the incision with hooks, and then dissect the artery and free it from the membranes.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle underneath it and pierce it to the other side.  Tie the artery with a doubled thread in two places, as I described to you in the ligation of the arteries in the temples.  Then pierce with the lancet the spot between the ligaments until all the blood within it is expelled.
The swelling will subside, then use the treatment that produces pus until the ligaments fall off, then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the swelling is from a ruptured vein, you should grasp as much of the swelling with the skin as possible.  Then insert a needle below the place you grasped with your hand, and pierce it, with a doubled thread in it, until you bring it out from the other side. Then tie the swelling firmly, according to what I told you about tying the hernia at both ends of the thread.  If you fear that the threads will slip, insert another needle with another thread under the entire swelling at the intersection of the first needle.
Tie your threads in four places, then slit the swelling in the middle until what is inside comes out. Cut off the excess skin and leave what was tied. Then place a compress soaked in a drink and oil on it. Then use treatment with twisting and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
In the swelling that arises from nerve twisting.
Just as swelling occurs in arteries and veins, swelling also occurs in nerves if there is a blow or excessive fatigue or the like.  This is more common in the wrists and ankles.
And in every location where joints move, it is a hard swelling resembling the color of the rest of the body.  It is mostly without pain, and if pressed hard, the patient feels a sensation like numbness. The swelling is not deeply embedded in the body.
Rather, it is under the skin and moves in every direction, but does not go forward or backward.  Whatever is in the joints should not be treated with iron, as it may cause a festering wound.  And whatever is in the head or forehead...
Cut the skin with a scalpel. If the swelling is small, grasp it with a forceps and cut it from the root. If it is large, suspend it with a hook and strip it, then remove it. Close the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-one.
On the cutting of warts that appear on the abdomen.
Warts, called fungous because of their resemblance to mushrooms, frequently appear on the abdomens and other parts of the body of some people. Their base is thin and their head thick; sometimes their lips are fused. Some are small, others very large.
I once observed a man with two warts on his abdomen, resembling mushrooms in appearance. Their thin, whitish bases had fused, and their lips were cracked, constantly oozing moisture. I cut them off, and their weights were approximately eighteen ounces and six ounces respectively.
When cutting them, if the affected area is moist and the wart's color is white, moist, and thin-based, cut it with a broad scalpel.  Have a cautery iron ready, as considerable bleeding often occurs during the cutting process.
If bleeding is profuse, cauterize the area. If the affected area is sensitive and reacts to the iron cutting, take a strong lead thread and tightly bind the wart. Leave it for two days, then increase the tightening of the lead thread, continually tightening it.
Continue tightening until the wart breaks off and falls away on its own, without any difficulty.  If the wart has a thick base, there is some risk in cutting it, especially if it is on the abdomen, but it may be possible to cut off half or part of it.
Then cauterize it to prevent recurrence, and treat the wound until it heals. Beware of cutting a wart that is dark in color, insensitive, and dull in appearance, for it is a cancerous tumor.  We will discuss cancer later.
Chapter Fifty-two.
On treating umbilical growths.
Umbilical growths may arise from many causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum on the abdomen, from which pus or intestines protrude, as occurs in other hernias; or from blood issuing from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or from accumulated gas.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and the discharge of pus, the tumor will be similar in color to the body, soft and painless, and its position will be irregular. If it is due to the protrusion of intestines, its position will be even more irregular than what we have described; when you press it with your fingers, it will disappear, then reappear; perhaps there will be rumbling accompanying it; and it will increase greatly in size during bathing and severe exertion.  If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
If it does not disappear when pressed with your hand, and does not increase or decrease, then if it is due to blood, the swelling will, along with these signs, appear dark. If it is due to a growth of flesh, the swelling will be hard, solid, and unchanging in size.
If it is due to gas, its touch will be soft.  The treatment in this case is to observe.  If the umbilical protrusion is caused by blood from an artery or vein, or by gas, then it is advisable to refrain from treatment, as this carries risk and uncertainty, as I informed you in the chapter where I discussed tumors caused by arteries and veins.
If the umbilical protrusion is caused by the intestines or pus, you should instruct the patient to hold themselves still and stand upright, stretched out, then apply a bandage around the entire navel area. Then, instruct him to lie on his back before you.  Then, make a wide incision around the navel, in the area you marked with the bandage.  Then, pull the center of the swelling upwards with a large hook.  Finally, tie the incision site with strong thread or silk cord, firmly.
The knot of the bandage should be a running knot.  Then, open the center of the swollen area, stretched above the bandage, and insert your index finger.  Search for the intestine.  If you find it has been caught by the bandage, loosen the running knot and push the intestine back into the abdomen.  If you find pus, pull it out with a hook.
And cut off any excess.  If an artery or vein obstructs you, tie it off.  Then return to your work. Take two needles, and thread strong threads into them. Insert the two needles into the incision you made around the swelling, making sure they pass completely through.  Then, pull the swelling tight at four points on the needles.
If you prefer, remove the needles and leave the area until the pulled flesh becomes putrid and falls off on its own, or cut it off if it becomes thin and putrid.  Then treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals.  If the umbilical protrusion is from flesh that has grown there.
Or if it is from moisture, then it is necessary to cauterize the swelling, as we said, and remove the blood or moisture you find there.  Then treat it with what will heal the area.
Chapter Fifty-three.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have mentioned in the classification the types of cancer and how to treat it with medications, and warned against treating it with iron so that it does not ulcerate.  We also mentioned cancer originating in the uterus, and warned against its treatment.
The ancients stated that if the cancer is in a location where it can be completely excised, such as cancer in the breast or thigh, and similar removable organs, especially if it is small and nascent, then it should be completely removed. However, if it is advanced and large, then one should not approach it.
I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve this.  If it is located in a removable area, as we have said, one should proceed, easing the patient's condition with black salve several times, and then cauterize it if there is a clear accumulation of blood vessels.
Then the patient should be placed in a position that facilitates the procedure, then appropriate hooks should be inserted into the cancer, and it should be cauterized from all sides with the skin, thoroughly, until nothing of its roots remains. Allow the blood to flow, do not cut it quickly.
Instead, squeeze the affected areas and remove all thick blood with your hand or any available instruments. If the procedure causes a large bleeding from a severed artery or vein, cauterize the affected area until the bleeding stops, then continue with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Forty-five.
On the treatment of scabies.
We have previously described the types of dropsy, how water accumulates, the signs of each type, and its treatment with medications.  Only the "zāqī" type is treated with iron; the "ṭabīʿī" and "lahmī" types should never be treated with iron.
If you have treated this "zāqī" type of scabies with medication and it has not been effective, then examine if the patient has extreme weakness or another illness besides the scabies, such as...
If there is coughing, diarrhea, or similar conditions, avoid treating it with iron, for that is dangerous.  If you see a strong patient, with no illness other than scabies, who is neither a child nor an elderly person, then act by having the patient stand before you. and a servant behind him will squeeze his abdomen with his hands, pushing the water downwards towards the pubic region. Then, take a pointed scalpel in this manner:
narrow at both ends, long, and pointed like a scalpel, but with a slight curvature to prevent it from entering the intestines during use and harming them.  Then, observe if the scabies originated from the intestines.
Then, the incision should be three fingerbreadths below the navel, aligned with the pubic region.  If the scabies originated from liver disease, the incision should be three fingerbreadths to the left of the navel.  If it originated from spleen disease, the incision should be three fingerbreadths to the right.
Furthermore, the incision should not be on the side the patient intends to lie on, to prevent fluids from flowing to that weak area.  Then, pierce the skin with the instrument all the way through, then insert the instrument into the incision and lift your hand.
With the scalpel, separate the skin from the peritoneum as if you were peeling it.  The amount you peel should be roughly the size of a fingernail.  Then, pierce the peritoneum until the scalpel reaches the empty space where the water is located.  Then, remove the water and insert the instrument, which has this shape, into the hole.
It is like a thin tube made of silver, brass, or smooth polished leather.  It has a small hole at the bottom and three holes on the sides, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
Its tip may have a small, pen-like probe, like this image.
At its top, there is a ring.  If the instrument reaches the water, it will immediately drain from the instrument. Drain the water in a moderate amount, for if you drain more than necessary at once, the patient may die due to the dissolution of their vital spirit.
Or he may suffer an almost fatal faint.  However, drain it according to his strength and what his pulse and complexion indicate.  Then, remove the instrument and contain the water, for it will stop flowing immediately.
Due to the skin obstructing the opening in the peritoneum, which you were told to smooth in that manner, then use the instrument again the next day if the patient seems capable of tolerating it.
Remove also only a small amount of water.  Do this for several days, remaining vigilant and avoiding mistakes, until only a little water remains.  If you fear for the patient and leave much water, treat him with burial in hot sand.
Excessive sweating in the bath and sun, enduring thirst, and treating him with dried remedies until he recovers.  Cauterize the stomach, liver, and spleen after removing the water, as described previously.

Chapter Fifty-six.
Concerning the sores that appear on the foreskin, shaft, and dark discoloration, decay, and adhesion of the foreskin to the shaft.
This sore often appears on the urethra, a fleshy, dark protrusion, and can be malignant or non-malignant.  The non-malignant one should be caught with a delicate hook.
Cut it completely until it is cleansed, then apply a cotton swab dipped in Egyptian ointment, then treat it with palm ointment until it heals.  If the sore is malignant and dark-colored, cauterization should be used after cutting and removing the affected area.

This adhesion occurs in those whose foreskin was healthy and did not require circumcision. Adhesion can also occur from a wound or a tumor.  The foreskin should be peeled back with a sharp scalpel until the ligament is loosened and the glans is separated from all sides.
If it is difficult to separate them by careful examination, then a small part of the glans and foreskin should be peeled back.  The foreskin is thin, so it may easily tear due to its thinness. Then, separate the foreskin and glans with a thin, damp linen cloth that has been soaked in cool water, so that they do not stick together again.
Then treat with a styptic drink until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-seven.
On the purification of boys and treatment of errors affecting them.
Circumcision is nothing more than separating a connection, like other surgical procedures. However, since it is an act of our own will and is particularly used on boys, we must establish the best procedure for it.
The easiest path to safety, I say, is that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, because it was not practiced in their laws; rather, it is something we have adopted.
Through experience, I found that the majority of artisans and barbers use purification with a scalpel and scissors, and use forceps and ligatures with thread and cutting with a knife. I have tried all these methods.
I found nothing better than purification with scissors and tying with thread, because purification with a scalpel often causes the skin to tear, as the foreskin has two layers; perhaps the outer layer is cut while the inner layer remains.
One must then cut again, causing further pain.  Purification with forceps does not guarantee that the end of the urethra is not cut, as it may have entered the opening.  As for purification with a knife, the skin might tear, ruining your work, or the child's skin might be naturally too short.
Many are born in such a way that they do not need purification, and I have seen this.  As for purification with scissors and tying with thread, experience showed me its superiority because the scissors make even cuts, for the blade on top is identical to the blade on the bottom.
When you squeeze your hand, the blades cut in the same manner and at the same time, so the thread becomes a wall for the urethra's skin from all sides, leaving no errors.  First, you must reassure the child.
Especially if he is slightly understanding, explain that you are only tying a thread to his urethra and will leave it until another day.  Then, please cheer and delight him by any means that you can.  Then, stop him before you, standing upright.
Do not have him sit; hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot, so the child's eyes do not see them or any other instruments at all. Then insert your hand into his urethra, inflate the skin, and pull it upward until the head of the urethra emerges.
Then, cleanse it of any accumulated dirt.  Then tie the marked spot with a doubled thread, then tie a second thread a little lower.  Then, grasp the lower tie with your thumb and forefinger, firmly, and cut between the two ties. Then quickly pull the skin upward, exposing the head of the urethra. Allow the blood to flow a little; this is better and lessens urethral swelling. Then, dry it with a damp cloth, and sprinkle dry, burnt pumpkin ash over it; this is the best I have tried, or else finely ground cumin seed powder, which is also excellent.
Then quickly pull the skin upward, exposing the head of the urethra. Allow the blood to flow a little; this is better and lessens urethral swelling. Then, dry it with a damp cloth, and sprinkle dry, burnt pumpkin ash over it; this is the best I have tried, or else finely ground cumin seed powder, which is also excellent. Then, place it on a poultice of a hard-boiled egg in rosewater, beaten with soft, fine rose oil, and leave it on overnight. Then, continue treating it with the rest of the treatment until healed.
This is a suitable scissor design for the procedure.
The scissors should be straight, sharp, and nail-like.  The blades should be the same length as the handles.  A mistake in the procedure is that sometimes the inner skin is all or partially turned inside out during cutting; therefore, it should be pulled back using your fingernail before the area swells.
Cut it [the skin] straight. If you can't grip it with your fingernail, pull it with a hook and cut it. If three days pass and the area beneath the head of the urethra remains swollen, leave it until the inflammation subsides and gently remove the skin.
Cut it as best you can, and be mindful of the head of the urethra.  If a bit of the urethral head is cut, it won't do harm. Treat it using the poultices described in the chapter on poultices.
If more skin is cut than necessary and it retracts upward, that is not much of a concern either. Treat it as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-eight.
On treating urine retention in the bladder.
Urine retention in the bladder occurs due to obstruction by a stone, coagulated blood, pus, or growing flesh, and the like. If you treat it with the various treatments described in the classification, as previously mentioned.
If the urine has not been released, and you suspect the retention is due to a stone that has damaged the neck of the bladder, the patient should be placed on their knees, then one leg should be placed on the patient's back.  The patient should extend their abdomen toward their back and hold themselves as firmly as possible.
This will force the bladder neck to push the stone backward, allowing the urine to flow. If the urine does not flow as described and the patient's condition worsens, the extraction of the stone using an instrument called a catheter, whose image is [as follows]: should be employed.
It is made of silver, thin and smooth, hollow like a bird's feather tube, about one and a half spans in length. It has a gentle funnel at its head.  To draw the urine with it, one should take a doubled thread and tie a piece of wool or cotton securely to one end.
The thread's end is inserted into the lower end of the catheter, and any excess wool is trimmed with nippers so it can be inserted into the tube, sealing it like a stopper.  Then, the catheter is lubricated with oil, butter, or egg white, and the patient sits on a chair.
The bladder and urethra are softened with moist ointments or lukewarm oil and water.  Then, the catheter is gently inserted into the urethra until it reaches the base of the urethra.  The urethra is then pulled upward towards the navel, and the catheter is pushed inside.
Once the urethra is pulled, the catheter is pushed inside until it approaches the rectum.  The penis is then tilted downwards, the catheter inside, and pushed until it reaches the bladder, where the patient feels it has reached an empty space.
This is done in this way because the channel through which urine flows is curved.  Then, the thread with wool is pulled slightly tightly, so the urine follows the wool.  Then, it is removed, and the urine is drained, and the catheter is reinserted.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.
Chapter Fifty-nine.
How to inject the bladder with a syringe.
Images of the instruments suitable for this purpose are made of silver or of hollowed ivory.  They have a long, slender tube, and one would wish to instill water and medicines into it.  This is done with an instrument called a syringe, and this is its image.
Made of silver or hollowed ivory, it has a long, slender tube, hollowed completely except for the end, which is solid with three holes; two on one side and one on the other side, as you see.
The hollowed portion where the projectile is, should be just large enough to hold it without any excess, so that if you draw something of the moisture, it is drawn out; and if you push it, it is pushed out to a distance, just as the oil-sling used in naval warfare does.
If you wish to remove moisture from the bladder, insert the tip of the syringe into the moisture and pull the projectile upward.  The moisture is drawn into the syringe.  Then, insert the tip into the urethra, as we described in the catheters, and push the moisture out using the projectile.
Then that moisture will reach the bladder, so that the patient feels it.  This is also an image of a gentle enema, used to irrigate the bladder.
It is made of silver or a leather-like material. Its upper end resembles a small funnel, and below it is a groove where the cord is placed. Then, a camel's bladder is taken, and the moisture with which you wish to irrigate the bladder is placed inside it. Then it is firmly tied between the two grooves with a doubled thread.
Heat that moisture slightly over the fire. Then insert the tip of the irrigator into the urethra.  Then firmly press on the bladder with the moisture, until the patient feels that the moisture has reached the bladder.
If you do not have a bladder, take a piece of parchment, make a circle out of it, and pierce it all around. Then, tightly insert a thread into these holes, circling them. Then, fold the circle as a table is folded, having placed in it whatever moisture, ointments, and water you desire.
Then tie it to the instrument and use it as you did with the bladder, pressing with your hand until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
On removing stones.
We have previously described the types of stones, their treatment with medications, and the differences between kidney stones and bladder stones, along with the signs of each.  We have also discussed stones where manual removal and incision are necessary.
This is specifically the stone formed in the bladder, and which develops in the urethra.  I will describe this clearly and concisely.
I say that bladder stones are most common in children.  A sign of this is that the urine is discharged from the bladder, thin like water.
Sand is visible in it, and he rubs and plays with his penis, often hanging down.  Often, the anus is also affected.  Children recover more easily from this before they reach fourteen years old, while it is more difficult in the elderly.  For young adults, it lies in between.
Those with larger stones are easier to treat, while the opposite is true for smaller stones.  If we turn to treatment, one should first administer an enema to evacuate all the waste in the intestines, as this may prevent the stone from being found during examination.
Then the patient is taken by the feet and shaken downward to make the stone descend to the neck of the bladder, or he is made to jump from a high place repeatedly.  Then, you should sit him upright before you, with his hands under his thighs, so that the entire bladder tilts downward, and then examine him.
And feel for it from the outside.  If you feel the stone in the space, immediately make an incision on it. If it is not felt at all, you should anoint the index finger (if the patient is a boy) or the middle finger with grease from the left hand.
If it is a fully grown boy, insert it into his rectum and search for the stone. Once it is under your finger, move it slowly to the neck of the bladder, then press on it with your finger and push it out towards the area you intend to incise.
And instruct a servant to compress the bladder with his hand, and instruct another servant to extend the buttocks upwards with his right hand, and with his other hand to extend the skin beneath the buttocks toward the area where the incision will be made. Then take the dissecting forceps, which has this shape:
Make the incision between the buttocks and the pubic area, not in the middle, but on the side of the left hip.  The incision should be directly over the stone. Your finger should be in the rectum, pushing the stone outward, angled so the incision is wide on the outside and narrow on the inside, as much as possible to allow the stone to pass out.
Do not press too hard, as the finger in the rectum might dislodge the stone during the incision, causing it to come out without difficulty.  Know that some stones may have angles and edges, making their removal difficult.
Some are smooth, like acorns, and round, making them easy to remove.  Those with edges and angles, increase the incision slightly. If it still does not come out, you must devise a way to remove it, perhaps using a tightly fitting forceps with a rasp-like tip.
To grasp the stone firmly, so it does not slip, or perhaps insert a flexible, curved instrument underneath it. If that is not successful, widen the incision slightly. If excessive bleeding occurs, stop it with a cautery.
If there is more than one stone, first push the largest one towards the bladder opening, then make an incision on it.  Afterwards, push the smaller stones out.  Do the same if there are more than two.  If the stone is extremely large, one should not make a large incision, as this would expose the patient to one of two outcomes.
Either he dies, or he has constant dribbling of urine, because the wound will not heal.  Try to push it out, or devise a way to break it with forceps to remove it piece by piece.
After completing the procedure, treat the wound with kandar, sabr, shiyan, and shiddah. Place a bandage soaked in oil, syrup, rosewater, or cold water over it to reduce inflammation. The patient should lie on their back, and the bandage should not be removed for three days.
Once the swelling subsides, wash the area thoroughly with water and oil. Then treat it with the palm ointment and the basil ointment until healed.  If excessive inflammation, festering, or similar problems arise in the wound, such as blood clotting within the bladder preventing urination, which is recognizable by blood in the urine, occur.
Insert your finger into the wound and remove the blood, for its remaining will lead to bladder corruption and infection.  Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type of condition with a corresponding therapy until healed.
Throughout the treatment, the thighs should be bound together to secure the medications applied to the affected area. If the stone is small and has lodged in the urethra, causing inflammation and preventing urination, treat it as described previously before it ruptures.
Often, this treatment obviated the need for incision.  I have tested this method, using a steel file of this shape:
A triangular, sharp, pointed steel file is attached to a stick.  Then, a thread is used to tie the penis below the stone to prevent it from returning to the bladder.  Gently insert the file into the urethra until it reaches the stone.
Rotate the file gently against the stone, gradually attempting to pierce it until it penetrates to the other side.  Urine will then flow.  Then, grasp the remaining portion of the stone on the outside of the penis; it will crumble and exit with the urine, curing the patient.
If this treatment is not possible due to an obstacle, tie a thread below the stone and another above the stone. Then, incise the stone on the penis between the two threads, and remove it.
Then, untie the threads and cleanse the coagulated blood in the wound.  The thread was tied below the stone to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other thread was tied above to allow the skin to return to its place and cover the wound after the stone is removed.
Therefore, when tying the upper thread, you should lift the skin upwards so that it returns to its place and covers the wound, as we have said.
Chapter Sixty-one.
On removing stones in women.
Stones rarely form in women. If one of them presents with a stone, treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons, one of them being that the woman may be a virgin, and another that you will not find a woman who allows herself to be examined by a physician.
If she is chaste or from among those with close relatives, and thirdly, that you will not find a woman skilled in this practice, especially in manual work; and fourthly, the site of the incision on the stone in women is far from the stone's location, so a deep incision is required.
In that case, there is a risk. If necessity demands it, a skilled female physician should be sought, though this is rare.  If not available, seek a virtuous and discreet male physician or have a skilled female midwife or a woman experienced in this craft present.
She should be present and instructed to perform all the examinations necessary to locate the stone.  First, if the woman is a virgin, a finger should be inserted into her vagina to search for the stone; if found, it should be pressed against the finger.
Then instruct her to make the incision. If she is not a virgin, but a married woman, instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the woman's vagina to search for the stone, after placing her left hand on the bladder and pressing it firmly.
If found, she should guide it from the opening of the bladder downward, as far as possible, until it reaches the root of the thigh.  Then, she should make an incision in the vicinity of the middle of the vulva, at the root of the thigh, from whichever side the stone appears to be located.
Her finger should remain on the stone, pressing it. The incision should initially be small.  Then, a probe should be inserted into the small incision. If the stone is felt, the incision should be enlarged as much as is necessary to extract the stone.  Be aware that stones come in various forms.
They vary in size, smoothness, roughness, length, shape (round, and branching).  Know their types so that you may recognize what you want. If bleeding is excessive, apply pulverized astringent to the area, holding it for an hour until the bleeding stops, then return to your procedure.
Until the stone is removed.  Ensure you have the tools mentioned for removing stones from men, to assist you in your procedure. If bleeding is excessive, and you know from the pulse that it is from a severed artery, apply a styptic to the area.
Hold it firmly in place with the styptic, and do not repeat this procedure. Leave the stone alone. If the patient dies, then treat the wound. After a few days, if the wound is no longer bleeding or is beginning to fester, resume the procedure to remove the stone.
Chapter Sixty-Two.
In the case of a water tumor.
A hydrocele is the accumulation of moisture in the white peritoneum that is beneath the skin of the scrotum surrounding the testicle.  It may also occur in a special membrane created by nature on one side of the testicle.
So that it might be mistaken for another testicle, and it lies between the scrotum and the white peritoneum which we have mentioned. This occurs rarely.  This hydrocele results from weakness, affecting women, into which this substance accumulates, or it may arise from a blow to the affected area.
This moisture takes on various colors, either yellow, or blood-red, or dark-red, or white, which is the most frequent.  The signs by which it is recognized.
Where water accumulates, if it is in the white peritoneum mentioned, the swelling is somewhat oblong, like an egg, and the testicle does not appear because the moisture surrounds it from all sides.
If the moisture is in a special membrane of its own, the swelling is rounded on one side of the testicle.
For this reason, one might imagine it as another testicle. If the moisture is between the scrotum and the white peritoneum, it is palpable.  To ascertain the color of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe previously described; judge its contents by what emerges from under the probe.
If we turn to treatment with iron, the patient should be bled if possible, and if you see his body is full, then he should lie on his back on something slightly elevated, placing numerous cloths underneath, and you should sit on his left side.
Have a servant sit on his right side, extending his penis to one side of the scrotum and toward the lower abdomen. Then, take a broad scalpel and make a lengthwise incision through the scrotum, from the middle, towards the groin.
The incision should be made in a straight line parallel to the line dividing the scrotum in half, until reaching the white peritoneum.  Carefully dissect it, avoiding further incision;  focus your dissection more on the side of the testicle, and continue dissecting as much as possible.
Then, dissect the water-filled peritoneum widely, removing all the water.  Separate the edges of the incision with forceps, and lift the peritoneum upward, avoiding contact with the scrotum. Cut the peritoneum as much as possible, either all at once or piece by piece.
Especially the thin part; if you do not completely sever it, there is a risk of the water returning. If the testicle emerges from its covering during your procedure, return it to its place, then sew the edges of the scrotum together.
Then treat it as you would any other surgical wound until it heals. If the testicle is already damaged by another disease, you should tie off the vessels in the suspensory ligament to prevent bleeding, then remove the testicle from the suspensory ligament and take out the testicle, and then treat it as described earlier.
If the accumulated water is in both areas, know that they are two hydroceles. Incise the other side as you did the first, or if you prefer, perform the procedure once. Then clean the blood.
Place wool soaked in oil or rose oil into the incisions, and place another layer of wool soaked in medicated wine and oil over the testicles and the abdominal wall.  Cover this with folded bandages (the *rafā'id*). Tie it (the bandages) from above with the six-pronged bandage, in this manner:
Then, take two bandages, sew them together, and fill them with fluffy wool, in this manner.  Make the fastening ends from cotton or damp wool, and secure them tightly with a belt around the trousers. One of the fastening ends is placed on the buttocks, towards the back.
The second fastening end goes towards the pubic area, and the other two ends go along the lower thighs. The remaining two ends are placed on the upper thighs. All the fastenings are brought together in the belt around the trousers.  These hydroceles can also be treated with cauterization instead of incision with an iron.
This involves taking a gentle, heated, knife-like cautery iron and cutting through the testicle's skin, until the white peritoneum containing the water is exposed. Take another cautery iron, in this manner:
It resembles a Greek eye; then, using it while hot, cauterize the peritoneum until all the moisture is expelled.  Then, using forceps, stretch the peritoneum and cauterize the surrounding layer, cutting it as much as possible to remove the entire hydrocele, being careful not to burn the testicle.
Know that this method is safer and avoids blood loss, and is superior to incision.  If during the incision or cauterization a hot swelling or bleeding or something else occurs, you should treat it accordingly with the appropriate remedies, as described previously.
If the patient is timid and cannot endure this procedure because of its unpleasantness, let him use the cauterization method as described, if it is valid in your opinion according to the evidence provided earlier.
If the hydrocele is watery, the patient should sit on a raised chair, then cauterize the swelling from below with a wide spatula.  Make the cauterization along the body and keep the opening wide so all the water drains out. Then apply cotton to the area and bandage it.
Allow the remaining water to drain, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not drain during the cauterization, it is because the white membrane obstructs the opening of the wound and prevents the water from escaping.
In that case, you should insert a goose feather, or the instrument used for drawing out the water of hydroceles (a description of which has already been given), or slightly enlarge the wound opening.
Know that the water may return and accumulate after six months or so.  If it does, cauterize it in the same manner and the patient should be treated accordingly.
Sixty-third Chapter.
On the treatment of fleshy fistulas.
Know that a fistula on these fleshy tumors is often a dangerous condition leading to death.  Therefore, it is best to leave it alone and seek safety.  I will describe the procedure and the types of tumors involved.
I say that many tumors can arise in the organs of the female anatomy, for various reasons.  Sometimes, it comes from a sharp, concentrated discharge to the female organs; sometimes from a blow.  The tumor's color will be the same as the body's, and it will not contain any water.
The tumor will be hard, and possibly stony, with a dark color and no sensation.  It may be caused by the entanglement and swelling of arteries, as previously mentioned, or by the swelling of veins.  If caused by arterial swelling, this is recognized by the tumor's unevenness.
If you press it with your fingers, it should not be incised.  However, if it is caused by swelling of the veins, nothing will dissipate when pressed with the fingers. This type can be incised, as can other fleshy tissues.
The procedure is to incise the scrotum, then pull the testicle upward, remove it from the white peritoneum, free the attachments from the vessels, tie the vessels, and cut the attachments after freeing them from all sides of the testicle. If the testicle has adhered to the growing flesh.
Then the testicle must be removed and cut. If the adhesion is between parts of the peritoneum or between the vessels, all such adhesion must be freed and cut in a circular fashion.
If the fleshy growth is in the area of attachment behind, all of it must be cut and the testicle removed, as we have said.  Once your work is done, dress the wound with wet wool, rose-oil, and drink, then treat it with further remedies until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Four.
On treating the dropsy associated with dropsy.
Dropsy is a twisted swelling, somewhat like a cluster, with relaxation of the limbs, making movement, exercise, and walking difficult for the afflicted person.  Treatment for this condition is similar to what was previously mentioned.
However, we should note the method used by the ancients in this case.  I say that the afflicted person should sit on a raised chair, then pull the relaxed limbs downwards, and hold the scrotum skin with your fingers, along with the vessels close to the penis.
A servant of yours should hold it and stretch it firmly.  Then, with a wide, sharp scalpel, make a slanted incision near the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.  Then, dissect from every side as I described to you in the dissection of the arteries in the temples.  Then, insert a needle with a double thread into them.
Tie it at the initial points where the dropsy presented, and also at its end.  Then, make a vertical incision down the middle, to remove the collected, putrid, corrupted moisture.
Then, treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds, allowing time for the vessels that nourish one limb to recede.  There is no harm in that.  If the dropsy affects all the vessels, then one limb should be removed along with the vessels, lest the testicle be deprived of nourishment. Before cutting the vessels, it will wilt and be of no use.
Chapter Fifty-six.
On the treatment of intestinal dropsy.
This dropsy arises from a tear in the peritoneum extending across the abdomen, approximately at the groin area of the abdominal wall, causing the intestines to protrude into one of the testicles.
This hernia may result either from a tear in the peritoneum or from its stretching.  These two types occur due to various causes, including blows, leaps, shouts, or lifting heavy objects.  If it's from stretching of the peritoneum, it develops gradually over a long time.
It does not appear suddenly.  The swelling is gradual, extending deeply because the peritoneum compresses the intestines.  If it is from a tear in the peritoneum, it is initially accompanied by severe pain, and the swelling is visible, distinct, and close to the skin's surface.
This is due to the intestines protruding outside the peritoneum.  Fluid may also protrude with the intestines, making this dropsy intestinal and fluid-filled.  It may also contain air, and the intestines may become filled with feces, which could lead to the patient's demise.
Because it causes severe pain and rumbling, especially when compressed.  Treatment for these types of ailments with iron is dangerous; therefore, one must be cautious.  The procedure involves instructing the patient to push the intestines back inside their abdomen if they can return.
Then, he should lie on his back with you positioned above him, and his legs should be raised.  Then, pull up the skin around the groin area, and cut the skin of the scrotum all the way lengthwise. Then, insert hooks into the edges of the cut, as much as needed to open the cut further.
The incision should be as wide as possible to allow the testicles to protrude.  Then, peel back the peritoneum beneath the scrotum's skin until the firm, white peritoneum is visible from every side.  At that point, insert your index finger into the area below the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum under the testicle's skin and the second peritoneum, release the adhesions behind the testicle.  Then, use your right hand to pull inward on the scrotum's skin, while simultaneously using your left hand to pull the white peritoneum upward and lift the testicle and peritoneum towards the incision.
Instruct the attendant to pull the testicle upward, while you fully release the adhesions behind it.  Use your finger to check for any twisted intestines within the firm white peritoneum.  If you find any, push them back into the abdomen below.
Then, take a needle with a thick thread, twisted from ten strands, and insert it at the end of the peritoneum beneath the scrotum's skin, near the incision.  Then, cut the ends of the thread so that there are four strands remaining. Then, braid some of the strands together and tightly tie the peritoneum, which we said is beneath the scrotal skin, on both sides.  Then also tie the ends of the threads tightly so that nothing from the blood vessels that feed it can reach it. To prevent a painful swelling.  Also, make a second ligature, positioned outside the first, a distance of less than two finger widths away.  After these two ligatures, leave a section of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin the size of a finger bone.
To prevent a painful swelling.  Also, make a second ligature, positioned outside the first, a distance of less than two finger widths away.  After these two ligatures, leave a section of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin the size of a finger bone. Cut the rest completely around and remove the testicle. Then, make a cut beneath the scrotal skin, one that bleeds and oozes as we previously described. Then, use wool soaked in oil, placing it in the wound, and apply the ligature as we described.
The white peritoneum, which we mentioned after cutting it, may need cauterization to prevent bleeding, as this often occurs.  Leave the ligature until it falls off by itself. If it is slow to fall off, apply hot water and apply something to sweat it off until it comes away.
Then treat the wound with the usual treatment for surgical wounds until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Six.
In this windy colic, I have seen no one dare treat it with iron.  The ancients described a treatment, as we described for colic with a fistula, in that the vessels are to be tied gently below the incision.
Then it is incised in the center, and the swelling is treated to open it, until the vessels fall out.  Treat the wound as we described until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Six.
In the hernia that occurs in the groin.
A hernia in the groin may appear, as we said, bulging the area, but nothing from the intestines descends into the groin.  If it does descend, it is slight and always returns.
But if it persists for a long time, the opening in the peritoneum increases so that the intestines and the testicle descend into the scrotum.  This occurs due to the extension of the peritoneum in the groin, as we said. This is because the peritoneum extends and then relaxes, causing the area to bulge, and the treatment is by cautery, as I previously described.
And it can be treated with iron in this manner: the patient lies on their back before you, then you make a transverse incision in the bulging, protruding hernial area, about the width of three fingers, and then you cauterize the peritoneum under the skin.
Until the white peritoneum beneath it is exposed.  Then you take a thread, place it on the protruding area of the peritoneum, and press it into the depth of the abdomen.  Then you stitch the two protruding areas on the thread of the peritoneum, and sew one to the other.
Then you pull out the thread, and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle or anything else, as I instructed you in the treatment of intestinal hernias.  Then treat it with surgical treatments.
When the sutures are broken, clean them, and dress the wound until it heals.  Then the peritoneum contracts, and the hernia does not increase.
And this method is superior in this location, because it is closer to safety.
Chapter Sixty-eight.
Relaxation of the scrotum's skin.
The scrotum's skin often relaxes in some people without the underlying tissues relaxing, which makes the appearance unattractive.  Therefore, those who wish to treat this should have the patient lie on their back.
He should cut all the relaxed skin away from the thick skin, then bring the edges together.  If desired, one may first stitch the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, securing the stitching, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the other treatments for wounds until it is healed and the stitches fall out.
Chapter sixty-nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
For this reason, I should not have mentioned it in this book.  However, I have done so for two reasons: one is so that a physician, if asked, will be knowledgeable about it and will know the treatment for those who are afflicted; the other is that we often need to castrate certain animals for our benefit.
For example, sheep, oxen, and cats, and similar animals.  I say that castration comes in two types, either by crushing or by cutting.  The method of crushing is to place the animal in hot water until its testicles relax and soften and hang down.
Then you crush them with your hand until they dissolve and are no longer distinct to the touch.  As for castration by cutting, it is necessary to hold the animal and grasp the skin of its testicles with the left hand, then tie the cords and make one cut on each testicle.
Until the testicles protrude, then cut them after peeling them, leaving nothing of the membranes except the thin membrane that is on the vessels. This method of castration is better than that which is done by crushing.
Because crushing might leave a part of the testicles, and the animal may desire to mate.  Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
In the treatment of hermaphrodites.
The hermaphrodite in men is of two types. One appears in the area around the groin or on the scrotal skin, between the testicles, resembling a woman's vulva with hair, and urine may flow from the area on the scrotal skin.
In women, there is one type, located above the vulva on the pubic bone, like small, protruding male testicles. One of them resembles a man's penis, and both resemble female genitalia.  The treatment for these three types.
The two types of men and the one type of women, the excess flesh must be cut so as to remove its effects, and then treated with the treatment of other wounds until healed.  As for the second type of man from whom urine issues from the scrotal skin, no treatment is effective and no healing occurs.
Chapter seventy-one.
In the excision of the clitoris and the protruding flesh of women's vulva.
The clitoris may grow beyond its natural size, becoming unsightly and discolored.  In some women, it may enlarge to resemble that of a man, interfering with sexual intercourse.
One should grasp the excess clitoris with one's hand or forceps and cut it off, but not excessively, especially not into the depth of the root, lest excessive bleeding result.
Then treat it with surgical remedies until it heals.  As for the flesh that grows, it is flesh that sprouts in the vaginal opening until it fills it up.
And sometimes it protrudes outward, like a tail. Therefore, some earlier practitioners called it the "tail disease."  One should cut it off, like the clitoris, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-two.
On the treatment of dislocations.
Dislocation is when a woman's vulva is not perforated or the perforation is small, and it may be either natural at birth or accidental.  The accidental case arises from a preceding cause, which may be from excess, overgrown flesh.
or thin or thick peritoneum, and it may be in the depth of the uterus or in its sides or above or below it. It prevents intercourse, procreation, and childbirth, and may even prevent menstruation.  Recognition of this is through examination by a midwife.
If the obstruction is visible and near, but if not visible, let her examine it with her fingers or with a probe. If the obstruction is from thin peritoneum and is close to the labia, then quickly pierce it.  This involves placing something like a splint on the labia, then placing both thumbs on it.
And the woman is on her back with her legs spread, then she stretches the labia forcefully until the thin peritoneum is pierced and the obstruction opens. Then she takes wool, soaks it in oil, and places it on the area, and the woman is to have intercourse each day so that the area does not close up again.
If the peritoneum is thick and dense, then it should be cut with a wide scalpel resembling a thin leaf. If the obstruction is from overgrown flesh, then it should be suspended with forceps and cut.  Have with you remedies to stop bleeding without causing further wounds, such as acacia, myrrh, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then a wide lead tube is used to prevent the wound from closing quickly; it is held in place for days.  Dry linen thread is used, and then the wound is treated as with any abscess until it heals.  Another growth of flesh may appear in the uterus, and it should be cut in this manner.
Unless it is a cancerous growth, the cancerous tumor that is in the uterus should not be treated with iron at all.
Chapter Seventy-three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red boils that appear in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the veins' openings, causing a constant flow of copious blood.  If hemorrhoids progress, they become warts.  Their types and signs were mentioned in the classification, and we describe them here.
Regarding those that accept treatment and those that do not: I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if deep within the uterus and not perceptible to touch, have no treatment with iron.  Those in the mouth of the uterus, which are felt, are treatable.
The woman should enter a cool room, and then the warts should be scraped with a scalpel or a rough cloth, and cut from their roots.  Then, when blood flows from one of the blood-cutting instruments, they should be dusted without pricking.
Such as acacia, cinnamon, and frankincense, etc.  Then, raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cold water. If the bleeding persists, seat her in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate rind, and astringents, etc. Then take barley flour, knead it with honey and vinegar.
Apply this to her back.  If the bleeding stops, then stop.  Otherwise, apply leeches to the pubic area, breasts, without restrictions.  Then take wool, dip it in the juice of mugwort, or the juice of goat's rue, or the juice of willow branches, with astringent drink, and apply it to the affected area.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals.  As for the red sore, it resembles the heads of boils, having a rough appearance.  It should be cut away as much as is visible, as described for warts, and treated until healed.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
On treating a tumor in the uterus.
Many types of tumors can appear in the uterus, such as cancer, stony tumor, ulcers, wasting disease, devouring disease, polyps, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and hot tumors.  All of these diseases and their types and symptoms have been discussed in the classification.
In this treatise, we must describe the hot tumor that appears in the uterus, if it is one of the tumors that have collected for a time.  How should it be treated with iron?  One must observe whether the initial pain of the tumor is sharp, accompanied by throbbing, inflammation, and fever.
And if you see it red, if touched, do not rush to treat it with iron. Treat it with what aids maturation until these symptoms subside.  Then, the woman should sit on a chair with two seats, leaning back.
And she should bring her legs towards the lower abdomen, spreading her thighs, and placing her arms under her knees, binding them with a suitable bandage.
Then, the midwife should sit on the right side and use the instrument that opens the cervix, a picture of which will be shown later.  When using this instrument, one should estimate the depth of the woman's uterus to ensure that what enters from the instrument does not exceed the depth of the uterus.
For if the instrument is longer than the depth, this will injure the patient.  Therefore, pads should be placed on the lip of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the uterus. The spiral that runs in the instrument should be on the upper side.
While you hold the instrument, the servant rotates the spiral until the cervix opens.  If a boil appears and is soft and tender when touched, it should be incised at the head with a broad scalpel.  Once all the matter has been evacuated.
A soft wick soaked in rose oil or green oil with some astringent should be placed in the wound. The wick should extend out of the slit in the cervix, and pure wool soaked in boiled water should be applied to the outside of the uterus and the pubic area.
Then, it should be treated with the appropriate ointment for two days until healed.
The uterus and wound may be washed by injecting water and honey, then water boiled with a long-stalked sos or zarawand root.  Then, return to the ointment, if the boil is hidden within the uterus.
One should refrain from treating it with iron, but rather treat it with what we mentioned in the classification.
Chapter seventy-five.
On instructing midwives how to treat living fetuses that emerge in an unnatural form.
The midwife should first know the natural form of childbirth.  Its signs include the woman's descent to the lower parts, her desire to breathe the air, and the easing of her postpartum discomfort.
If the delivery proceeds quickly, know that this birth is natural.  The fetus emerges headfirst, with the placenta either attached or hanging from its umbilical cord. If you observe these signs, then you should massage her abdomen to expedite the delivery of the fetus.
If it descends headfirst, the placenta descends with it, and is thoroughly cleansed of impurities.  Any birth not conforming to this pattern is unnatural and objectionable, as the fetus might emerge on its feet, or with its hands and feet coming out before the head.
And one hand, or one foot, or its head and hand, or it emerges curled, or perhaps turned upside down, and similar objectionable forms. The midwife should be skilled and gentle with all these things, and wary of mistakes and errors.
I am describing each form and how to deal with it so that you can recognize it. If the baby emerges headfirst, the natural way, and the delivery is difficult for the woman, and you see her strength waning, then have her sit on a chair and instruct other women to support her.
Bind her uterus with cooked licorice root and moist ointments. Then, the midwife should insert a small scalpel between her fingers to sever the placenta, or cut it with her fingernail, so that the moisture within it drains.  Massage the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then the woman should be injected with licorice root saliva mixed with shellac ointment. Then, after the injection, instruct her to cough and sneeze using a stick, and hold her mouth and nose. The fetus will emerge soon. If the baby's hands emerge first, gently push them back in gradually.
If entry is still not possible, place the woman on a platform, raise her legs, and shake the platform on the ground while the woman holds on to prevent falling during the shaking. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them out gently; it will come out. If the fetus emerges on its feet, push the feet up, then gradually turn the fetus to straighten it, and then gently pull one leg gradually.
If they emerge to the thighs, tell her to cough and sneeze with the stick; it will come out. If it does not come out as described, gradually return the fetus to its natural shape, and it will come out smoothly. If it still resists, as described.
Take saliva from the *khatmi* plant, saliva from licorice root, shellac ointment, and dissolved gum. Grind all thoroughly in a mortar. Then apply this mixture to the woman's vagina and lower abdomen.  Then, have her sit in lukewarm water until the lower intestines are reached.
If you see that the lower part has softened, make a poultice from myrrh and apply it.  If she holds it for an hour, have her sit on a chair, then sneeze, and plug her mouth and nose, and gently press her lower abdomen; the fetus will emerge immediately. The fetus will come out on its knees and hands.
If it emerges in this manner, gently insert your hands, and then the woman lies on her back, with her hips hanging down, on a bed, and holds her hands together, then gently press on top of her abdomen. Then remove the cushions under her legs so that her feet are elevated. If the fetus does not come out, take both legs and move them vigorously, then press gently on top of the intestines until the fetus ascends.  Then the midwife inserts her hand and shapes the fetus gently.
Instruct the woman to strain until the fetus emerges. The fetus will emerge sideways, with one arm hanging down.  Try to reposition the arm; if you cannot, have the woman stand and walk. If she cannot walk, have her lie down.
Shake her legs vigorously, then use the poultice described from saliva. If the delivery is delayed, have her sit in a chair, then try to reposition the arm and straighten the fetus. Have the woman sneeze and instruct her to strain until it emerges.
If the fetus emerges on its back, arms outstretched, and face towards its mother's back, the midwife should grasp the fetus's hand and gently turn and straighten it. Once straightened, apply the fat and saliva as previously described.
Then instruct her to move slowly.  Once she does, have her sit in a chair and lean to her right side.  Instruct her to strain and have her sneeze with the instrument (kandus).  This will cause the fetus to emerge, upright on its side.
If the fetus and placenta descend in this manner, cut the placenta with your fingernail until the moisture is released. If it is clinging to the fetus's face or neck, cut it from the umbilical cord so it doesn't suffocate and die. Then push the fetus inward until it is positioned correctly.
Instruct the woman to strain repeatedly; this will result in an easy delivery.  Regarding the delivery of twins or multiple fetuses, know that twins are frequent; sometimes three or four are born and survive, but this is rare.  Five is unnatural and they will not survive.
They are surrounded by a single placenta, even if numerous, and separated by a membraneous partition attached to the umbilical cord of each.  They may be delivered like a single fetus—naturally and easily—or be delivered in a difficult manner, as we have described.
If any of them are difficult to deliver and present the unnatural forms mentioned, treat them as described.  Let the midwife be gentle and do everything gently and carefully, using any available means to achieve safety.
It is possible for one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or more than ten fetuses to develop in the womb, and it has been confirmed to me that one woman miscarried seven and another fifteen, all of them conceived.
By the decree of the all-knowing, all-powerful God, this knowledge must be possessed by the midwife, so that she may be prepared for such an eventuality.
Chapter Sixty-seven.
On the delivery of a stillborn child.
If, after treating the stillborn child with the methods of treatment described in the section on treatment, the child does not emerge, and you are forced to intervene manually, then you must observe the woman.  If she is strong and healthy, and does not show any symptoms that cause concern for her well-being, then the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
Her head should be tilted downwards, and her legs raised. A servant should hold her from both sides, or secure her to the bed, to prevent her body from moving as the fetus is delivered.
Then, moisten the mouth of her uterus with moist ointments, along with the saliva of the nose, fenugreek, and flaxseed. The midwife then anoints her hands with these ointments and saliva, then gently inserts them into the uterus.  Then, she searches for a place to insert the hooks, targeting the appropriate locations on the fetus.
Observe; if the fetus is positioned to descend headfirst, insert the hooks into its eyes, or back of the neck, or mouth, or palate, or under its chin, or collarbone, or in locations near the ribs, or under the breastbone.
And if it descends feet first, then into the pubic area and the middle ribs.  The hook should be held in the right hand, its point positioned between the fingers of the left hand. The hand should be gently inserted and the hook implanted in one of the aforementioned locations.
Until she reaches an empty space, then inserts another or a third hook if needed to ensure an equal pull and prevent it from tilting to one side.  The pulling should be even, not just in a straight line, but also moving it in all directions.
To facilitate its removal, just as one does with a tooth when extracting it.  And in between, the pull must be relaxed. If it is caught on one side, the midwife should anoint some fingers with ointment and insert them to turn whatever is obstructing it.  If part of the fetus is expelled, the hooks should be moved to other locations.
She should slightly raise it to do this until the entire fetus is expelled. If a hand emerges before other parts and cannot be returned due to compression, it should be wrapped in a cloth and pulled until it is all out, and then it should be cut.
Either at the wrist joint or the elbow.  Likewise, this should be done for the other hand and the feet. If the fetus's head is large and encounters resistance during expulsion or has collected water in the head, a pointed instrument should be inserted between the fingers.
With it, the head is split open to allow the water to drain, or it is crushed with the instrument called a *mashdakh*, the image of which will appear with the images of other instruments in the following section.
Likewise, if the fetus has a large head, one should cut the skull or crush it with the *mashdakh*, as mentioned, then remove the bones with forceps. If the head comes out and is compressed at the collarbone, it should be cut to release the moisture in the chest.
Then the chest will contract.  If the chest does not contract and cannot be expelled, cut as much as you can.  If there is a swelling or lump in the lower abdomen, it should be incised so that the moisture flows out.
If the fetus is on its feet, pulling it out and aligning it to the opening of the uterus is easy. If it is compressed at the abdomen or chest, it should be pulled with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and the abdomen or chest should be cut to allow the contents to flow out.
If all the other organs are removed and the head is retracted and stuck, let the midwife insert her left hand. If the opening of the uterus is open, insert the hand into the cervix, seek out the head, and pull it with the fingers toward the opening of the uterus.  Then insert a hook or two hooks and pull with them.
If the opening of the uterus has closed due to a hot, inflamed swelling, do not force it.  Instead, use applications of moist, fatty substances, apply dressings, and sit in relaxing, moist waters.
If the fetus is on its side, if it can be straightened, use the methods described for a living fetus. If this is not possible, cut it and remove it.  Absolutely nothing of the placenta should be left inside.  When finished treating, use the remedies mentioned for hot swellings that appear in the uterus.
If bleeding occurs, she should sit in constricting waters and use other treatments.  I have witnessed a woman who conceived, the fetus died inside her, and subsequently conceived again.
Then the other fetus also died.  After a long time, a swelling appeared on her uterus, became inflamed, and started discharging pus.  I was called to treat her and treated her for a long time, but the wound did not close.  Then I applied some strong, drawing ointments.
A bone emerged from the area, and after a few days, another bone emerged.  I was astonished by this, as the abdomen does not contain bones.  I surmised that they were bones from the dead fetus.  I examined the wound and removed many bones from the fetal skull.
I continued this until I removed many bones, and the woman was in the best of health.  She lived for a long time, with a small amount of pus continuing to discharge from the area.  I have presented this unusual case here.
Because it contains knowledge and assistance for what the skilled physician attempts to achieve through treatment by hand.
Chapter Seventy-seven.
It contains images of the instruments needed for removing the fetus.
Image of a spiral for opening the cervix.
This binder, with which books are bound, has two spirals at the ends of the wooden boards.  However, this spiral should be finer than the binder and made either of ebony or of boxwood.  The width of each board should be equal to two fingerbreadths.
Their edges should be about a finger's width, and their length one and a half spans.  In the middle of the boards are two pegs of the same wood, securely fastened to them, whose length is half a span or slightly more, and their width about two fingerbreadths or slightly more.  These two pegs.
They are the ones that enter the cervix to open it when you turn the spirals.  An image of another instrument, similar to this but finer and lighter.
It is made of ebony or boxwood, shaped like tongs, but with two pegs at its tip, as you see. Each peg is about one span long and two fingerbreadths wide. If you wish to open the cervix with it...
Then have the woman sit on a bed, with her legs spread apart, and insert these two pegs, together, into the cervix.  You hold the end of the instrument underneath her between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you would with tongs, to whatever degree of opening you desire for the cervix. So that the midwife may do as she wishes.
