Chapter seventy-eight.
In the removal of the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, it is necessary to instruct the woman to hold herself, then to make her sneeze with a cupping glass, and to hold your hand over her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, and if not, use a certain amount.
Pierce a hole in the cover, place in it herbs that open the cervix, such as fumitory, sow thistle, dill, chamomile, rue, and yarrow. Place all or some of these remedies in the hole, cover them with water, and place over the fire. Then, place a silver tube on the hole of the cover, with the other end in the cervix.
Hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the uterus, and then make her sneeze, as we said. If the placenta comes out quickly, and if it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in the grease of the shrew or the saliva of the viper.
Then, she inserts it into the vagina and searches for the placenta. If she finds it, she grasps it and pulls it out slowly, little by little. If it is attached deep within the uterus, insert the hand as described until the placenta is found, then gently pull it out slowly.
So that the uterus does not fall during forceful pulling, it is necessary to gently move it to the sides, right and left, and then increase the pulling force.  This will help it release from the adhesion. If the cervix is closed, the treatment with sneezing and the pot and herbs remedy was described previously.
If it does not come out despite all the methods described, avoid forceful pulling. Instead, tie what has been expelled to the woman's thigh near the cervix. Then, inject the four-ingredient ointment. It will cause decomposition after a few days.
It will dissolve and come out, but if it decomposes, a foul odor will rise from it to the stomach and head, harming the patient.  Therefore, the appropriate fumigation should be used.
Therefore, some early practitioners tried the smoke of incense and dried figs.  A depiction of the device used to fumigate a woman when menses or the placenta are retained, and similar situations.
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or made of copper. The narrow end is placed near the vagina, the wide end over the fire.  Incense is placed on the embers and held until it is gone, then replaced.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
For the treatment of the unperforated rectum.
Many boys may be born with an unperforated rectum, blocked by a thin membrane.  The midwife should pierce this membrane with her finger, or, failing that, puncture it with a sharp scalpel, taking care not to touch the muscle.
Then place cotton soaked in wine and oil on it and treat with ointments until healed.  If you fear that it will close again, place a lead tube in the opening for many days, removing it when the child wishes to defecate.
The rectum may also be blocked by a healed wound or tumor.  It is necessary to incise this blockage, and then treat it with the remedies previously mentioned, and with cauterization.
Chapter Eighty.
On treating warts that appear on the lower part of the body.
Warts that appear on the lower part of the body are a complication and thickening that occurs near the anus, externally, or within the space on one side. The wart may be one or more.
If this thickening persists, it may open and discharge watery, whitish fluid or thin pus. Some of these warts may penetrate the rectum and intestines.  Those that are not penetrating may be recognized by what is expelled in stool and flatulence when the patient uses the bathroom.
And sometimes worms may emerge from them.  Some warts, if located in the space, may penetrate the bladder and urethra, or penetrate the hip joint and coccyx.  A penetrating wart to the anus can also be identified from those that are not penetrating.
Insert your index finger into the anus and introduce a thin probe of brass or iron into the wart, if there is no winding path. If there is a winding path, insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or the hair in your finger. If you feel nothing at all, and no stool, flatulence, or worms emerge from the opening, as we said, know that it is not penetrating, and proceed with treatment.
If the wart penetrates the bladder or urethra, the sign is the discharge of urine from it and the inability of the area to heal with medication.  If it penetrates the hip joint or coccyx, the sign is the probe reaching those locations.
If there is no winding path to the bone, and there is pain in a specific area, and pus constantly emerges from it, and treatment does not work and it does not heal with ointment, then none of these penetrating warts are likely to heal.
Treatment is a futile effort for those physicians who are ignorant of the matter.  However, for those warts that are not penetrating or chronic, healing is expected using iron, as I have described and tested.  Have the patient lie on their back before you.
Raise his legs upward, and tilt his thighs toward his abdomen. Then, insert the copper or lead probe into the wart, if there is a winding path, so you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it near the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel the probe has pierced through your finger, uncovered, without feeling any skin or flesh between your finger and the probe, then know for certain that it is penetrating.  Do not tire yourself with it, for it will not heal, as we said.
They say that it heals in some people rarely.  A helpful treatment is to heat a thin cauterizing iron according to the size of the wart, as previously mentioned.  Insert it heated into the wart until it reaches near the anus, and then repeat this two or three times.
So you know that all the excess, clotted flesh resembling a bird's feather tube has been burned.  Then treat it with a cloth soaked in clarified butter until the burned flesh comes out.  Then treat it with healing ointments; if it heals.
Otherwise, it will not heal with any other treatment.  If you insert the probe and it does not reach your finger in the anus, and there is a thick layer of flesh or membrane between the probe and your finger, and you see the wart is just below the skin surface, then cut the skin from the beginning of the wart. While passing the incision with the probe in the wart, continue the incision until you reach where the probe tip ends, remove the probe, and let it fall. Then cleanse the clotted flesh, which resembles a bird's feather tube, until nothing remains.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If bleeding overwhelms you and prevents you from removing the flesh, the best and fastest remedy is to cauterize it with fire or a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire combines two beneficial results: burning the excess flesh.
And cut the blood and dry the moisture. Then treat it with a cloth soaked in clarified butter or crushed sulfur with oil until the area becomes suppurative and the burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that promote firm flesh growth.
These are the remedies that are astringent and drying.  If it is cured, it is healed and there is no need to return. If the wart has reached deep into the anus and is far from the surface of the body, insert your finger into the anus and search with it.
If you feel the probe and between you and it is a membrane or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then the only action is to attempt it with hope.  This means to use one of three methods: either to cauterize it, as we said.
Or to slit it until you reach its bottom so that you can use a cloth and treatment from close.  Do not make the slit too large, lest you cut the muscle surrounding the anus, causing the patient to lose control of bowel movements.  Then treat it with the methods mentioned before.  Perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or, pierce through that membrane and puncture it after slitting it close to the anus, as we said, either with a probe or another sharp instrument. Then cleanse all the accumulated flesh in the slit to the extent possible.
Then close the entire wound, including the slit and the upper opening, until it heals.  The wart may remain open within the anus, which will be less troublesome for the patient.  The wart might be perforated in this manner.
If, when inserting the probe into the wart, it is on one side of the anus, towards the surface of the body, near the skin and the ring of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated tip, like a farrier's awl, in this way:
And insert a twisted thread of five threads or thereabouts into it. Then, insert the probe with the thread into the wart until it reaches its bottom.  If it is protruding into the edge of the anus from within, near the edge.
Then, remove the thread from that hole by inserting your finger into the anus and pulling out the end of the thread.  Bring together both ends and tie them firmly. Leave it for a day or two.  Whenever the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the ends of the threads breaks and falls away. Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the opening of the wart is not protruding, make it protrude as best you can, but not if it is deep within, for you must not do this because of the muscle, lest you cut it. Then do to it as we described until it is cured.
The image of the pointed scalpel used to cut warts should have a very sharp curve on one side, and the other side should not be sharp, so as not to cut what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-One.
Concerning anal fissures that bleed and their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are inside the anus, resembling red swellings or like grapes, and they come in small and large sizes, and blood constantly flows from them.
They may be single or multiple, and some are located outside the anus, at its edges. However, those outside the anus are usually less moist, dripping yellow water or a little blood continuously, and they are the color of the body.
The treatment for those inside the anus is to instruct the patient to defecate and strain until the anus protrudes and the polyps are visible to you.  Then quickly grasp and remove them with forceps or your fingernail, and cut them at their bases, if the forceps do not hold them due to their moisture and laxity.
Otherwise, grip it with a rough cloth or a piece of sharpened wood, or pull it out with your fingers, and then cut it.  Then, after the cut, sprinkle some sharp powders to act as cauterization, or cauterize it as previously described in the chapter on cauterization.
Then treat it with further remedies until it heals.  If the anus does not protrude, inject the patient with an injection containing a little stinging substance to wash out what is in the anus and prompt its quick expulsion when the patient strains. As for the polyps outside the anus, their treatment is easy and straightforward.
It is to grasp it with your fingernail or forceps, cut it, and then treat it as described until it heals.  If one dislikes cutting with iron, then it is necessary to use a perforation in this manner: Take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the polyp upward and pierce it with the needle at its base from the other side, wrap the ends of the thread around the needle, which is positioned across it, and firmly tighten the polyp.  Then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this with all the polyps, leaving one unpierced.
To cause the discharge of excess blood, then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or qirrooti on the anus and tell the patient to rest.  Then leave it until the polyps fall off.  If the polyps fall off, treat them with ointments and other remedies until they heal.
And as for the treatment of fissures, it often arises from dryness of the faeces and retention of the temperament.  If it persists and is not cured by medication, it is necessary to scrape it with a scalpel or with your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper layer of skin which prevents it from uniting is removed.
Then treat it until it forms a scab as is appropriate.  If it does not form a scab, repeat the scraping more forcefully than the first, until it bleeds profusely and is removed. Then treat it, and it will heal.
Chapter Eighty-Two.
On the treatment of ingrown nails, both ingrown and non-ingrown, dry warts, and the pimple.
The ingrown nail is a rounded protuberance the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail, which occurs throughout the body, particularly on the soles of the feet and fingers, and causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to cut around the nail, and to grasp it with a lancet or a hook and pull it out from its root. Then treat it.  Or, if you prefer, cauterize it as described in the relevant section.  As for dry warts, each is a small, rough, hardened, rounded protuberance on the surface of the body.
They frequently appear on children's hands.  Treatment involves stretching the wart, cutting it, or piercing it with a silk thread or hair until it falls off.  Or, if desired, cauterize it, as previously described, with fire or a sharp medicine.
Ingrown and non-ingrown warts can be removed with the instrument I mentioned in the treatment of the pimple.  A pimple is also a small, hardened, thick protuberance on the body's surface, extending deep into it.  If cold affects it, it feels similar to a stinging sensation of ants.
It occurs throughout the body, most often on the hands.  Treatment involves taking a strong goose or eagle feather tube and placing it around the pimple, so that the tube encircles it completely.  Then rotate your hand with the tube until you rupture the skin around the pimple.
You place it deep into the flesh and the pimple is pulled out from its root.  Or, if you prefer, you do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The top of the tube should be smooth and twisted, to allow easy gripping and twisting by the fingers. Or, if you prefer, cauterize the pimple, as previously described in the cauterization of warts.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
In the forms of instruments used for injections in rectal ailments, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe may be made of silver, or tin, or of hollowed or hammered copper.  Small and large sizes of this instrument can be made, according to the users' needs. Those used for treating young children, or those with narrow or painful rectums, should have very fine syringes.  Image of a large syringe:.
Its upper funnel, where the nozzle is attached, should be wide, with a partition where the nozzle is attached as shown.  The lower end, which enters the rectum, must be extremely smooth and polished, with two holes on one side and one hole on the other.
The size of the holes should be the thickness of a reed, or slightly thicker. The nozzle, into which the medicine is put, should be made of animal bladder or sheep's gut, shaped like a small spoon, with a diameter of one span.
It is pierced with many holes, as it is rotated, with the space between each hole being the thickness of a finger.  Then, a strong thread of doubled yarn, of ten threads or so, is inserted into these holes, collecting the nozzle like a spoon. If medicine is placed in it, tighten the top of the syringe between the stones firmly, then inject.  This is an image of the nozzle and holes.
The method of injection is to fill the gut or bladder with the medicine, while it is warm to the patient's tolerance.  Beware that it is not excessively hot or cold.  Then the patient lies on their back with their legs raised.
A mat or blanket is placed beneath.  Then the tip of the syringe is lubricated with ointment, egg white, or licorice juice, or similar.  Then it is gently inserted into the rectum.  The attendant holding the nozzle squeezes it firmly with both hands until all the contents of the syringe are emptied into the bowels.
Then the syringe is removed, and the patient lowers their legs against the wall. If the medicine is urging its way out, the patient should hold it back as much as possible. If the patient remains with it overnight, it is more effective in expelling it. If injected for cases of diarrhea and the expulsion is hastened, the injection should be repeated until healed.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, the syringe should be very gentle, smoother than the syringes used for children.  This is an image of a gentle syringe, its skin taut.
Chapter Eighty-Four.
On the treatment of surgical injuries.
I have previously discussed the treatment of injuries resembling burns in the classification.  Here I shall detail their treatment with hands and medications comprehensively.  I say that wounds differ according to the nature of the wound and the location of the injury.
The causes of wounds are numerous, such as a stone blow, a sword or knife cut, a spear or stick thrust, an arrow, and other similar things.
And as for wounds according to location on the body, such as wounds to the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver, and similar organs.
I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a standard and rule for all wounds.  I will begin with simple head wounds, specifically, as the complex ones will be discussed in the first chapter of the third book of this book.
So, if a simple wound occurs on the head, and it is not a bone fracture, I observe.  If it was caused by a stone blow or similar, and only the skin was damaged, and the wound is large and you fear the patient will develop a hot swelling, then promptly lance it with a lancet at that location. Do not delay this, and draw blood from him according to his strength, unless there is an impediment to bloodletting.  Especially if the blood coming from the wound is slight.  Let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the injured area.
Warn the patient against fullness, drinks, and heavy foods. If bloodletting is prevented, then reduce the patient's food intake, avoid meat and drinks.  If a hot swelling occurs, apply a cotton swab soaked in rose oil, alone or with a drink that has a constricting effect, to the wound.
If you fear a hot swelling, apply to the wound, if it is fresh and its blood has not been altered by the air (the specific type of air I will describe to you shortly), a compress. However, if the air has altered it, apply some ointments.
Until the pus comes out, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large, and caused by a sword or similar weapon, and its edges do not meet naturally, join them together with stitches, as described in the suturing of abdominal wounds.
If the skin has been peeled back from the bone, and is only attached by a small flap, then cut it off.  Then treat the wound with medications that will form strong flesh in place of the skin.  Instruct the patient to eat foods that provide strength, such as heads and viscera.
If the skin develops gangrene, and it is not attached to the bone, cut off all the gangrenous tissue and dispose of it. Then treat the wound. If, in these wounds, an artery or vein is bleeding, and the bleeding cannot be stopped with medications, locate the artery.
If you hit it, and it does not stop bleeding, cut it with a scalpel and tie it off.  If necessary, and the aforementioned treatments are ineffective, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and superficial, their treatment is easy and simple; they can be treated by simply sprinkling this powder (dhurur) onto them while they are still bleeding.
Before the air changes it, the recipe for the dhurur is: one part frankincense, two parts cinnamon, and three parts slaked or unslaked lime.  Grind all of these together, sift, and pack the wound with it.  Bind tightly so it adheres well.
It will coagulate with the blood, then apply bandages on top. Leave it undisturbed as long as it does not swell.  The medicine will not be removed until it heals.  The lime alone may achieve this, if frankincense and cinnamon are not available.
This same action can also be done in many large wounds, if its application is skillful and they are bound tightly.  This is the case with wounds that are fresh and still bleeding, provided the air hasn't altered them. However, if the air has changed them somewhat, especially if it is during the summer.
Therefore, there is no use in applying this dhurur.  Instead, treat it as described previously: apply some ripening ointment or a poultice of barley flour made with water and honey until the pus is released, then treat it with other remedies until it heals.
However, if the wound involves a minor fracture of the bone, pull it with the jift.  The treatment of head fractures is discussed in the section on neck injuries.  If the wound is in the neck, there is no difference in treatment from a simple head wound.
If a nerve or artery in the neck has been severed, if it's a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treatments that constrict too tightly, such as alum, vitriol, and the like, because they harm the nerve and cause spasms.
Absolutely nothing cold should be applied to the wound, because the essence of the nerve is cold and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs.  Instead, use gentle remedies like moistened rose water or rose oil and ceruse, which have been repeatedly washed with fresh water.
If also washed with fresh water, and other minerals in this manner, and moist ointments.  If the wound is large, use sutures or bring the edges together with strips, and then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has a cavity and pus has collected at the bottom, drain it at the lowest point.  If an artery has been severed in the wound and you see blood flowing, tie it off or cauterize it if necessary.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilage of the throat, but the veins are intact, bring the edges of the skin together with sutures along the cartilage of the throat.  Do not touch the throat itself, but smooth it and restore it to its natural shape, then tie it firmly and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that have a binding and drying effect until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, explore it. If there are fragments of bone, remove as many as possible.  If not all can be removed, leave it until the wound becomes necrotic and heals, making removal easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle it with the powder we described.  If bone in the wound becomes necrotic and forms a fistula, treat it as described in the section on chest and shoulder surgery.
If the wound is from a spear or knife and you see a cavity, look to see if the patient exhales wind when breathing.  If so, know that it is a fatal wound. If there is no cavity and it is fresh with blood, do not immediately apply the powder nor tighten it, lest the blood be trapped in the cavity.
For it will recede to the heart and kill the patient.  Instead, apply a drawing ointment, twice daily.  If you do not have ointment, place a piece of old cloth in the wound to absorb the moisture.  Place the patient on their back, with the wound facing up.
To drain what collects there. If three days or more have passed since the wound was inflicted and the patient shows no spasm, no bad palpitations, and no shortness of breath, and you find their other conditions good, know that the wound is healthy. Treat it with bandages and other therapies until healed.
If it is difficult to see and the wound is continuously discharging, know that it has become a fistula. Treat it according to its appropriate section. The patient should lie on the wound to drain what collects in it.  If the wound is on the chest or back, treat it with the sutures described previously.
If it is large, or if it is small and has been treated with powder, if it has affected the bone and broken off fragments, probe the wound and promptly remove the fragments if they are loose. If they are not loose, leave them until...
The wound will fester, and then it will be easier to remove them.  As for the other wounds that occur in the various organs, their treatment is the same as what we have described.
Eighty-fifth Chapter.
On wounds of the abdomen, and the expulsion and suturing of the intestines.
A wound that appears in the abdomen may be large, small, or medium. A large wound may cause the expulsion of one or several intestines, making their return and suturing more difficult and arduous.
A small wound, too, may present difficulties in another way, making the return of the intestines more arduous. If the intestines are not returned to their proper place immediately, they swell and thicken, making their return difficult.  Therefore, the best type of wound is the medium-sized wound, because returning the intestines is not as difficult as it is with these other two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen is done in four ways. Two sutures are general-purpose, suitable for suturing the abdomen and other bodily wounds. The other two sutures are specialized, applicable to all wounds, but are more specifically used for suturing the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation. I say, if the wound is small and something of the intestines has come out and its return is difficult, then this is due to one of two reasons: either the smallness of the wound, as we have said, or because the intestines have experienced inflation from the cold of the air.
If this is the case, it is necessary to warm it by dipping a sponge or damp cloth in lukewarm water, alone, or that has been cooked in it with the plants known as iḍḵar, saʿdī, and sanbal, and apply it to the intestines to dissolve the swelling.
A drink that has a binding quality may also be used, which is better than water alone in resolving the swelling. If you see that the swelling has been dissolved, moisten the intestines with water that has been cooked with the plant called ḫaṭmi or ḵabbāzī, for this will facilitate their return with ease.
If its return is still impossible after this treatment, then make a small incision in the wound using the instrument used for lancing boils. The initial treatment is preferable to incision, and incision is only resorted to when necessary and all other methods have failed. This is a depiction of the instrument.
Its one curved side is limited, while the other is unlimited. The thin end is not as thin as the scalpel's, but is slightly blunt. It is a small, dagger-like instrument, as you see. If the wound is wide and the intestines have entered, then it is necessary for their return to be to their natural shape.
Its specific location, if achievable, is best. However, if the wound is wide and in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs elevated higher than their head. If it is in the upper abdomen, then the head and chest should be elevated higher than the lower part of the body.
Likewise, if the wound is in one side of the abdomen, always aim to keep the side with the incision higher than the other side. This method should be used in major as well as moderate surgeries.
For minor surgeries, position it as best as you can. Then, have a helpful assistant hold the entire wound in their hands, bringing the edges together, and gradually exposing it to the surgeon for stitching.
This is the general method for single-stitch closure: Take a needle or several needles, proportionate to the wound's size. Then, leave a space equal to the thickness of the little finger on either side of the wound, and insert one needle without thread into the edges of the skin. With the edges of the peritoneum beneath the skin, from the inside, so that you pierce it from that side. The edges of the skin and the peritoneum have now joined, forming four layers. Then, pull the double thread around the needle several times on both sides, bringing the edges of the wound together firmly.
Then leave another space equal to the thickness of a finger and insert another needle. Then, thread it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue this process with as many needles as necessary until you have closed the entire wound.  The needles should be of medium thickness.
Because very thin needles readily cut through the flesh, and thick needles are difficult to insert into the skin, the needles should be of medium thickness.  One should not insert the needle into the edge of the skin too deeply.
So that the flesh is not cut quickly and the wound reopens before healing.  Also, do not space the stitches too far apart, so that the wound cannot heal.  Then cut off the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the patient while sleeping, and prepare for them a linen covering on each side to hold the needle ends.
Leave them until you know that the wound has healed.  This type of stitching with needles is best for small surgeries, because one or two needles may suffice for the stitching.
As for the method of stitching for the common person, it is to gather the four edges—namely, the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum—at once with a needle that has a thread of moderate thickness and thinness.  Then, after the needle has passed through all four edges, return the needle from the same side from which it began.
So that the thread is interwoven from above the wound, to make the stitches like the stitches used to secure bags.  Leave the space between each stitch the width of a little finger.  This type of stitching is known by everyone.
With this stitching, a surgeon treated a man who had a knife wound in his abdomen. The wound was more than a span in length and approximately two spans of the small intestine had protruded. The wound was located in the middle of the abdomen.
After the intestines protruded from the wound for twenty-four hours, he returned them. The wound healed in about fifteen days, and he was treated until he recovered. He lived many years afterward, managing all his affairs.  Physicians had pronounced that he would never recover.
Surprisingly, I did not treat it with ointment, because I was in a place where there were no medications available.  I placed worn cotton on the wound twice a day and moistened it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the special types of stitching, I mention it according to Galen's text: Begin the stitching from the skin, inserting the needle from the outside to the inside.  Once the needle pierces the skin and the muscle running along the entire length of the abdomen.
Leave the peritoneum edge on the side where you inserted the needle.  Then, insert the needle into the other edge of the peritoneum from inside to outside.  Once you have pierced it, pierce it again in this same peritoneum edge from outside to inside.
Leave the peritoneum edge on this side and insert the needle into the other edge from inside to outside.  At the same time as inserting it into the peritoneum, insert it into the peritoneum edge on its side, until it pierces all of it.
Then, begin again from this same side and line it up with the peritoneum edge on the other side. Bring the needle out of the skin near it, then return it to that skin and line up the peritoneum edge on the other side with this edge of the peritoneum.
Bring it out of the skin on its side, and do this again and again until you sew the entire wound in that manner.  In summary, the characteristics of this stitch are that of a furrier's stitching for fur.
By sewing the peritoneum once from one side with the skin edge, and leaving it once from the other side until it is finished; and as for the second type of specific suture that Galen also mentioned, this is his account verbatim.
It is that you sew it in the manner that some healers stitch it, by bringing each part to its matching counterpart, naturally; so they bring the peritoneum edge to its other edge, and the peritoneum edge to its other edge, in the manner I describe to you.
The needle should be inserted into the peritoneum edge nearest to you, from the outside, and penetrate it to the inside, alone; and leave the peritoneum edges. Then return the needle and penetrate it from the outside to the inside of both peritoneum edges, then return it again.
And penetrate it from the inside to the outside in the other peritoneum edge on the opposite side.  This type of suture is better than the common, simple suture previously mentioned. This is Galen's account verbatim. Some have said that a suture of the abdomen may be possible with two needles. That is, inserting a single thread, beginning by inserting the needle from your side, and penetrating it to the other side; and inserting the other needle from the opposite side, penetrating it to your side, according to the way the leatherworkers sew.
Know that if the rupture is in the middle of the abdomen, its suture is more difficult than in other parts of the abdomen.  As for the treatment of the wound, its treatment is the same as other wounds. That is, if you encounter the wound fresh with its blood before the air alters it, and return the intestines to their position and stitch them firmly, then apply the healing poultice to it.
If the wound has been altered by the air, apply some ointments that are available to you until it suppurates and the stitches fall out, and the peritoneum and peritoneum are joined together.  Then treat it like any other wound until it heals.
If you do not have any medicines available, apply to it from the beginning of suppuration, old cotton wool, and replace it twice a day as I instructed you until it heals.  You will not need further treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple, but if you fear that the wound may involve the major organs in pain.
Therefore, it is necessary to dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and armpits.  If he feels pain or foul smell in his intestines, this often occurs, then irrigate him with a lukewarm, black astringent drink.
Especially if the foul odor has reached the intestines and become a penetrating wound into its interior. Know that intestines which are thick are easier to heal, and those which are thin are more difficult to heal.
As for the intestine known as the jejunum, it will not heal at all from surgery occurring therein, due to the multitude and size of its blood vessels and the thinness of its substance and its proximity to the nerve's nature.  If the protrusion from the wound is a small portion and you grasp it promptly, return it in the same way you would return the intestine.
If a significant amount of time has passed and it has turned green or black, then you should tie it off with a thread above the blackened area, lest blood flow occur, for there are blood vessels and arteries in the protrusion.  Then cut what lies below that ligature.
And you shall leave the ends of the thread hanging from beneath the wound, outside it, so it is easy to pull and remove it when the protrusion falls and the wound suppurates.  The wound which occurs in the intestine has been described.  Now, if a perforation occurs in the intestine and it is small...
For in some people, it may be possible to heal it, for I have seen a person who had been wounded in the abdomen by a spear thrust, and the wound was to the right of the stomach.  The wound festered and became a fistula, from which feces and gas escaped. I treated it, though I did not hope for a cure. I continued to treat it until it healed and the area closed.  When I saw that the area had closed, I feared that the patient might experience some bad event in his belly.  However, he suffered no such mishap whatsoever and remained in the best of health. Indeed, he ate, drank, engaged in sexual intercourse, bathed, and exercised in his service.  Some experienced individuals have stated that if a wound occurs in the intestine and is small, it should be treated in this manner.
To do this, large, headed ants are taken, and then the edges of the wound are brought together.  An ant, with its mouth open, is placed on the edges of the wound.  When it grasps and closes its mouth, its head is cut off; then it will adhere and not separate.  Then another ant is placed near the first.
Continue doing this with several ants, according to the size of the wound.  Then, suture the wound.  These heads remain adhering to the intestines until the intestines heal and are cured.  No ailment occurs in the patient.  The intestines can also be sutured with thin thread.
To extract the animal adhesive from the Egyptian animal's adhesive, after having inserted it into a needle, one must take the end of this thread from the Egyptian animal's adhesive and extract it.  Then, a thin, spun linen thread is tied to its end, and this thread is then inserted into the needle. The Egyptian animal's thread is used to suture the intestines, then returned to the abdomen.  This type of suturing with ants and the Egyptian animal's adhesive is done with hope and expectation.
However, if the tear is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, there is no remedy, and no cure is possible.
Chapter Eighty-six.
On the treatment of colds and catarrh.
Know that any wound or swelling that becomes chronic, longstanding, and ulcerated, and continually discharges pus without ceasing, is called, in general terms, regardless of the affected part, a "nasus" or "cold.
It is, in fact, a hardened, dense, white mass without pain, having a cavity like that of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some called it a "feather."  Sometimes it is moist, continually discharging pus, and sometimes the moisture might stop. This moisture can be abundant, sparse, thick, or thin.
I have previously stated in the classification of surgical conditions that any ulcer that does not heal and in which flesh does not grow is due to one of nine causes, one of which is either a lack of blood in the body, or its poor quality, or because there is hard flesh inside the ulcer or on its edges that prevents the growth of healthy flesh. Or because it is excessively harmful and dirty, or because the ulcer itself is foul and the material discharging from it is of poor quality, or because the treatment is inappropriate, or because of a widespread disease affecting the region, or because of a specific local condition.
Or because it is excessively harmful and dirty, or because the ulcer itself is foul and the material discharging from it is of poor quality, or because the treatment is inappropriate, or because of a widespread disease affecting the region, or because of a specific local condition. As observed in the city of Saragossa, where the maturation of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is slow by nature, or because it contains a single bone or several bones.  I have described all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medications in the classification.
Here, I must inform you about the treatment of nasal polyps and colds that can be treated manually.  Know that nasal polyps can occur in all parts of the body, some of which lead to large vessels, arteries, or nerves.
or to the peritoneum, or to the intestines, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of complex structures like the hand and foot, and some lead to proximity to a major organ or similar.
Therefore, those polyps that exhibit these characteristics are among the difficult-to-heal diseases that do not accept treatment, and dealing with them is a burden and a cause for ignorance.  If there are polyps that have not reached these locations I mentioned, I hoped for and anticipated their treatment.
If you wish to ascertain this, take a probe of copper or iron. If the polyp is straight, probe it with that. If the polyp has a curve, probe it with a thin lead probe, as lead is pliable.
If the probe enters and turns towards the curve, if the polyp has many openings that you cannot identify with the probe, inject one opening with moisture, then the moisture will flow towards the other openings and drain out.
Then thoroughly investigate in any way possible to determine if there is bone or nerve, whether the polyp's base is far or near, or whether it is a single polyp with multiple openings.  Ascertain all of this to the best of your ability through careful examination, and consider the pain when you touch the area.
And similar to this are the signs and causes of the tumor. If you have determined all of this accurately, then proceed to treatment with confidence, and consider this: if...
If the polyp is visible, near the surface, or in a healthy location distant from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the locations previously mentioned, then cut the polyp as described, and remove any accumulated hardened tissue, rotten flesh, or excess flesh from within.
And treat any flesh or warts that grow in its mouth, until it heals. If the polyp is deep-seated and located on a straight line, you should cut it deeply as possible and then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh.
Then use a twisted, medicated thread soaked in potent medicines and insert it into the base of the polyp, which you can locate with the surgical instrument. Repeat this several times until the potent medicine consumes any remaining corruption in the base of the polyp.
Then apply ointments that promote healthy tissue growth until it heals. If it does not heal with this, cauterize it as described before.  If the cause of the polyp is bone, and you are sure of this, investigate and cut it as I previously instructed, unless prevented by something.
If the cause is a vein, nerve, or a principal organ, as we said. If the bone is exposed and has some corruption and blackness, remove the corruption until it is gone, and then treat it with a method that promotes healing until it heals. If it does not heal, and the pus persists as it had been.
Know that you have not reached complete removal of the corruption.  Expose everything affected again, and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with all your effort. Then apply the healing treatment. If it heals, well and good.  If not, know that this corruption lies deep within the diseased area.  Expose all affected areas again.
Thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with all your effort. If the corrupted bone is small and you can pull it out, pull it out with the fine forceps suitable for this purpose.
If there are many bones, thoroughly remove them all; leave nothing.  If any ligaments are obstructing them, and cutting them would not be dangerous, cut those ligaments and any surrounding flesh, and free the bones.
If you cannot remove the bones right away, apply something to fester the wound. Leave it for days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones have festered. Keep the wound open; do not allow the opening you created during your work to close.
Instead, if you fear that, apply a cotton ball soaked in powdered sulfur mixed with oil, or a cotton ball soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments.  When those ligaments fester and the bones loosen, they will be easy to remove and extract.
If it is a single large bone, like a leg bone or thigh bone, and only its surface is corrupted, thoroughly scrape it until the blackness and corruption disappear, then close the wound.  If a large portion is corrupted and the corruption has reached the bone marrow, it must be spread open.
Cut it completely to where the corruption ends.  Then treat it until it heals.  I am telling you about a case of illness that happened to a man in his leg, to make it an example and help in your treatment. This man was young.
About thirty years had passed; he had pain in his leg, originating from movement inside the body, until materials reached the leg and a large swelling developed. There was no cause from the outside. Then time passed, along with the mistakes of the physicians, until the swelling opened.
A large amount of material flowed from it, and he was poorly treated until the leg festered, and many openings appeared, all emitting pus and bodily fluids.  A group of physicians treated him for about two years, but none of them were skilled surgeons.
Until they came to me. I saw his leg, with a large flow of material pouring from those openings. The man's body was wasted, and his color was yellow. I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
I found that some of them connected to each other from all sides of the leg.  I then incised one of those openings, until I exposed some of the bone. I found it corrupted, gnawed, blackened, putrid, and perforated, until the perforation reached the marrow.  I reported what I had discovered.
I extracted the corrupted bone, and I believe that there was no corruption in the bone other than what I had removed and exposed. I then used healing drugs to try and close the wound for a long time, but it did not heal.  I then exposed the bone again, beyond the previous exposure.
I found the corruption connected to the bone. I then exposed what I had found of that corruption. I tried to close it but it did not heal or close.  I then exposed it again.  I continued to cut the bone piece by piece, attempting to close it, but it would not heal until I had removed about a span of bone.
I removed it along with its marrow, then closed it with medicines.  It healed quickly and recovered.  This repetition of work and cutting was necessary because of the patient's weakness, their poor tolerance, and my fear of their death, since they had suffered frequent fainting spells due to excessive vomiting.  They recovered completely.
Firm flesh grew in place of the bone, and their condition improved.  Their strength returned, and they regained their normal activities.  They suffered no impairment in their walking.  If a bone that had broken had grown in that location.
Therefore, it is necessary to expose it in this manner: Take a bandage and tie it to the end of the protruding bone. Have someone pull it upwards.  Use another bandage of thicker wool than the first.
Then tie it to the flesh beneath the bone and pull the end of the bandage to pull the flesh downwards.  As you expose the flesh in the area you want to expose, to prevent the saw from injuring the flesh.  Place a piece of wood or plank beneath the bone securely from below.
Because if you do that, the saw will not hesitate to cut the corrupted bone.  The exposure should be slightly above the corrupted area so that there is no corruption in the hollow of the bone, so it does not appear superficially to the sense, and you will not be forced to expose it again.
If the bone is corrupted, but not protruding, and its parts are joined together, and the corruption is in its center or in a part of it, then expose the flesh from all sides, place the plank underneath, and then expose it from the first side where the corruption is.
When you have finished exposing it, expose it from the other side.  Let the exposure be a little distance from the corruption, as we said.  If the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupted joint itself and expose the bone where it joins from the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy but removal. If the corruption is in the finger bones of the hand or foot, the treatment is very difficult.  However, you must scrape away the corruption however it appears, remove it, and clean it in any way you can.
By any means possible, as long as you are not obstructed by a vein or nerve. Chapter Seventy-eight.
In cutting off limbs and spreading the bones.
The limbs may decay either from an external cause or from an internal one. If you treat this decay with medicinal remedies, and the treatment is ineffective, and you see the decay spreading within the limb, nothing will restrain it.
Therefore, you should cut off that limb where the decay has reached, so that the afflicted person may be saved from death or a great calamity, which is greater than the loss of the limb.  A sign that this has happened is that the limb will turn black, as if fire has burned it.
Or it may decay after turning black, until that decay spreads to the limb adjacent and takes hold of the entire body.  So hasten to cut it off.  Likewise, if the cause of decay is from the sting of some venomous creature, like the sea scorpion, snake, or centipede, and the like.
If the decay or the sting is in the tip of the finger, cut off the finger, and do not delay the decay from spreading to the hand. Likewise, if it has taken hold of the hand, cut it off at the base of the forearm, and do not delay the decay from spreading until it affects the arm's forearm.
Likewise, if it has taken hold of the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow, at the joint itself.  If the decay spreads and you see it affecting the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder at all, for that would mean the patient's death. Use other treatment, to the best of your ability.
Likewise, do the same for the leg. If the decay affects the toe, cut off the toe at one of the phalanges. If it affects the foot, cut off the entire leg. If it ascends to the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
If the decay has reached above the knee, there is no remedy except to leave it and allow the patient to succumb to death. The method for cutting or dissecting a limb is to tie a bandage at the lower part of the place you want to cut and tie another bandage above the place.
One attendant stretches the lower bandage downward, and another stretches the upper bandage upward. You yourself, using a wide scalpel, expose all the flesh between the bandages. Then cut or dissect.  You should place linen cloths on all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the correct location and cause the patient excessive pain and a hot swelling.  If bleeding occurs during your work, quickly cauterize the area or apply some hemostatic agent, then return to your treatment until it is complete.
Then bandage the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals.  I tell you of a similar case that occurred to a man on his leg:  a blackening accompanied by a burning sensation like fire.
This decay first appeared on his finger, and eventually affected the entire limb.  The man, experiencing severe pain and burning, cut off the affected limb at the joint, and recovered.
After a long time, the same decay reappeared on his index finger.  He came to me, and I applied remedies to his hand, after cleaning his body.  The decay did not stop, and continued to spread to the other finger.
The decay spread to the entire hand, and he asked me to amputate it.  I refused, hoping to stop the decay, and fearing that amputation would lead to death since the man was close to death.  When he despaired of me, he returned to his own country.
I was later informed that he eventually amputated his entire hand and recovered.  I have related this story to assist in cases of similar disease and to provide a model for treatment.
Eighty-eighth Chapter.
In treating abscesses and how to inject medicines into them.
If a tumor develops in some fleshy organ and its duration continues until it accumulates pus, then bursts or breaks open and releases all the accumulated pus, leaving the area empty as if it were a vessel.
The skin over it, like a torn cloth, has become thin, and the damage was not severe enough to affect the bone, nerve, or ligament.  Therefore, it deserves to be called an abscess, and not a fistula, unless its duration extends so that the corruption affects one of these organs.
In that case, it is called a fistula or ulcer, and its treatment is to cut away all that skin, as it circles around, especially if it has become thin and like a rag, and you are certain that it does not adhere to the area due to its corruption.
If you hope that the skin will adhere and the corruption has not reached that extent, and there is thick flesh in the skin, then treat it by injection.  Look at the abscess; if it is large and the pus it emits has a foul odor, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
Which is vinegar, oil, honey, and copperas in equal parts collected in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine thickens and reaches the consistency of honey. Then take what you need of it, thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess, compress its opening, and leave the medicine in it for about two hours.
Then remove it by pressing. Do this for several days until the abscess is cleansed and the foul odor is gone.  You can also inject it with ash water if you don't have this medicine.  This is done by taking the ash of grapevine wood or oak wood and pouring water over it.
Then filter it and inject it until you are certain that the abscess has been washed clean. If the patient is not responding to Egyptian medicine or ash water, inject it with water and honey mixed with some ground copperas.
Or inject it with honey and drink mixed together, because honey cleanses and washes, and the drink adheres to the abscess, especially if the drink has a quality of tightening and drying.  If you do this repeatedly and are certain that the abscess's corruption is gone, inject it with something to promote flesh growth.
For example, take some of the palm ointment and dissolve it in rose oil and a astringent drink, or inject it with some of the other medications and ointments whose properties we have described in the article on ointments.  If the abscess opening is narrow and does not allow the injection tube to fit,
Then widen it slightly with iron, or place a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or sirkon ointment inside to make it wider.  Likewise, if the opening is wide, stitch its edges together, leaving only enough space for the injection tube to enter without extra room.
Also, if its opening, from which pus is dripping, is elevated, make a cut at the lowest point so that the pus drains downwards, because if the pus is retained in the depths of the abscess, it prevents the flesh from growing there.
If you cannot cut the abscess towards the bottom as desired, then consider positioning the limb so that the pus drains easily, as best you can, and to ensure that no pus is retained at all.
As for the adhesive medications to be applied to the abscess from the outside and how to apply them: take one of the adhesive ointments suitable for your needs, spread it on a linen cloth, making the cloth large enough to cover the entire abscess. Then, with scissors, cut an opening slightly larger than the abscess's opening opposite the mouth of the abscess.  Then, spread a soft ointment on another cloth sized to fit the opening, placing it over the opening, keeping it a distance from the mouth of the abscess to allow easy drainage. Do not remove the larger cloth until later.
For several days.  As for the one on the abscess's opening, it is the one to remove at all times to allow the accumulated pus to drain.  So you can determine whether the pus draining is a lot or a little, or ripe or not ripe.
And also, check the abscess itself: does the patient feel pain there, or not? Is there swelling, or not?  If you continue treatment this way and see that the material coming out from the abscess opening is little.
Know then that the abscess has adhered or is close to adhering.  In that case, treat it again tomorrow or on the third day.  When you have treated the wound, replace the cloths and renew the ointment. If after a long time, thin pus comes out of the abscess, do not despair of its adhesion.
For its healing is near, because some people have slow tissue growth in their wounds, while others have rapid growth. If pus that is not ripe comes from the abscess after many days, know that the abscess has not adhered. If the abscess adhesion is delayed.
Then increase the drying medication.  Let the medications be moist in texture and dry in strength, like a Palestinian ointment, if more colocynth has been added.  The most effective treatment is using Palestinian ointment.
It is treated by applying rose oil and sprinkling with moderate, viscous, old wine, then mixing it with the wine and applying it.  A similar action can be taken if Palestinian ointment is not available.  Honey, if boiled until it thickens, may be used or you can take extracts of mastic, frankincense and kender, grind them all, and sprinkle them on honey over heat. Then apply it to a cloth and bandage it to the abscess, or apply the boiled, thickened honey, sprinkle the herbs on it, and bandage it with cloth.
In such cases, long-stalked zeraun, the root of the asmanjūn lily, kresna flour, and rue, either alone or mixed together, are ground, sifted, and sprinkled onto the described honey, and then applied.  If the abscess persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption within it.
If it has affected the bones, or other hard tissues or ligaments, treat it with the treatment of fistula as previously described.
Eighty-ninth Chapter.
On the treatment of the inflamed finger, the injured fingernail, the amputation of superfluous fingers, and the separation of fused fingers.
Daḥs is excess flesh that grows beneath the fingernail of the hand or foot, and possibly on other fingers.  If it persists and treatment is neglected, it swells into a hot tumor.
Or it may corrupt and fester, even consuming the base of the nail, and possibly destroying it entirely.  The corruption may even reach the bone, producing a foul odor, widening the fingertip, and making it dark-colored.  If you treat it as described in the section and your treatment is ineffective,
then you should cut with an instrument all the excess growth from the nail, and subsequently cauterize the wound.  Cauterization is very effective in this case.  But if the bone is intact and the nail as well,
and if the nail's edge has pressed the flesh inward, causing it to be pierced and irritated, then you should place a thin strip of material under the edge of the nail that is piercing the flesh, and lift it upward, and carefully cut away that flesh.
and apply to the remaining flesh the corrosive, burning medicines until it all disappears, then treat it with ointments until it heals.  But if the corruption has affected the bone, then it is necessary to cut away and remove that bone, for it will not heal at all while there is diseased bone present.
If you see decay and corruption spreading in the finger, amputate it at one of the phalanges, as previously mentioned, and then treat it until it heals.  If the nail is struck or wounded, and severe pain develops, you should first lance the afflicted area.
Then split the nail with a sharp scalpel, making an oblique split from top to bottom, and be careful not to reach the flesh beneath the nail with the split, for that would cause the afflicted area intense pain and become a cause for excess flesh to grow in that spot. Then treat the area with whatever relieves the pain.
As for the extra finger that develops in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or some of it may be bone, or it may have a nail. Some of it may grow from the root of a joint of some of the fingers.
Or it may grow on some of the finger's phalanges.  Those that grow on some of the finger's phalanges do not move; those that grow at the finger joint may move.  If it is purely fleshy, cutting it is easy.  Cut it at its root with a wide scalpel.
However, if it grows from the joint's origin, treating it is difficult.  Avoid cutting it.  If it grows on the finger at one of the phalanges, first cut the flesh in a circular motion towards the bone, then expose the bone using one of the appropriate chisels for that.
Then treat it until it heals.  As for the adhesion that sometimes occurs between fingers, it frequently happens, either present at birth or arising from a healed wound, burn, or similar injury.  It is necessary to split that adhesion.
So that the fingers return to their natural position.  Then place between them a compress or a small opening soaked in rose oil, to prevent rapid adhesion, and separate them, or place a thin sheet of lead between them until the area heals properly.
Likewise, if adhesion occurs between a finger and the palm, split that adhesion according to what seems appropriate and beneficial for the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On cutting the ligatures.
Ligatures are twisted, thick veins filled with black, viscous fluid that occur in most parts of the body, but are most common in the legs, especially in the legs of those who wear tight shoes, carriers, and porters.  First, it is necessary to use vigorous rubbing of the body to remove the black fluid repeatedly.
Then, lance the affected area with a lancet.  Treatment with iron comes in two forms: one in which the vein is split and the black blood is removed; the other in which the vein is cut completely and all the fluid is removed.  As for the splitting, first, soak the leg in warm water.
Until the thick, murky blood dissolves.  Then, wrap the patient's leg from above the thigh to below the knee with a bandage.  Then split the vein in one, two, or three wide incisions.
Then, drain the black blood from the lower part of the leg to the top, and from the top to the bottom, until you have removed an amount of blood you deem sufficient or what the patient's strength allows.  Then, tie it off and instruct the patient to avoid foods that generate black fluid.  Repeat the emesis and phlebotomy.
When the veins become filled and this harms the patient, as for cutting them, it should be done in this way: shave the patient's leg if it has much hair, then take them to the bath and soak the leg in hot water until it turns red and sweats, or they undergo strenuous exercise.
If a bath is not available to warm the limb, then cut the skin along the vein, either near the knee or below at the heel.  Then, open the skin with hooks and pull the vein from all sides until it is visible.
Upon its appearance, you will see it is red and taut.  Once freed from the skin, it also appears like a tendon. Then, insert a probe beneath it until it rises and comes out from the skin.  Fasten it with a smooth, blunt hook. Then, make another incision close to that first incision, about the width of three fingers.
Then, peel the skin away from the vein until it is exposed.  Then, lift it with the probe as you did before and secure it with another hook, as you did initially.  Then, make another incision, or several incisions if necessary.
Then, cut it at the end of the incision near the heel, pull and dissect it until it emerges from the second incision.  Then, pull it to the incision above it, and do this until you pull it through the third incision, the highest of all the incisions, until all of it is out.  Then, cut it.
If pulling and dissecting do not work, insert a needle with a strong, doubled thread, tie it, and pull. Insert the probe underneath and twist your hand in every direction until it comes out, ensuring the thread does not break. If it breaks, dissecting will be very difficult.
This can cause harm to the patient.  Once you have dissected it completely, apply wool soaked in a drink and rose oil or oil to the wound site and treat it until it heals. If the varicose vein is twisted and has bends with turns in different directions...
If it is not straight, as we said, it is necessary to incise it at every bend and twisting point. Then, suspend it with hooks until you dissect it completely. Be careful when incising it not to cut or injure the vein, as this will make dissecting it difficult, so conserve your effort.
Image of the incision used for varicose veins.
Image of the blind hook.
It should not have a bend like other hooks, nor should the tip be sharp to avoid injuring the vein.  It should be thick and smooth in its bend, because if it is thin, its thinness will cut the vein. Instead, it should have the advantage of thickness, as we said.
Chapter Ninety-one.
In the case of a superficial vein.
This vein originates in the legs in hot countries like al-Hijaz and the Arab lands, and in hot, sparsely populated countries. It may also originate in other parts of the body besides the legs, arising from an infection occurring beneath the skin.
As it occurs within the bodies of snakes and worms, and abscesses and the worms that develop between the skin and flesh, and a sign of the beginning of this vein's formation is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg, then the area swells, and then the vein begins to emerge from the site of the swelling.
As if it were the root of a plant or an animal, and once its tip appears, a small piece of lead weighing one to two dirhams should be wrapped around it, tied, and the lead left hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it outward, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it grows too much, cut off some of it and wrap the remainder, but do not cut it from its origin until all of it emerges, because if you cut it, it will contract and enter the flesh, causing a tumor, an infection, and a foul ulcer in the area.
Therefore, it should be gradually treated and pulled little by little until it emerges completely from its end and nothing of it remains in the body. In some people, this vein may emerge five or ten cubits long.
I have heard that it has emerged from a man twenty cubits long. If it breaks during your treatment, insert a reed into its opening, and press it tightly against the body for a long time until all the matter within it is emptied. Then try to heal the area with medicines for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors.  This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or the leg itself, producing numerous openings and a branch emerging from each opening. Treat it as described in the section and as previously mentioned.
Chapter Ninety-two.
Concerning the abscess on the worm produced beneath the tendon, and called the "cattle disease.
This disease is called "cattle disease" in some of our countries, because it often affects cattle. It is a single small worm that develops between the skin and flesh and crawls throughout the body, both upwards and downwards.
It is perceptible to the touch as it crawls from one limb to another, until it pierces the skin in a place, emerges, and forms from the putrefaction of certain humours, as occurs with worms, snakes, and the "gourd fever" in the abdomen.
Its danger lies in its ability to crawl within the body and ascend to the head, reaching the eye, potentially causing it to rupture and exit, thus damaging the eye. This is a common occurrence.  Therefore, if one seeks to treat and remove it, it must be done when it is crawling and perceptible to the touch.
Therefore, it is necessary to bind firmly above and below the worm with a strong bandage, then to incise it and remove it.  If it has burrowed into the flesh and cannot be found, then apply cautery with fire to the area until it is burned.  The greatest danger is its potential to damage the eye, as we have mentioned.
If you see it has reached the head near the eye, bind firmly below it on the forehead, then incise and remove it.  The patient should maintain the cleansing of his body with laxatives for the putrid and bad humours, and should avoid foods that generate putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-three.
On the incision of the disease known as the "naفر".
This disease, called the "naفر" in our country, is a pain that appears in some members, then spreads from one member to another. I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was summoned to a woman in distress in one of the deserts.  She uncovered her arm, and I saw a slight swelling in the vein of the arm.  After an hour, I saw that swelling travel up the forearm, like a worm ascending to her shoulder as quickly as mercury.
If it travels from one place to another, the pain subsides from that location and settles in the shoulder.  Then, after an hour, it spread throughout the rest of the body, even to the other arm.  She recounted that it seemed to circulate her entire body, as I observed.
I was amazed by the speed of its transfer from one limb to another.  Before this, I had only heard of this disease affecting different parts of the body, but I had not personally witnessed it in such a manner as I did with this woman.
I surmised that this was because the woman was a desert dweller, with a lean body and exposed veins.  This is why this moving sensation was perceptible.  It should not appear in this manner in those of affluent society with soft bodies, hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it, and the afflicted person feels the pain visibly as described, then tightly bind above and below it with haste, and lance it until the accumulated wind/malignancy is expelled.
If you do not see it with your own eyes, treat it by shaking the body and dispersing the winds, such as the stink bean and the scabieous bean, and similar remedies.
Chapter Ninety-Four.
In the extraction of arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and the locations in the body where they lodge.  Differences in type include large and small arrows, hollow and solid glass arrows, and those with three or four angles.
Some have tongues, others have splinters.  As for the types based on the organs they enter, they fall into two categories:  either the organs are major hollow organs, such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. If an arrow lodges in one of these organs, and signs of death, which I will describe to you later, appear, then you should avoid extracting the arrow, for death will befall the patient in most cases. If these bad signs do not appear and the arrow is not deeply embedded in the organ, extract it and treat the wound.  Signs of a blow to the brain, where the arrow has penetrated the bone and wounded the meninges, include a severe headache, dizziness, and drowsiness.
If these bad signs do not appear and the arrow is not deeply embedded in the organ, extract it and treat the wound.  Signs of a blow to the brain, where the arrow has penetrated the bone and wounded the meninges, include a severe headache, dizziness, and drowsiness. Redness in the eyes and tongue, spasms, confusion, vomiting, and possibly bleeding from the nostrils or ears.  Speech and voice may be lost, and a whitish, porridge-like moisture may emerge from the wound, accompanied by a watery, fleshy discharge.
If these signs appear, refrain from treating the patient and extracting the arrow, if it has not yet been removed.  As for signs of an arrow striking the heart, if it is near the left breast, and felt as if it had penetrated something solid, not hollow.
The arrow might pulsate like a heartbeat, and black blood might flow from the wound, followed by cold extremities, cold sweat, and fainting. Know then that death is inevitable.  A sign of an arrow wounding the lung is the discharge of frothy blood from the wound.
The blood vessels near the neck swell, the patient's complexion changes, and he breathes heavily, seeking to inhale cold air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm in the chest, it will be near the smaller ribs, and breathing will be labored, accompanied by severe pain.
He sighs and all the shoulder muscles move.  If the arrow strikes the liver, intense pain follows, and blood resembling the liver in its redness emerges from the wound. If the arrow strikes the stomach, undigested food might emerge from the wound.
Its condition is evident.  If the arrow strikes the abdomen, penetrates it, and feces, or intestinal contents, emerge from the wound, there is no hope for treatment or arrow removal.  If the arrow strikes the bladder and urine emerges,
If something protrudes from the wound and the patient's pain intensifies, know that he is doomed.  As for other organs, like the face, eyes, throat, shoulder, arm, spine, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, they are often unharmed.
Unless the arrow has struck an artery or nerve, and unless the arrow is poisoned, I will describe some of what I have observed regarding these arrows for you to understand your treatment. An arrow struck a man in the bridge of his nose near the base of his eye.
I extracted it from the other side, beneath the earlobe, and he recovered without any eye damage.  I also extracted another arrow for a Jew who had been struck in the earlobe below the lower eyelid. The arrow had disappeared, only its small tip, which adhered to the shaft, remained.
It was a large arrow from a composite bow, with square, smooth iron, and no barbs. The Jew recovered without any eye injury. I also extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and it was an Arabic arrow.
It had two barbs, and I split it open between the tonsils.  It had lodged deep in his throat, and I gently worked it out.  The Christian recovered and healed. I also extracted another arrow from a man's abdomen, and we judged that he would die from it.
After about thirty days, or thereabouts, and no change in his condition, I split the arrow, worked it out, and he recovered without further injury. I also saw a man with an arrow lodged in his back, and the wound had healed over it.
Then, after seven years, the arrow emerged from the base of his thigh. I saw a woman with an arrow in her abdomen, where the wound had healed over. The arrow remained, and there was no change in her condition, nor did it interfere with her usual activities.
I saw another man with an arrow in his face, where the wound had healed over.  He experienced little pain.  Many similar cases exist. I extracted an arrow from the nose of one of the sultan's officers, which had lodged slightly to the right side of his nose.
The arrowhead disappeared completely.  I was called to treat him three days after the arrow struck. I found the arrow wound very narrow.  I probed it with a slender instrument but could not feel it.  He was experiencing a prickling sensation and pain under his ear, on the right side.
I hoped that this prickling sensation was from the arrowhead. I applied a dressing with a drawing and ripening effect, hoping that the area would swell and reveal the arrowhead's location so I could extract it.  However, no sign appeared to indicate the arrow had reached that spot.
I continued to apply the dressing for many days, but nothing happened.  The wound healed over.  The patient remained hopeless for its removal for a long time until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose.  He informed me of this, so I applied a strong, corrosive medication to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it.  I felt the slender arrowhead, which had become embedded in the nasal bone. Then, I continued to use the strong medication to enlarge the wound further until the arrowhead was clearly visible.  This took approximately four months.
Then, when the wound had widened and I could insert the forceps, I pulled and manipulated it, but it wouldn't come out.  I continued to use various instruments until one day I grasped it securely with strong forceps, as will be illustrated in the latter part of this chapter.
The physicians had determined that the cartilage of his nose could not be repaired, but I repaired it. The wound healed, and the patient recovered completely, without any further discomfort.  I am informing you of this method of removing certain arrows so that it serves as a model and example for what I have not yet described.
For the parts and details of this craft cannot be grasped through description, nor encompassed by a book. Instead, the skillful artisan compares the little with the much, the present with the absent, and invents a new technique and a new tool in the face of unusual cases.
If I proceed with this craft, I say that arrows are extracted from the affected body parts in two ways: either by pulling from the point of entry, or from the opposite side, the same way they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding into fleshy tissue, in which case it is pulled out, or if it does not come out immediately, you should leave it for a few days until the surrounding flesh decays, making it easier to pull out and remove it.  Likewise, if it is lodged in bone.
If it does not come out, leave it for a few days and try pulling and moving it daily.  If it does not come out after a few days, you should pierce around the arrow in the same bone from every side with a fine awl until the space around the arrow is widened, then pull it out.
If the arrow is lodged in the skull, having penetrated one of the brain cavities, and some of the symptoms mentioned previously have appeared, refrain from pulling the arrow out and leave it until its condition improves after a few days.
Persist in pulling out the arrow. If it is deeply embedded and cannot be pulled out, use the awls around the arrow as described, then treat the wound until it heals.  If the arrow has become hidden in a part of the body, If it is hidden from sight, probe for it. If you feel it, pull it out using instruments suitable for extraction. If you cannot, because the wound is narrow and the arrow is deep, and there is no bone, nerve or blood vessel in the way,
make a slit to widen the wound and gain access to the arrow so you can remove it. If it has two protrusions, grasp them and remove the flesh entrapped in them in every way possible. If you cannot remove the flesh by breaking or twisting the protrusions, persist until you can extract it.
When attempting to remove the arrow at any location, use your hands and tongs in every direction to extract it. Be extremely gentle so the arrow does not break, making its removal more difficult. If you cannot remove it after some time,
leave it for a few days until the surrounding flesh rots. Then try again, as it will be easier. If bleeding occurs, use the treatments described in the appropriate section.  Exercise care to avoid cutting a vein, nerve or tendon, and use skill.
Use every means possible to remove the arrow gently, carefully, and firmly, as described.  When pulling the arrow out, the patient's body position should be the same as when the arrow was lodged in it; this is optimal.
If you cannot do that, use any possible methods.  As for the arrow coming out from the opposite side, either something will have protruded outward, or you will find the arrowhead near the surface of the skin and see it protruding.
Cut around it, making the incision as large as the tongs can reach, then pull it out.  It will be easier to remove. If it is lodged in bone, twist your hands in a circular motion until the arrow affects the bone and makes space for itself, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for a few days, then try again until it comes out. If the arrowhead is still attached, push it out with it. If the arrowhead has fallen off and you want to use a pushing method, insert the hollow instrument into the arrow's shaft, so that its hollow part is inside the arrowhead, then push it out with it.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out with an instrument that fits inside the hollow part; the arrow will come out easily. If the arrow is poisoned, you should cauterize all the flesh affected by the poison, if possible, then treat it with an appropriate remedy.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or side, and is close enough to be felt with a probe, and you can make an incision, cut around it, taking care not to sever any veins or nerves, and remove it.
Then suture the wound if necessary, then treat it until it heals.  Image of the tongs used for extracting arrows:
Its tips are like the beak of a bird, crafted like a file, so that when it grasps an arrow or something, it does not let go.  There are large, small, and medium-sized types, depending on the size of the arrow, the size of the wound.
Image of the hollow cannon:.
Image of the solid cannon:.
This one is solid at the tip, like a rod, to easily enter the hollow arrow and push it out.
Chapter Ninety-five.
