In the opening of veins.
The veins customarily opened in the body are thirty.  Sixteen of these are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the *hassisin*; and the two veins on the temples, visible; and the two veins at the corners of the eyes, known as the *nazirin*.
And the vein rising in the middle of the forehead, the vein at the tip of the nose, and the two jugular veins in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lip, and these veins known as the *jayhark*, and the two veins under the tongue.
And as for the veins that are opened in the arm and hand, there are five veins. One of them is the *qayfal*, which is on the outer side, and the common people call it the vein of the head. The *akhal*, which is the middle vein, is composed of a branch of the *basliq* and a branch of the *qayfal*; and the common people call it the body vein.
And the *basliq*, which is situated on the inner side, is also called the axillary; and the common people call it the abdominal vein, and the arm cord, which is situated on the elbow, and to which it adheres, and it is the one that appears clearly above the thumb; and the *asilim*.
It is the vein between the little finger and the ring finger, with two branches.  In the leg and foot there are three veins: one is under the bend of the knee, on the outer side; the second is the *safin*, located at the heel, on the inner side; and the vein of the women, located at the heel, on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to them. As for the two veins behind the ears, their opening is beneficial for chronic colds, migraines, shingles, and chronic, foul head sores; and the method of opening them, as I describe.
This is done by shaving the patient's head and rubbing its back in the area of the veins with a rough cloth, rubbing well. Then the patient's neck is constricted with a turban until the veins appear behind the ears in the lower parts of the head.  Then probe them with your finger, and where you feel a pulse beneath your finger, mark that spot with ink.
Then take a scalpel, the one known as the *nushal*, and insert it under the vein into the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upward and sever the vein with the skin, cutting it cleanly, and the length of the cut should be the size of two clenched fingers.
Or similar, and release the amount of blood desired. Then tie them with ligatures and leave them until healed.  In the beginning of the book, it was mentioned to cut and cauterize them.  As for the two arteries in the temples, their opening is beneficial for chronic migraines, severe headaches, persistent eye inflammation, and severe, persistent eye discharge.
The method of opening them, as I describe to you, is to bind the patient's neck with a turban until the veins clearly appear and their pulse is clearly felt beneath your finger.  At that point, mark it with ink. Then lift the skin above the vein with your index finger.
Insert the *nushal* scalpel from below, lift the vein upward and sever it as you did with the other veins. Release the amount of blood needed. Then, release the patient's neck constriction and place your finger on the vein for a while.
Then place a piece of cloth and a ligature on it, tie securely from above, and leave it until it heals.  These were previously mentioned, cut, and released in the beginning of the book.  As for opening the forehead vein, its benefit, after opening the cephalic vein, is for chronic facial ailments like ringworm, sores, and inflamed redness.
The method of opening it, as I describe to you, is to bind the patient's neck with a turban until the vein appears. Then take the instrument called the "fأس" (axe), and this is its image:.
Place the pointed end of the axe on the same vein and strike it from above with a spatula or similar object. Allow the blood to flow to the desired amount. Then, release the patient's neck constriction and tie it tightly until it heals.
It may be opened with a broad scalpel, though it should not be as sharp as other scalpels, but rather slightly broad at the tip.  Open it by twisting, because the bone is nearby, so the scalpel might break if it is thin.
As for the veins located near the eye sockets, their benefit is for eye ailments like scabies, redness, and abscesses, and facial illnesses.  The method of opening them is for the patient to tighten their neck with a turban, then you open them while standing over their head.  Open them twisting slightly lengthwise with a small, somewhat broad scalpel.
If the area has little flesh, the scalpel might break if it is thin.  Then, draw the amount of blood you need and place a bandage over them.  Tie them for one night, then release them.  As for opening the nasal vein, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
Severe headaches and facial ailments like the red swelling that appears on the nose, especially if chronic, are treated.  The method of opening it is to have the patient tighten their neck, then grasp their nose with your left hand and take a long, thin scalpel.
Insert it into the middle of the nostril itself between the nasal passages in a straight line, because the vein is not visible there.  The blood will appear immediately.  You should insert the scalpel a little deeper, draw the amount of blood you need, and then bind it for a night, as it will clot quickly.
As for the jugular veins, opening them is beneficial for shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, and melancholic diseases that appear on the skin's surface, such as black vitiligo, scabies, and bad ulcers and cankers.  The method of opening them is for the patient to tighten their neck beneath them with a bandage, and the surgeon stands over the patient's head.
The patient sits on a chair, then open the vein lengthwise with a somewhat broad incision.  Then draw the moderate amount of blood, or as much as is needed, as you see.  Then do the same to the other vein, and then release the bandage.
The two veins are tightened moderately so the patient does not suffocate, and are left until the next day, when the wound will heal.  As for the veins of the neck, opening them after opening the temporal veins is beneficial for sores in the mouth, gum disease, bad ulcers, cracked lips, and bad sores.
Those located in and around the nose.  The method of opening them is to have the patient sit in front of you, tighten their neck with a turban, and then turn their lips, looking for the two veins: one on the right side of the lip, the other on the left, and distinguish them by their blackness.
Also, there are thin black veins around them.  Cut these completely off.  If it is unclear and you do not know which one it is, aim to cut the largest and most obvious one.  Do the same for the veins on the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, opening them after opening the temporal veins is beneficial for obstructions in the throat from diseases of the tongue and mouth diseases.  The method of opening them is to have the patient sit before you facing the sun, lift their tongue, and look under the tongue on one side for a vein, and on the other side for a vein. Their color is dark.
Open them, but be careful not to cut them deeply, because there are arteries beneath them, and bleeding might occur from those arteries.  The three veins that are opened at the elbow are those customarily opened in people.  Opening them can be done in two ways.
Either by piercing with a wide, fragrant or olive-shaped lancet, or by cutting with a knife-shaped lancet, which is the incision [and this is its image].  Image of the wide, fragrant lancet:.
It should be wide, as you see, to be suitable for opening the hollow, full, protruding, visible, thick veins that contain thick, dark blood. This is the image of the olive-shaped lancet:.
This lancet is narrower and has a thinner tip, suitable for opening thin veins containing thin, yellowish blood. This is the image of the incision lancet:.
This incision lancet, which is suitable for cutting, comes in various widths and thicknesses, depending on the width or narrowness of the veins.  It can be used as a model for other similar tools.  It is well known among craftsmen.
Regarding the jugular vein, which is one of these three veins, its benefit from incision is that it draws blood from the ailments that are beneath the throat and neck, and from those in the chest and abdomen. The surgeon, when performing the incision, must be cautious, and remain alert to the area beneath, for if an artery lies underneath, an error in piercing the vein will cut the artery.
This will cause a hemorrhage, therefore the incision should not be made with a pointed lancet, but rather a cut with the incision lancet. If the jugular vein is not clearly visible, it should be avoided and the surgeon should turn to another vein, or look for a branch of it.
Or, the arm vein can be opened, as it is clear, and incised with the incision lancet, as we have said.  If you wish to open the jugular vein itself, then before tightening the arm, you must feel the spot to find the pulse location, then mark it with ink.
Then, the arm is tied and the vein is incised, in a deflected manner with the incision lancet as we said, and make sure the blow is far from the artery.  When you see a puff in the spot you marked with ink, after tightening the bandage, then that puff is the swelling of the artery.  Avoid it.
If you see the blood during the incision jerking like the urine of a child, and the blood is thin and red, know that it is artery blood. In that case, immediately place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger. If the blood stops, often it will stop, so tighten the arm.
Leave it, and warn the patient not to neglect it, and let it be on the neck, and do not move it for days until it heals. If the bleeding does not stop, and you are overwhelmed and have no medicine available, sever the artery if it appears; then its ends will contract, and the bleeding will stop.
Or, take a pistachio shell, split it, and take one half. Tightly bind it to the vein with bandages and splints until the next day. If the bleeding stops, otherwise, treat it with the previously mentioned methods of applying cauterizing agents to stop the bleeding. Stopping the bleeding is not usually difficult.
Because of the small size of the wound and the firmness of the bandage on the arm, understand this.  As for the dark vein, the benefit of incising it is to draw blood from the top of the head and the bottom of the body, because it is composed of a branch from the basilic vein and a branch from the cephalic vein, as we said.
The surgeon should be careful in his incisions, for beneath them are nerves. If the lancet goes too deep and strikes a nerve, numbness occurs, difficult to cure, and perhaps it never heals. This nerve is often apparent to the touch.
If it is hidden in some people and is delicate, so that it cannot be seen, then one should make an incision with a quick, light cut to avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, then cut the vein lengthwise.  As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of incising it is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye ailments.  Especially if you wish to incise this vein, use a smooth lancet or a broad lancet, for it is the safest of all veins, because there is neither an artery nor a nerve beneath it.
However, when making the incision, you should avoid hitting the head of the muscle with the lancet, and instead seek the soft area.  If it is not struck in the first attempt, it is not harmful to repeat the incision multiple times. However, in some people, it might swell if not incised on the first attempt.
But that swelling does no harm.  As for how to make the incision, its symptoms, and what should be done to treat it, first you should know that bloodletting is used either to maintain health.
Or it is used for illnesses.  The kind used to maintain health and prevent illness should be done on one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the cephalic, the basilic, and the median cubital vein.  It should be done in the early spring when signs of fullness appear.
The bloodletting should be performed on a Sunday or Tuesday, after three hours of daylight have passed.  As for bloodletting used for illnesses, it does not have a fixed time, but rather whenever needed and required, whether at night or during the day.
At every hour and at every time.  Boys should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should elderly men who have exceeded sixty years of age.  If someone intends to perform bloodletting for any reason.
He should cleanse his bowels before bloodletting with a gentle enema if there is much accumulated phlegm, so that foul matter from the bowels does not be drawn into the veins during the bloodletting and harm the vital organs.  The apprehensive, the drunk, and the intoxicated should not be bled until this condition has passed.
Furthermore, bloodletting should be avoided following dysentery, vomiting, influenza, excessive sexual activity, exertion, exercise, late nights, fasting, and any physical or mental activity that weakens the body.  Then one should consider thinning the humors if the blood is thick.
With food, drinks, and medications, if possible.  Then he should take a bath if nothing prevents it, or engage in some exercise to thin the blood.  He should perform the bloodletting in the middle of the day, as we have said, and strive to clear his mind of all negative psychological conditions that day.
Such as worries, anger, and fear, and from all physical ailments such as excessive fatigue and exertion, sexual activity, and the like.  Pleasant things, such as perfumes, herbs, and amusements, should be present in the consultation room, in accordance with the patient's capabilities.
Then the blood-letterer sits on a higher cushion than the patient, then extends the patient's arm and the blood-letterer rubs it with their hand two or three times, then tightens the bandage with a tourniquet and twists it two or three times, with a moderate degree of tension.
Because if the tightening is excessive, it will prevent the flow of blood, or if it is too loose, it will also prevent the flow of blood.  Then, after tightening, the patient should rub their hands together.
Until the veins swell and become visible to the touch. Then the blood-letterer should lightly lubricate the lancet with old oil, especially. Then he should place his left index finger on the vein slightly below the spot where he intends to lance it, so as not to misplace the vein and avoid the blow. For some veins are like tendons and retract during bloodletting, and some are filled with air.  When the lancet is placed on them, they sink below the lancet and deceive the blood-letterer, who does not open the vein.  If it is opened, the opening is narrow.
Therefore, the blood-letterer must be firm and cautious in all of these matters.  Then he should lower the lancet; if it opens the vein in that instance, then he should do it again, a bit below or above that spot, quickly if the area has not become swollen.
If the patient swells or becomes unwell, leave him for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage, for it may cause a hot swelling.  He should not enter a bath.  Then he can repeat the bloodletting if he wishes, if the lancet has pierced and the opening is small and the blood flow is thin.
And if you fear that the amount of blood required will not be drawn, re-insert the lancet into the same puncture, gently, in a straight line, and widen the opening slightly. Do this quickly before the area swells, as a small opening can cause swelling in many people.
If you see that it has swollen, do not re-insert the lancet at all, as it will not help. Apply some clarified oil to it, as it facilitates the flow of blood.  It is better in this location than the oil itself and any other ointments.
Use clarified oil in all your bloodletting of veins when the flow of blood is difficult.  This remedy (the *faruq* and *shakhzana*) may achieve this if applied to the area, as it will thin and dissolve thick blood.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of bloodletting, which often happens, especially for someone who has not had bloodletting before or if the vein opening was small, quickly apply a sponge soaked in warm salt water to the swelling and bind it for an hour.  It will dissolve.
This should be done after the complete bloodletting from the vein itself or from another vein.  If any dark or greenish discoloration remains at the site after a few days, it will not cause harm.  If you wish, apply some resolved (dissolved) alum and myrrh.
Or some juice of *foudanj* or similar.  A swelling and protuberance often occurs when bloodletting the basilic vein. Place your hand on it. If you find it pulsates when you gently touch it, this is a bad protuberance. Be careful not to apply any of the things we mentioned.
For it may be that arterial blood will bleed from it.  Bandage it with something that will constrict to harden the area, then treat it with other remedies until it heals.  The amount of blood that should be drawn from each person should be in proportion to their strength and the prevailing color of their blood.
If the blood is black, let it flow until it turns red.  Similarly, if you see that it is thick, let it flow until it becomes thin.  Also, if it is sharp, let it flow until its sharpness subsides.  Bloodletting should be done in one session for those who are strong and robust.
For the strong, the vein should be incised wide, and the lancet should be broad. For the weak, the opposite should be done.  The blood should be drawn in several sessions, and the incision should be narrow.  The best tool for bloodletting a vein is a curved, oblique incision, not a piercing one.
This method of bloodletting is safe from bleeding and from severing a nerve. It is better and safer than bloodletting across or lengthwise.  If fainting frequently occurs during bloodletting, one should feed the patient some bread soaked in pomegranate juice or sherbet before the procedure.
If the patient is feverish and has blood drawn three or four times, if the temperament is cold, the patient should consume bread soaked in myrrh drink, or honeyed drink seasoned with spices, or a fragrant herbal drink before bloodletting.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting and the cause is excessive blood loss, the patient should be given meat broth and a light herbal drink.  Incense with galbanum should be used, and the chest should be washed with it.  Use all the methods described in the section on fainting.
This is for the purpose of expectoration.  As for someone who wants to further relieve their arm and release their blood a second time, if the bloodletting was for a large amount of expectoration and the patient's strength is weak, the blood should be released little by little, according to the patient's strength, over several days.
As for someone who wants to relieve their arm and release their blood a second time, if their body is strong, let them do this after seven or nine hours from the first bloodletting. As for someone who wants to draw blood from their body to the opposite side of the first bloodletting site, this should be done on the second or third day.
If the patient has a lot of blood in their body, which has become heated and intense, causing a fever, blood should be drawn in one single, large amount.  The opening should be widened until fainting occurs, after having carefully considered all the conditions of bloodletting. Place your hand on their pulse while the blood is flowing to prevent death from occurring instead of fainting.  This often happens if the bloodletter is ignorant and neglectful.  This should not be done if you are seeking to release the arm and drain blood a second time.
If the vein opening closes and blood flow is difficult, do not forcefully poke or twist it, as that is very bad.  Instead, either leave it until it can be opened again, or use the scalpel blade to remove the clotted blood from the vein opening.
Or apply something dissolved in water, like salt, or a remedy like *al-taryaq al-faruq* or *al-shakhzaniya*, and gently poke the vein until blood flows. If the vein is swollen, leave it alone.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If the need for further bloodletting arises, then either open a new vein above the previous site, or open a vein in the other arm, or another vein.
For bloodletting of the arm's vein, it is done instead of the *akhl* and *basliq* if those are unavailable or hidden, because the arm vein is made up from them.  The method is that the patient should immerse their arm in warm water until the vein is heated and shows clearly, then a medium-strength bandage is placed around it.
Then open the vein with a slight twist, neither sideways nor lengthwise, and make a wide opening, doing the bloodletting slightly above the wrist joint. If blood flow is difficult, return the arm to the warm water container and let the blood flow into the water.
Until you reach your desired amount. If it is summertime, you may not need to return the arm to the warm water.  More often, bloodletting into warm water is done in winter.  This vein's bloodletting is safer than all other veins. Because there is no vein or nerve underneath it.  Bloodletting from the *asilim* vein of the right arm is beneficial for liver ailments. The procedure is to bandage or hold the wrist with your hand, after immersing the arm in warm water until the vein swells.
Because there is no vein or nerve underneath it.  Bloodletting from the *asilim* vein of the right arm is beneficial for liver ailments. The procedure is to bandage or hold the wrist with your hand, after immersing the arm in warm water until the vein swells. It becomes very apparent to the touch, then open it with a slight twist.  Even if you accidentally cut it completely, it will not harm him.  Be careful, do not use the lancet forcefully, as there is a nerve of the fingers under it, and the area is bare of flesh. Then return the arm to the warm water.
Leave it to continue flowing. If you do not return it to the warm water, the blood will clot in the opening of the vein and stop flowing.  Once you have drawn the required amount of blood, place some oil and salt on the vein to prevent rapid clotting.
Likewise, you should do this with every narrow vein.  The benefit of bloodletting from the left arm is for ailments of the spleen.  You should do the same procedure for it as you did for the second one, no matter what.  As for the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases in the lower body, like uterine ailments.
And for menstrual retention, kidney ailments, chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar illnesses.  The procedure for bloodletting it is to immerse the leg in warm water and apply massage until the veins flow. Then, compress above the joint of the leg with a bandage. The vein is located at the heel, visible near the big toe.
Many branches extend from it in the direction of the leg.  Bloodletting should be done in the widest branch of this vein, or at the heel where it joins, as this is the best and safest approach.  If you bloodlet it on the side of the leg, be careful of the nerves underneath on the side of the leg.
Make your incision on it by twisting, as if you were trying to cut it off, and the lancet should be sharp. If the blood is difficult to draw, return the leg to warm water and let the blood flow until it is emptied. If the blood-letter misses the vein the first time.
If the blood-letter misses the vein the first time, he should move the incision slightly higher.  The location is safe, there is no danger if one is careful about the nerve, as we said.  Likewise, do the same with the saphenous vein of the other leg. As for the femoral vein, its location, as we said, is at the heel on the outer side.
Its benefit is for hip pain if it is due to hot blood. The method of bloodletting is that the patient should enter a bath, and quickly, hold the leg from the hip to above the heel with four fingers, using a thin, long bandage. It will only appear then.
Once it appears, blood-letting should be performed, in any case.  Either use a twisting motion, which is better, or cut it off with a cut, or make a slit, as its location is safe.  In most people, it is very hidden; if you don't find it.
If it does not appear at all to the touch, then blood-let some of its branches, which appear on the back of the foot, near the little and ring fingers. Be careful of the nerves, and draw the amount of blood desired.
Then release the hold, place a bandage on the bloodletting site, and bandage the area.  It will quickly heal.
Chapter Ninety-Six.
On Cupping and its Application.
Cupping vessels may be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass.  Cupping is of two types: one with incision and blood extraction, and the other without incision.
And this cupping without incision is of two types.
And cupping of the nape, which are the sides of the neck on both sides; and cupping of the chin, which is under the lower jaw of the mouth; and cupping of the shoulders; and cupping of the coccyx, on the base of the spine; and cupping of the forearms, which are the middle of the arms; and cupping of the legs; and cupping of the ankles.
Cupping draws blood from the thin veins spread throughout the flesh; therefore, it does not exhaust strength like venesection.  Cupping, whether with or without fire, should not be used in any illness resulting from fullness until the entire body is emptied.
If cupping is needed, whether for illness or custom, we use it at any time, at the beginning, middle, and end of the month.
For some people, when blood increases to the point of needing cupping, they experience heaviness and headaches in their heads. Some experience fullness, redness in their face, head, and neck. Others experience itching in their face, temples, and darkening and heaviness in their eyes.  Some itch the area where they were cupped.
Some laugh excessively, some taste blood in their mouths and gums, and spit blood. Some sleep excessively, and some see blood, redness, slain people, and wounds, and the like, in their dreams.  When we see any of these things...
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we order cupping after two or three hours have passed from midday.  The benefit of a light cupping is in alleviating heaviness of the head and water dripping into the eyes.
However, this should be done after the body has discharged its contents.  This cupping may be a substitute for venesection of the temples.  Caution should be taken not to use it on those with a cold head or cold, as it will greatly harm them.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly, or those with cold diseases in the head, and those who overuse it will develop forgetfulness.
Therefore, the cupper should lower the cup slightly to avoid the onset of forgetfulness.  Cupping of the shoulder is a substitute for venesection of the temples and venesection of the veins.
Therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, cracking of the respiratory apparatus, coughing, and fullness.  The cupping of the shoulder should be raised slightly, for if it is placed too low, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
Cupping of the temples is beneficial for pains in the head, eye inflammation, migraine, strangulation, and toothache.  It is a substitute for venesection of the veins.  The cupper should be instructed not to insert the instrument too deeply, lest a vessel be cut and bleeding result.
Cupping under the chin is beneficial for sores in the mouth, gum problems, and similar mouth ailments.  It can take the place of venesection of the veins in the lips.  Cupping of the shoulders is beneficial for palpitations arising from fullness and heat.
Cupping of the forearm is beneficial for what the venesection of the three veins—the basilic, the temporal, and the cephalic—is beneficial for, because it draws blood from all those fine veins in the flesh and draws those fine veins from other thicker veins until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
The cupper should be instructed not to press too hard with the instrument, as the area is lacking flesh and has nerves and arteries beneath.  As for a single cupping on the coccyx, it is beneficial for hemorrhoids and sores of the lower parts.
The cupper should be instructed to use a large, brass cupping vessel, because the area requires strong suction; glass cupping vessels may break, and excessive pressure is required.  Cupping of the legs diminishes fullness noticeably.
Because it draws blood from the entire body, it is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, promoting menstruation and aiding in the treatment of boils and abscesses.  It takes the place of venesection of the saphenous and popliteal veins, although it greatly exhausts the body and causes fainting in most people.  Cupping of the popliteal veins is similar in benefit to cupping of the legs.
How to apply cupping: First, place an empty cupping vessel and apply moderate suction. Do not prolong the application; rather, quickly place and quickly remove the cupping vessel to allow the humors to collect evenly in the area. Repeat and continue this process until the area is red and open.
When the redness of the blood appears, then press and repeat the suction gradually.  Then observe the condition of the bodies; in those with delicate, porous flesh, a single application is sufficient to avoid causing an ulcer.
The cupper should widen and slightly deepen the pressure, and adjust the suction with gentle pressure and movement. If the blood is thick, it should be pressed twice. The first time to open a passage for the thin, watery blood; the second to remove the thick blood.
If the blood is very murky, the pressure should be repeated a third time to achieve the goal.  In general, if one wishes to remove a small amount of blood, one application is sufficient; if one wishes to remove a large amount, one uses more applications, especially if the blood is thick.
The pressure should be deep, but the moderate limit for pressure is just the skin's depth.  What ointments and waters should be used when applying cupping, and what should the cupper be wary of?  For those with thick, hard, dry skin and narrow pores...
The areas for cupping should be treated with softening, relaxing, and dissolving ointments. In summer, for example, the ointment of Khairy, or violet, or sweet almond, or pumpkin seed oil.
In winter, ointments like narcissus, lily, chamomile, or hyacinth, and the like. If the material is thick and cold, use ointments like marjoram, plantain, frankincense, or fennel, and the like.
If the cupper has open pores and soft flesh, then ointment should be avoided.  These should wash their cupping vessels after cupping with rose water, or cold water, or elderberry water, or pumpkin water, or rosemary water, and the like.  As for those whose blood is excessively moist...
They should wash their cupping vessels with vinegar, or rue and sumac water, and the like.  As for those whose material is thick, they should wash their cupping vessels with old wine, or marjoram water, or fennel or chamomile decoction, and the like.  Cupping should be avoided in the bath, and after the bath.
Rather, it should be used one or two hours after leaving the bath.  One should not sleep after cupping.  This is what the cupper and the leaches should arrange before and after cupping.
First, one should consider whether the cupper or leacher is bilious, with a predominating tendency toward heat and inflammation.  Then, cooling things like pomegranate, dandelion, vinegar, lettuce, and squills should be taken.  Their food should consist of poultry, mutton, and game.
If their temperament is cold, they should be given honey-based drinks, or drinks of honey and  seeds, or spiced squills.  Moderate, fragrant wine, between old and new, should be taken.  A reduction in food is ordered, and their diet should consist of poultry, quails, and sparrows, as well as pigeon’s breasts.  On the day of cupping and bloodletting, drinks should be more plentiful than food.
Sometimes, some people should be given antidote, musk medicine, or shilajit before cupping or bloodletting, or after, to strengthen the main organs and thin the blood.  It should not be given to the feverish.
As for cupping without condition, those are the cuppings placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney area, and groin, because these organs cannot tolerate being conditioned.  Rather, the intent is to draw blood from one organ to another.
For example, placing leeches on the breasts in cases of epistaxis, or to remove a cold wind that has affected an organ, like placing a leech on the abdomen and navel.  This loosens the organ, warms it, and relieves the pain by dissolving the wind.
They may be placed on the kidneys if blockages or stones are present, as their strong suction might open the blockage or dislodge the stones.  Similarly, they are used on the liver and spleen when wind troubles them.  These cupping vessels can also be used empty by suction alone.
They may be used with fire, or filled with lukewarm water for cases of phlegm.  The cupping vessel, perhaps a large one, is filled with hot water alone, or with water in which certain suitable herbs have been cooked.
Then, filled, it is placed on the area, held, and removed, repeated as needed.  This is the form of cupping that is used with fire.
Its opening should be two open fingers wide, as depicted, and its depth about half a span.  Near the middle of one side, there will be a small hole, just large enough for a needle, made of thick Chinese or brass.
It should be smooth, level, and polished so that it does not injure the organ when placed on it.  In the center, a transverse piece of brass or iron is fitted to hold the candle for the fire.  This cupping vessel may be made larger or smaller, depending on the illness.
The practitioner must use discernment; cupping for children and thin individuals is different from cupping for men and those with larger bodies.  To place the cupping vessel on the body with fire, a wick is lit from linen or a small candle made of pitch and placed on the hardened rod in the center of the cupping vessel.
To ensure the fire ascends from the top to the bottom of the cupping vessel, to prevent the patient's body from burning, it is then placed on the area.  The finger is placed in the hole mentioned, so that, when the cupping vessel is held, the finger does not need to be removed.
Steam will emerge from that hole, and the cupping vessel will be drawn to the spot. Then, the wick is relit as needed.  For cupping used for dropsy, there is no hardened rod or hole.
Instead, it is used by filling with water and placing it directly onto the area.  This is its form.
The larger the cupping vessel, the more water it holds, the better.
Chapter Ninety-Seven.
On the application of leeches.
Leeches are used most often on parts of the body where cupping cannot be applied, either because they are small, like the lips and gums, or because the area is bare of flesh, like the finger and nose.
To use them, select leeches from clean, unpolluted fresh water, then leave them in fresh water for a day and a night so that they become hungry and have nothing left in their stomachs. Then, the body is first purged with bloodletting or cupping, and then the affected area is cleaned.
Until it turns red, then the leech is placed on it. If it fills up, it will fall off. If it is possible to draw the affected area with a cupping vessel, this is more beneficial. Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar, then with plenty of water, and massage and squeeze it. If bleeding continues after the leech falls off, and it is a trickle, then soak a linen cloth in cool water.
Place it over the area until the bleeding stops. If the bleeding is excessive, sprinkle powdered alum, or a similar astringent, over it until the bleeding stops, or place scraped halves of chickpeas on the area.
Leave it until the chickpeas adhere to the area, and the bleeding will stop.  If it is necessary to re-apply the leeches, do not use the same leeches if possible. If the leeches refuse to attach, then wipe the area with fresh blood.
Or, pierce the area with a needle until some blood comes out, then apply it. If it senses some blood, it will attach to the spot. If you want it to fall off, sprinkle some dates, salt, or ash on it, and it will fall off.
Chapter Three.
On Fractures.
This chapter also, from the stratagems necessary in the art of medicine, is on the setting of fractures and dislocations that occur in the bones.
Know, O my children, that this chapter is claimed by the ignorant among doctors and the common people, those who have never perused the books of the ancients on it, nor read a single word from them.
For this reason, this art of knowledge has become extinct in our country, and I have never encountered a skilled practitioner in it. Rather, I benefited from it to the extent that I benefited from it through my long study of the books of the ancients and my diligence in understanding them, until I extracted the knowledge from them. Then, I adhered to experience and practice throughout my life.
I have depicted for you in this chapter everything encompassed by my knowledge. My experience has passed over it, after I brought it closer to you, purged it of prolixity, condensed it to the utmost brevity, elucidated it to the utmost clarity, and depicted for you numerous images of the instruments used in it.
It is, indeed, from the increase of clarity, as I have done in the preceding two chapters.  There is no power except with God, the Most High, the Great.
The First Chapter.
In it are summaries and collections concerning the matter of fractured bones, which should be presented beforehand. Before we begin to describe the fractured and dislocated limbs one by one, it is necessary in the introduction to this chapter to mention summaries and sections. This will first compel you, and anyone else zealous to learn this noble craft, to understand them and grasp their true meaning.
If someone suffers a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall, one must first hasten to reduce the fracture or dislocation, or both. Unless prevented by factors such as weakness, or if the affected person is a child or an elderly person, or the weather is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then, his diet should be restricted to cold legumes, poultry, and venison.  Drinks, thick meats, overeating, and all foods that fill the veins with blood should be avoided, until the swelling subsides and there is no further anticipated effusion to the affected area.
Then, the patient should return to his original diet, as was his custom.  If the broken bone starts to knit, the patient should eat foods that are nutritious, substantial, and firm, with a thick consistency, such as barley, rice, bulgur, and ox tripe. Eggs, soft fish, and thick drinks, and similar foods, will result in faster and better healing of the fracture.  Know that fractured bones in strong men and the elderly will not likely heal to their original state.
Due to their dryness and hardness, the bones may not completely heal.  However, those bones that are extremely pliable, like the bones of young children, may heal.  Nature produces a substance around the fractured bone, similar to glue, with a thick consistency that adheres to and binds the pieces together.
Binding them together until they reach the same strength and firmness as before, so that nothing hinders their function.
For this reason, the patient's diet should consist of foods that are strong, thick, and viscous, as we have stated.  Know that fractures differ in type based on the affected limb, as a fracture of the leg differs from a fracture of the head, and a fracture of the chest differs from a fracture of the spine.
And so do all the other limbs.  We will describe each type of fracture in detail in its respective section, and the type of fracture can even vary within the same bone.
Because the fracture may be a simple crack without splintering, or it may be along the length of the bone with splinters and fragments, some detached, some not.  The fracture may be accompanied by a wound and a break in the skin, or it may be a minor fissure.
Each type requires a specific method of reduction, which will be detailed later in its appropriate place.  A fracture is recognized by its distortion, unevenness, and visibility to the touch, and by the creaking sound when the bone is gently prodded with a hand.
If there is no visible distortion, no creaking, and no pain or discomfort on palpation of the bone, there is no fracture, but rather a sprain, a minor fracture, or a minor fissure.  In such cases, the affected limb should not be moved by pulling or prodding at all.
Instead, apply the appropriate medications, which will be described later, to the affected area, then apply a gentle bandage.  Know that when a bone is fractured in two without splinters, each part has completely separated from its partner.
You should immediately attempt to realign and straighten the bone before it develops a hot swelling. If a hot swelling does develop, allow it to subside for several days before attempting realignment with the gentlest possible methods.
Know that realignment and straightening are easier in a bone that has not splintered.  Then it is bandaged as described later. If the bone has splinters, the fractured limb (arm or leg) must be stretched from both ends.
Either by hand, if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand. Position the limb on a level surface in its natural shape.  When the fractured bone segments have been stretched, carefully return the protruding parts to their proper places using all possible gentle methods.
Strive to cause no pain or suffering to the patient through your actions.  Concentrate on joining the two bone segments together in the best possible alignment.  At this time, it is necessary to touch and examine them with your hand. If you find anything out of place, correct and align it to the best of your ability.
Beware of excessive stretching and forceful manipulation, as many untrained practitioners do.  Frequently, their actions cause a hot swelling or inflammation in the limb, as I have observed repeatedly.
After alignment, meticulous work, and bandaging, keep the limb still and quiet. Warn the patient not to move it during waking hours, sleeping hours, during changes in posture, during restlessness, during bowel movements, and with all his movements to the best of his ability.  He should strive to ensure the limb's position is one that avoids pain.
If, during the limb's positioned state, he feels pain or discomfort, he should reposition the limb to a position free of pain.  Furthermore, he should ensure that his position is straight and even.
To prevent a crooked limb if it is set, the method for bandaging a fractured limb is as I describe for you. Know that broken limbs differ in their size, age, and condition.  Those that are small, such as the arm, fingers, forearm, and similar parts, ...
Thin bandages are appropriate for smaller limbs, like the arm and fingers.  For larger limbs such as the thigh, back, and chest, bandages should be firm and broad.  A wide bandage is needed to support a large limb and to apply equal pressure on all sides without any gaps.
Then, after completing the alignment, apply the appropriate plaster. First, apply a soft bandage to the fracture site. Then begin wrapping the bandage around the fracture itself, three or four times, depending on the limb's needs.  Slightly tighten the bandage, and then move it towards the upper part of the fracture.
Applying less tension than you did on the fractured area, gradually move the bandage away from the fracture, loosening the pressure little by little until you achieve a correct position. Then take another bandage and wrap it similarly over the fractured area.
Wrap it around several times, then move the wrapping to the lower part of the fracture.  Maintain the same tension and looseness as in the first wrap on the upper part.  Place soft padding or bandages between the layers to correct any misalignment of the fracture.
If there is misalignment, correct it; otherwise, do not add anything. Then wrap another bandage around it.  Finally, if there is no swelling or heat in the limb, apply firm splints to these layers.
If there is swelling or heat, apply something to soothe the swelling and reduce the inflammation. Leave it for a few days, then apply the splints.  The splints should be made of smooth, skillfully cut reed stalks or from the wood of sieves.
These [splints] can be made from pine, palm leaves, reeds, or other similar readily available materials.  The size of each splint should be identical to the one previously described.
However, the splint directly applied to the fracture itself should be slightly thicker and wider than the others.  The splint's length should be determined by the size of the fractured limb.  [Image/Diagram of the splint]
Then, apply another bandage to the splints, tightening it in the same manner as the first bandage.  Tie it securely with strong threads from above, tightening the bandage more over the fracture site. The tighter the bandage, the closer to the fracture.
The threads should be of medium thickness and be made from damp linen.  If the threads are thick, like those made from twisted linen strips, as the ignorant often do, this is a grave error.
Because this creates an uneven tension.  Thin threads are also unsuitable, as they cannot provide the required tension.  There should be at least a finger's width between splints.
If the patient is injured by the edges of the splints after proper application, place soft padding such as linen or fluffy wool underneath to prevent further injury. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound and the skin is broken, we will address this separately.
Know that not every fractured limb should be splinted from the first day.  If the limb is large, splints should not be applied until five, seven, or more days later, depending on your assurance that inflammation will not occur.
This is a description of the bandages used by the ancients to treat fractures, dislocations, and sprains.  It is a concise, common bandage that sets bones, and is suitable for most temperaments, particularly children and women, as it is not affected by heat or cold.
Take the flour dust, the residue of flour from a millstone, and knead it as it is, without sifting it with egg white. Make the dough neither thick nor thin, then dry it.
Another bandage for fractures, dislocations, and sprains.  Take ten dirhams each of mastic, ladanum, acacia, asps, myrrh, and alum; five dirhams each of tragacanth and saffron; twenty dirhams of frankincense; and twenty dirhams of Armenian or Roman clay. Grind all the ingredients and mix with frankincense water or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot. This bandage is effective, sets broken bones quickly, and is suitable for most people due to its balance.
Another bandage useful for fractures and sprains. Take ten dirhams each of myrrh, mastic, and white hyacinth; five dirhams each of tragacanth and saffron; six dirhams of acacia; and twenty dirhams of Armenian clay. Grind all thoroughly, sift, knead with water or egg white, and apply.
A bandage for dislocated joints and bones, relieving pain and treating bone throbbing and fractures. Take softened wool, dip it in vinegar and cooked oil, and apply to the affected area.  This bandage does not provide the strength of a fracture repair.
However, it is excellent for relieving hot swelling and pain. Another bandage for setting a fractured bone. Take the leaves of the wild fig and the leaves of the wild poppy, grind them together, and apply the paste to the injured area. Another short bandage is used for an impacted fracture.
If you wish to treat the remaining swelling, take equal parts of the root of hyacinth, chamomile, violet, and carob flour. Grind all and knead with plaster if the limb is not inflamed. If it is inflamed, knead with coriander water or water, and apply.
Another stronger resolving bandage, to be used when a hard swelling occurs with an impacted fracture.  Take equal parts of hyacinth root, flaxseed, fenugreek, rosemary, violet, and chamomile.
Grind all together, kneading with vinegar water, or fresh water, or plaster, depending on the heat of the limb and its cooling.  Hippocrates, however, did not mention in his book that this should be applied to a fractured limb during setting.
Except for the cerot of wax and oil alone, described as being medium in thickness and texture.  Galen, however, considered that things containing dryness with a degree of warmth, such as myrrh, mastic, and frankincense, and similar substances, should be applied to the fractured limb during the setting procedure.
A bandage for weakness and pain.  Take equal parts of dried figs, roasted chickpeas, shaved human hair or bird feathers, hyacinth, and salt. Grind, sieve, knead, and apply as a dressing.  Now, as to the amount of ligament that should remain...
Then, it should be removed.  Observe; if the patient experiences no pain, rubbing, or displacement of the fractured bone, do not remove it for many days.  However, if there is severe rubbing, distressing pain, or swelling in the area,
Then immediately remove it, and do not delay.  Remove the dressing. Then take a soft cloth or a wet sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area until the itching and pain subside. Then allow the limb to rest for an hour.
Then apply softened wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose ointment, and bind it for the night, so that the inflammation subsides, the swelling of the limb diminishes, and the pain disappears.  Then, gently reapply a light bandage; do not apply it as tightly as the initial dressing.
And treat it gently until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and swelling have completely disappeared, and the dressing and bandage are needed, then reapply them as you did initially.  If none of these symptoms remain in the limb,
then do not remove it until three, four, five, or seven days later, or even twenty days, depending on the condition of the limb, as we have stated, until the fracture is secure and the flesh begins to knit together.
Then, increase the tightness of the bandage beyond the original bandage.  Also, increase the patient's nourishment, as previously mentioned. If you see that the fracture site has dried out and wasted more than it should, know that the nourishment is not reaching it.
So loosen it with lukewarm water each time you remove it, every three days, and reduce the tightness of the bandage slightly. This action will bring nourishment to the limb and promote rapid healing.  As for the actions of ignorant people, who cause the limb to break again...
...for if it was not initially set correctly and is set crookedly, that is a mistake on their part and a great deception.  If it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their writings and practiced it.  I have seen no evidence of this from any of them at all. The correct approach is not to do this.
Chapter Two.
Concerning fractures of the head.
Fractures of the head are numerous, their shapes varied, and their causes diverse.  Some fractures occur from a sword blow, and in these, the bone may break completely, extending to the peritoneum beneath the bone. As happens with wood when struck.
This type of fracture is called a "contusional" fracture. Alternatively, the sword may sever a portion of the bone, fracturing only one side, without penetrating completely; this is called a complete fracture, and the wounds from these two types of fracture can be either large or small.
Some fractures are caused by bruising or contusion, resulting from a blow with a stone, a fall on a stone or similar object.  This type of fracture can either penetrate to the membrane beneath the bone, or remain on the surface of the bone.
The wounds from these fractures can also be large or small.  Some fractures are hidden within the bone, as subtle as a hairline crack.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a hairline fracture.  It can result from a fall or a blow from a stone or similar object, causing a portion of the bone to sink inwards, creating a concavity, just as a piece of copper would sink in if struck.
This is most often seen in the soft, bony skulls of children.  All these types of fractures may have displaced or undisplaced fragments.  We will discuss the treatment for each in its proper place.  All these types of fractures can be identified by examination.
Examine them with probes and remove any infected flesh.  The hairline fracture is identified by examining the bone, rubbing and staining it with ink; the fracture will show as black.  The treatment for the fracture first involves assessing the patient's symptoms.
If you see obvious symptoms of danger, such as vomiting bile, stretching, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, bloodshot and reddened eyes, and similar symptoms, do not approach or treat the patient.  In most cases, death is inevitable with these symptoms.
If you see symptoms that do not alarm you and hope for recovery, then begin treatment.  This is especially true if the injured person comes to you at the very moment of the injury, during the winter months.
You should strive to remove the bone by the fourteenth day, regardless of when the injury occurred.  If the injury occurred during the summer, you should remove the bone before the seventh day, to prevent the membrane under the bone from decaying and leading to the symptoms described.
If the fractured bone has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is crushing and laceration, you should cut away the crushed and lacerated portion, as I describe to you: shave the head of the injured patient.
Expose the bone in any way possible, based on the shape of the wound and the patient's tolerance. If, during the exposure of the bone, you encounter bleeding or a hot swelling, treat it as necessary.
This involves filling the area with rags soaked in rosewater and oil, and cleansing the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding ceases.  Then, proceed with the repositioning and removal of the bone, which can be done in one of two ways.
One method is to cut the bone with a narrow, gentle incision.  This is how it is done:
Then, use another incision slightly wider than the first.  This is how it is done:
Then, use yet another incision even wider than the second.  You should have a set of different incisions, some wider than others, some shorter than others, with very sharp edges.  Let them be made from Indian steel or good quality iron.
Use gentleness when striking the incision to avoid displacing the head and injuring it.  If the bone is strong and hard, you should first pierce around it using the so-called "non-dipping" awls, before using the incisions.
Because they do not penetrate beyond the skull bone, as the awl has a rounded edge below its sharp head, like a ring or small circle, preventing it from sinking in and penetrating the thickness of the bone.
You should have many of these awls, each suited to the thickness of that bone, so that you have, for every skull, an awl whose sharp point is appropriate in length and shortness to the thickness of that skull.  This is an example of three types. Of awls, large, medium, and small.
As for how to pierce around the fractured bone, place the awl on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then move the awl to another place, and maintain a distance between each hole equal to the thickness of a rod or similar.
Then cut with the pliers between each two holes, doing so with the utmost gentleness, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either by hand or with another tool you have prepared for this.
Like fine forceps and pliers.  You must be extremely careful that the awl or pliers do not touch any of the peritoneum. If you have smoothed the bone and freed it from the peritoneum, if it was attached to it, and removed it, then it must be cleaned.
Any roughness remaining on the bone is smoothed with another instrument similar to the pliers, but it must be finer and more delicate than the other pliers.  If any small bones or fragments remain, remove them gently with the tools you have.
Then treat the wound afterwards with the compresses and ointments that will be mentioned.  The other approach to this procedure is quite straightforward and free from risk.  Galen spoke of it and praised it highly.  This is what he said. First, you should begin by exposing the section of the bone where the fracture is most severe and prominent.  Once you have exposed that section, place the tip of this lentil-shaped instrument under it. This is its image.
The lentil-shaped part of it should be smooth, not cutting anything.  The pointed part of it along its sides should extend in length, as you see, so the lentil-shaped part rests against the peritoneum, and the pointed part of the instrument is on the bone.  Then strike the instrument from one side.
With a small hammer, until all the bone is broken gently, as it turns.  You are safe from injuring the membrane, nothing frightening happens, even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and timid of men, yes, even if he were asleep.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some places, scrape it off with the tip of the lentil-shaped instrument itself, and remove it gently. It will come away without harm or fear.
If the fracture of the bone did not penetrate the membrane, and the broken surface of the bone remains rough and jagged, then you should remove those rough edges and fragments with sharp instruments of different sizes, so you can use the one best suited to each location, according to what the work dictates.
The broken bone and its shape, and you should use the broadest of those instruments to scrape the bone first, and then use a finer one after that, and continue to do so until you reach the use of the finest and slimmest of them all.
As for other small, hairline fractures and delicate breaks, one should use a treatment for each, as indicated by what will lead to its repair; this is something not hidden from anyone with the slightest skill in this craft who has studied what we have written and explained concerning larger fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after removing the bone, you should take a linen cloth sized to the wound, soak it in rose oil, and place it over the wound's opening. Then take another cloth, folded or triangular. Soak it in rose water and rose oil and place it on top of the first cloth.  Do this as gently as possible so as not to weigh down the peritoneum. Then, apply a wide bandage over it, tightening it only enough to hold the wound in place.
Leave it with this treatment for a day or two until the hot swelling subsides, then remove it.  Afterwards, use remedies with a drying quality such as iris root, poppy seed, kender, and zeraوند, and similar.  Make a paste from these remedies.
Dust the wound with it in its dry form.  In general, use a remedy that cleanses without stinging.  Strive diligently during wound treatment to ensure it is clean, free of grease or dirt, and do not allow pus to accumulate in it.
Because if pus collects on the membranes of the brain, it corrupts and contaminates it, causing the patient great harm.  This can happen in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If a dark discoloration appears on its surface, and if the patient displays the symptoms we mentioned, know that he is inevitably doomed. However, if the discoloration was caused by a remedy applied and the remedy's potency was such that it caused this discoloration, .
You should take one part of honey and three parts of rose oil, mix them thoroughly, and apply the mixture to a cloth.  Place the cloth on the peritoneum.  Then treat it with appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter Three.
For the setting of a fractured nose.
Know that only the upper portions of the nose, either both or one, can break, because they are bony.  The lower portion is cartilaginous and does not break, but may be bruised, bent, or punctured.
If one of the bony portions breaks, you should insert a small finger into the nostril and straighten the fracture from the inside with your index finger and thumb on the outside, until the nose is restored to its natural shape.  Do this gently, making sure not to cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is high up on the nose and cannot be reached with a finger, it should be straightened with a blunt, slightly rounded instrument.  If the fracture is in both sides, do likewise.  He should hasten to set the fracture on the first day after it occurs.
If possible, or else after seven or ten days, when the inflammation has subsided, a thread of linen should be inserted into the nostril(s) if the fracture is on one side, or two threads if it is on both sides.
The thread should be thick enough to fill the nostril.  Some early surgeons recommended soaking the thread in clarified butter and changing it each day, but I do not agree.  Instead, the thread should be soaked in beaten egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then the thread is left in place until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens.  Goose feather tubes may be inserted into the nostril where the thread is, wrapped with soft linen.  This would make the holding of the nasal fracture stronger and to prevent the patient from being unable to breathe.
This is not essential; you may do it or not, as you wish, and use the threads instead.  If, during the treatment, the nose becomes inflamed, bandage the nose with linen soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or with a preparation of diachylon.
If no inflammation occurs, bandage the outside with fine semolina and ground kender mixed with beaten egg white, then place soft linen over it.  Do not bind the nose at all, if the nasal bones are fractured in small pieces.
Or if it splintered, then it must be separated and removed with the appropriate instrument, then the split must be sutured and treated with ointments suitable for healing and suppuration.  If a wound occurs inside the nose, it must be treated with thread and lead tubes must be used. Until it heals.
Fourth Chapter.
On setting broken lower jaw.
If the lower jaw is broken but not accompanied by a wound, and if the fracture is only from the outside, not fractured in two places and does not curve inwards, its recognition is easier.
If the fracture is in the right side, the index finger of the left hand should be inserted into the patient's mouth.  Likewise, if the fracture is in the left side, the index finger of the right hand should be inserted and gently raise the hump of the fracture from the inside outwards.
Your other hand, from outside the bone, controls its alignment.  If the jaw fracture has splintered in two places, it is necessary to use traction from both sides in a straight line until alignment is achieved. If it has occurred in the teeth, it should be trimmed.
Or, if it has separated, tie together what you expect to remain with a thread of gold, silver, or silk.  Then place a splint of qirūṭī on the broken jaw, then place a folded cloth over it, and place a large, tight bandage, or a piece of leather the length of the jaw, over the cloth. Then tie it from above as you find suitable for its closure, so it does not dislocate.  Order the patient to rest and remain quiet. Give him soft food.  If you suspect any change in shape, proceed to remove it on the third day.
Then tie it from above as you find suitable for its closure, so it does not dislocate.  Order the patient to rest and remain quiet. Give him soft food.  If you suspect any change in shape, proceed to remove it on the third day. Then repair the bandage with flour from a millstone and egg white, or semolina flour, after removing the qirūṭī. Place a soft compress on the bandage.  As long as the bandage adheres and the condition of the bone has not changed, leave it in place.
Do not remove it until the fracture heals and strengthens.  Frequently, the fracture strengthens in three weeks.  If a hot swelling appears during that time, use the remedies we have previously described to relieve it until it subsides.
And if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, observe it. If a fragment or fragments of bone have separated from the rest, carefully remove these fragments with appropriate instruments.  If the wound opening is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as needed.
Then, after removing the fragments and there is nothing left, suture the wound opening if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the appropriate ointments. And close the wound until it heals.
Fifth Chapter.
On the treatment of a fractured collarbone.
The collarbone is most often fractured from the front, near the acromion.  The fracture occurs in one of three ways: either it breaks and splinters in two without causing fragments, which is easiest to treat;
Or, the fracture may involve fragments, which is more difficult to treat; or the fracture may occur with a wound. If the fracture is without a wound, have two assistants; one to hold the arm adjacent to the fractured collarbone, the other to extend the neck to the opposite side.
Then, straighten the fracture with your fingers until its shape is correct, without any protuberances or irregularities.  If further extension is needed, a ball of linen or wool should be placed under the patient's armpit.
Adjust the bone as needed, extending and elevating the collarbone and pressing on the ball with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned.  If you cannot draw the end of the collarbone outward because it has sunk inwards, the patient should lie on their back.
A moderate-sized cushion is placed under the patient's shoulder, and the assistant pushes down on the shoulder to raise the recessed bone of the collarbone.  Then, correct and align the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the collarbone has broken off and is moving, you should carefully separate and remove the fragment. If the fragment is lodged within the bone, use one of the instruments you have prepared to cut it out. After placing the instrument that protects the diaphragm, which is made of wood or iron, underneath the collarbone—this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a handle. Its width should correspond to the size of the bone, and its length should be appropriate for your work. It should have two ends, one wider and the other narrower, as you see.
If the wound you made to remove the bone fragment is wide and you anticipate inflammation, stitch the edges of the wound together.  If the wound is small or you fear inflammation, close the wound with sutures and bandages, commensurate with the size of the incision.
If an inflammatory swelling occurs, soak the bandages in rose-water and vinegar or a drink and apply them.  If the bone is dislocated without a wound or incision, apply a bandage made of mill dust mixed with egg white to the bone. Place a soft splint on it, then if needed, place a ball under his armpit.  Then, take a very long bandage, about a span wide or so.  Wrap a folded bandage over the splint and dressing.  Then take a splint made of thin wood. Make it three fingers wide and the same in length. Then wrap it in a cloth, and wrap that cloth with the splint into the part of the bandage that covers the fracture.  Then tie the bandage around the fracture, as you would a turban. Wrap it around his neck and under his healthy armpit and under his injured armpit, and return it repeatedly to each side.  How you see that the tightening secures the fracture firmly, is something you should know.
The whole point is that the splint must not move from the broken bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day.  Whenever the ligament loosens and you see the splint has moved, correct it and tighten the bandage.  Then, have the patient sleep on his back, and place a small pillow under his armpit when he sleeps at night.
To raise his arm from his side, so the clavicle fracture rises with the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck.  Do not loosen the bandage unless a wound or swelling appears at the site within twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see reason for it. Apply pressure and leave it until the fractured clavicle is healed and strengthened.  It will become even stronger within twenty-eight days. And in some people, it may happen sooner.
Chapter Six.
On the reduction of a fractured shoulder.  The shoulder rarely fractures in its broad area; rather, its processes fracture.  If a part or the middle fractures, this is determined by palpation.  Then, according to the nature of the fracture, apply traction.
Reduce and return it to its natural shape by every means possible.  Then, apply mill dust mixed with egg white and soft plaster to the affected area. Place a folded cloth dressing over this and, finally, apply a wide splint of wood.
Make the splint as wide as the entire shoulder, or slightly wider. If there is an indentation under the splint on any part of the shoulder, smooth this indentation with soft plaster until the splint lies flat. Then, secure it from above with a long bandage.
Secure the splint firmly so it does not shift from its position.  Examine the bandage each day; if it loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it shifts.  The patient should lie on their healthy side. The shoulder will be healed in twenty days.
After one or twenty-five days, remove the bandage and you will be safe.  It is a bone that does not fear splintering or dislocation.  If a bone fragment protrudes from the bone and pierces the skin, incise over it and extract it. Do what I have described for fractured clavicles to alleviate any inflammation that might occur.
Chapter Seven.
For the setting of fractured ribs.
The chest can break in its center, though this is rare.  Its extremities, however, are more likely to fracture and splinter.  If the center breaks, symptoms include a downward tilting of the chest, intense pain, and difficulty breathing and coughing.
Sometimes blood may be coughed up, and the depression in the fractured bone will be evident and palpable.  To set it, the patient should lie on their back, with a pillow between their shoulders. Then, the patient's sides should be pressed together, and the ribs brought together with the hands from the sides, gently smoothing the fracture in every possible way.
And maintain this until the bone returns to its proper shape. Then apply a bandage and splint, placing a splint of thin, light wood, such as poplar or willow, over it, after wrapping it in a cloth. Then, gently bind the splint to the fractured bone so it doesn't shift.  Wrap the bandage in a circular motion around the back several times, tightening it firmly.  Then, check the bandage regularly, and tighten it whenever it loosens.
If the splint needs to be removed due to an irritation, pain, or swelling in the area, promptly remove it and the bandage. Correct any issue that has arisen.  Then, if you deem it necessary, reapply the bandage. And apply pressure and maintain the tension until he is healed.
Chapter Eight.
In the setting of fractured ribs, know that fractures occur in the thick portions adjacent to the spine, and their anterior extremities.  Injuries occur because they are cartilaginous.  This knowledge is not hidden to the touch when examining with the fingers.
To treat it, align the fracture with the fingers in the appropriate manner until the shape is correct. Then, bandage and apply a splint to the fractured bone if necessary.  If the rib fracture is inward, the patient will experience severe pain.
And it causes pain like that of someone with a hernia, because the bone pierces the diaphragm.  This also causes difficulty in breathing, coughing, and the expulsion of a great deal of blood. This is a difficult condition to treat, and the ancients devised many methods for it.
Some have said that the patient's diet should include foods that produce flatulence and wind, so that the abdomen becomes distended and the fracture is pushed outward.  We do not approve of this, lest it confirm the development of a hot swelling. If it has not yet occurred, it will be further encouraged and confirmed.
Some said that a cupping glass should be applied to the area, and then suctioned forcefully.  This is more in line with reason, but there is concern that the cupping glass will be drawn to the area due to its weakness.  Others said that the area should be covered with wool dipped in hot oil.
The spaces between the ribs become filled in, so that the ligament becomes even when wrapped in a circular manner.  Then, treat the patient with the same treatment as a hernia, in terms of diet and medication, if the condition is too taxing for the patient to bear. The bone was piercing the diaphragm with a painful penetration.  We feared for the patient, therefore it is necessary to incise the area, uncover the broken rib, and place under it the instrument that protects the peritoneum, which has been previously described, and to carefully remove the bone and take it out.
Then, if the wound is large, we bring together the edges of the wound by stitching and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot swelling appears during this time, quickly apply rose-oil-soaked strips to the area and treat the patient internally with something that reduces the swelling.
He should lie on the side on which he sleeps more easily until he is healed.
Chapter Nine.
On the setting of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Concerning the bones of the neck, if fracture occurs, it is uncommon.  More often, contusion occurs.  Similarly, for the vertebrae of the back.  If this happens to someone, and you wish to know if he will recover or not... If you see that his hands have become relaxed, numb, and lifeless, and he cannot move them, extend them, or close them; and if you pinch or prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and finds no pain in them, know then that he will not recover in most cases; he is doomed.
If he moves them and feels the pinching and pricking, know that the spinal cord is unharmed, and the patient will recover with treatment. If a similar condition affects the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know if he will recover or not...
Examine his legs. If you see that they too have become relaxed, and the same condition as the hands has occurred, then if he lies on his back, wind and stool come out involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach, urine comes out involuntarily. If, lying on his back, he tries to urinate and cannot, know that he is doomed.  Do not bother with treatment.  If none of this occurs, the situation is less severe.  Treatment for what has happened should focus on calming the hot inflammation.
Apply rose oil, alone or with roasted egg yolks, to the affected vertebrae three times a day, until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply one of the strengthening, dry bandages, and bind it tightly with a bandage.  Instruct him to rest and remain still.
He should only sleep on the side that does not cause pain until he recovers. If, due to the injury, a splinter or something similar has formed within the bone and healed over, it is necessary to cut through the skin and remove that piece of bone. Then, if the wound is large, bring the edges together with sutures, and treat it with healing ointments until it heals.  If the last bone of the coccyx, the tailbone, is also broken, you should insert the little finger of your left hand into the rectum.
Straighten the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible and as easily as you can, then apply a bandage and splint if needed, and bind it tightly.
If you feel a fractured splinter in it, cut through it and remove it, and treat the wound with the previous treatment until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a fractured hip.
Fractures of the hip bones are rare. If a fracture does occur, it typically involves shattering at the edges, a longitudinal splitting, and an inward tilting.  The patient experiences pain, tenderness, and numbness in the affected leg.
To set it, run your hand over it to ascertain the nature of the fracture.  If the fracture is only at the edges, align the edges of the fracture to the best of your ability, until it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is longitudinal or has tilted inward, lay the patient on their stomach to facilitate setting the fracture.  Once aligned, apply a bandage, then a splint of wood or leather, and secure it tightly.
Do not fear the displacement of the fracture or the loosening of the splint.  Fill any depressions in the hips with material to ensure the splint is properly aligned.  Instruct the patient to sleep on their back or their healthy side if a hot swelling develops.
Refrain from manipulating or setting the fracture until the hot swelling subsides.  Apply remedies as previously described to reduce the swelling, then resume setting and securing the fracture as needed.  If fragments or splinters of bone are present, they should not be removed.
Do not touch [the bone], but align it from the outside, as we stated, and leave it secured until healed.
Chapter Eleven.
On setting a fractured humerus.
The humerus is the bone between the elbow and the shoulder. If it is fractured, set it by one of two methods. One is to take a smooth, slightly curved stick of medium thickness, like this:
Tie straps to its ends, then hang it from a high place. The patient sits on a chair, and then lays the fractured arm across the stick so that their armpit is positioned at the center of the stick's curve.  Then hang a heavy object from above it.
Or, an assistant pulls it downwards, and then the physician straightens the fracture with their hands, until the fracture is corrected. The other method is for the patient to lie on their back, and their arm is hung from their neck with a strap.
Then order two assistants. One should hold above the fracture, the other below, and each pull in their direction. If you want a stronger pull, tie a strap below the fracture and another above, and have each assistant pull in their direction.
If the fracture is near the shoulder joint, the strap should be placed under the armpit, and the other under the fracture towards the elbow. Likewise, if the fracture is near the elbow, the strap should be placed at that location and at the elbow itself.
Then gently straighten the fracture without force until the fracture is properly aligned and joined well.  Then tighten it, unless a hot swelling appears. If a hot swelling appears, delay tightening until the seventh day. Apply moistened cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil to it.
Until the swelling subsides. Then, tighten it.  The method of tightening is to place the bandage on the fracture, then wrap a fresh cloth bandage over the first. Then, hold the arm close to the upper arm, and place the hand open on the shoulder.
And hold the cloths and tightening on the upper arm and forearm so that the arm takes the place of splints, if nothing prevents you from doing so and nothing of the broken bone has changed. If you fear that something of the broken bone may change, then use splints.
