It is that you place a splint on the fracture itself, one that is wider and stronger than other splints.  Let there be a space of one finger's width between each splint.  Let the length of the splints be according to the fracture, with an addition of three fingers on each side.
Then tighten the splints with the tightening described in the first chapter.
It is that your tightening should be stronger at the site of the fracture.
And the further from the fracture, the less the tightening. If you see the placement of the splints and the tightening as we described in the section on setting the limb, then do so.
And if you fear the inflammatory swelling, leave the tightening and splints until the seventh day, as we said.  Then examine the ligaments every three days, lest a sore, or inflammation, or difficulty in the limb receiving nourishment arise due to excessive tightening.  All of this will be remedied as we have described.
If you are certain that none of this will occur, do not loosen the ligaments until after many days.  The patient should lie on their back, hand on their abdomen, and a padded armrest filled with wool should be placed under the elbow, even.
And examine at all times, night or day, lest the shape of the fractured limb shift or the ligaments loosen.  Correct all of this with your effort, and make the patient's diet as we previously stated, initially gentle, until the bone is strengthened.
Then his diet should be thickened.  It is the custom of the arm and leg to strengthen in forty days.  At that time, the ligaments should be loosened, and baths should be taken, and treated with ointments appropriate for this, if the fracture is severe and extensive.
Do not loosen the ligaments for fifty days, or until two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On setting a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones.  One is called the radius, the smaller one, which is next to the thumb. The other is the ulna, the larger one, situated beneath the smaller one, at the bottom.  Perhaps the larger ulna bone is fractured alone, or the smaller radius bone alone, or perhaps both are fractured together.
If the upper small radius bone is fractured, its reduction is easy and its healing is quicker. If the lower ulna bone is fractured, the fracture is bad and healing is difficult.  The worst case is when both bones are fractured together.  If the bone fractured is the upper small radius bone...
Therefore, the physician must, when setting it, apply a gentle and slight traction to align it. If the larger ulna bone is fractured, the traction should be stronger. If both bones are fractured, the traction must be very strong.
When setting and pulling the hand, it should be positioned on a cushion, with the thumb raised higher than all the other fingers, the pinky finger lower than the other fingers, and the patient seated upright.
Let the cushion be at his level in height so that the patient does not have to strain.
Then, a servant pulls the arm from below, either with their hand or a bandage, and another servant pulls from above.  The physician then aligns the bone, returning it to the best possible shape.  If the fracture has splinters, they are to be removed.
Return each splinter to its place.  If a splinter remains detached, piercing the skin, and you have no hope of setting it, cut over it and remove it in the manner previously described.  If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, a separate section is devoted to it.
If a hot swelling appears during the initial setting, apply a compress made from rose ointment and white wax, of a medium thickness, and bandage it gently until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the compress, and apply a dressing prepared from mill dust and egg white.  Then apply splints.  The splint placed directly on the fracture should be wider and stronger.  Know that in most cases, six splints will be used for the arm.
If the fracture is in one radius or both radii, then make your traction stronger at the site of the fracture.  And the more you pull upward or downward, the gentler the traction should become, as previously mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
Let the bandages wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, not overly stiff.  Let the thread used for tying be linen of medium thickness, as described.  Examine the limb and ligaments after several days.
If a problem arises, such as irritation of the limb, the limb should be rinsed with warm water until the irritation subsides.  The limb should be left untensioned for a night to rest, then traction should be resumed.
If the traction has become too weak and the bone has shifted, or something similar has occurred, correct it to the best of your ability.  Also, if the limb is deprived of nourishment due to excessive traction, the traction should be eased slightly, and the limb left undisturbed for several days to allow nourishment to reach it, then the traction should be resumed.
If none of the aforementioned issues arise, the splint should not be removed until about twenty days, or thereabouts.  Then suspend the patient's arm from their neck, keeping the arm straight.  The patient should avoid disruptive movements, and sleep on their back.
Know that this fracture of the arm is usually set within thirty, or thirty-two days, and sometimes in twenty-eight days, all depending on the temperaments and strengths of the individual cases.
Chapter Thirteen.
On setting fractures of the hand and fingers.
The metacarpals of the hand and the phalanges of the fingers rarely fracture, but are often bruised.  If the hand does suffer a fracture or bruise, ...
The patient should sit on the floor in a squat position with a chair placed before him.
Then, placing his hand on it stretched out, the bone setter should straighten the fractured bone, and the physician should align it until it is well joined.  Then, if there is no hot swelling, the dressing and splint should be applied, and a brace placed over the area.
And wrap it in a soft cloth. If the fracture is towards the palm of the hand, make a ball-like shape from cloth, and instruct the patient to hold it with their fractured hand, then bind it with a long cloth.
Let the splint be made of soft leather so that the skin heals while accommodating the curvature of the entire hand.  Bind it properly.  If the fracture is outward, a splint should be placed above and another below the hand, to keep the hand open and upright.
Then, the binding should be applied as the hand turns, and the fingers should be connected with a bandage. If the fracture is in one of the fingers, if it is the thumb, align it properly, then bind it with the hand.  If desired, a small upright splint can be used to hold the fracture in place.
Do not move it.  If the fracture is in the other fingers, like the middle, index, ring, or little finger, align them and bind them to the next healthy finger, or bind them all together in sequence, which is better, or place a small upright splint on them.
As we stated concerning the thumb, and examine during and after the setting of the fracture, if there is any heat, treat it as necessary, whenever such things occur, as described repeatedly.
Chapter Fourteen.
In setting the fracture of the thigh.
The thigh bone often breaks and is evident to the touch because it shifts back and forth.  It is set by applying a bandage above the fracture, and another below, with the patient lying on their face.
Then, each attendant pulls the bandage towards their side to straighten it if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. However, if it is near the hip, a soft bandage should be applied, whether of wool or similar material.
At the hip, towards the pubic area, the pull should be upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.  Likewise, if the fracture is near the knee, the bandage should be near the knee to pull downwards. Then, the physician should straighten the bone with both hands to return it to its natural form.
And the bone will heal well.  Then, the dressing and traction should be maintained, unless a hot swelling develops on the limb. If a swelling does develop, leave it for a few days until the hot swelling subsides, then return to the treatment.
As for the traction, a wide, firm bandage should be wrapped around the fracture two or three times, leaving excess material. Then the leg should be twisted so the heel is at the hip joint.  A long thread should be inserted between the thigh and leg near the knee from below. The ends of the thread should be above and on both sides.
Then wrap the excess bandage around the leg and thigh.  Then, apply splints to the thigh, in the location of the fracture itself, and place a single splint on the leg bone.  Fill the gap between the thigh and leg with soft cloths to ensure the traction is even.
Then begin with strong traction in the middle, over the fracture site, with three or four wraps.  As you move the bandage further from the fracture site, make the traction less forceful and softer.
Then, take the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie the adjacent splints above it.  Then, bring the thread ends down to the heel of the leg, and use them to also tighten the ends of the splints on the other side.
To prevent the bandage from shifting, maintain the traction as long as no sores, swelling, or inflammation appear on the limb. If any of these occur, immediately loosen it and correct any problems that have arisen, as we have mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a splinter protruding from the bone, it should be smoothed if possible. Otherwise, cut around it and remove it. Treat the wound as described previously until it heals.  Sometimes a fracture of the thigh can be treated without including the leg with splints.
As we have mentioned regarding the arm and forearm, applying this type of treatment does not result in lameness for the patient.  However, if the arm is treated alone without incorporating the leg, the patient will inevitably experience lameness.
Know that the thigh heals within fifty days, or slightly more or less, depending on variations in temperament and other circumstances.
Chapter Fifteen.
On fractures of the knee-cap.
The knee-cap rarely fractures, but is often injured. If a fracture does occur, it is either a crack or a fragmentation of its parts, with or without a wound.
The treatment is based on examination, and the fractured parts are brought together with the fingers, as smoothly, gently, and expertly as possible.  Then, a bandage and a circular splint are applied if necessary.
Further, the appropriate bandages are applied, and all the conditions described in other fractures, such as inflammation and similar issues, are addressed, each being treated appropriately until healing occurs.
Chapter Sixteen.
In the setting of a fractured leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the ulna.  They are susceptible to the same types of fractures as the arm bones. Therefore, its reduction is like that of the arm, the procedure being the same.  If both bones are fractured, the leg turns in all directions; if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg turns forward; and if the thicker bone is fractured from below, this is self-evident to you.
Therefore, use extension and straightening and bind the splints accordingly, but if the leg fracture is a severe one with many fragments, the extension should be less and gentler, and use more care in setting it.
In the case of the leg, there is an additional procedure beyond that of the arm: once you have straightened the splints and completed all your work, take two pieces of pinewood (used for plastering rooms, or similar palm frond strips).
Choose those of moderate thickness, not thin, and make them the length of the leg from the knee down.  Then, wrap a cloth around each one twice lengthwise. Place one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
Let them extend from the knee to the bottom of the foot.  Then tie the splints in three places, at the ends and the middle.  With this restraint, the leg will not tilt sideways and will be well supported.  A wooden gutter may also be used along the length of the leg.
Place it in the gutter to prevent movement, especially important if the fracture is accompanied by a wound.  Examine the leg daily, carefully watching for swelling or inflammation or anything else unusual. If any of this occurs, treat it as necessary until healed.  The leg bone will be set within thirty days or thereabouts.
Chapter Seventeen.
On Fractures of the Leg and Fingers.
The heel bone is never fractured; the leg bones may be fractured, and the fingers are rarely fractured, but rather more commonly bruised.
If a fracture of the leg bones occurs and you see the bones are protruding, have the patient stand on the ground with a straight foot, then place your foot on the protruding bones and press down to align them.
Until they are back in their proper places, then take the bandage and splint, place a small, flat board with two flat ends underneath the sole of the foot, and tightly secure the foot with the bandages and other necessary materials.
After three or four days, release the bandage, and you will find the bones aligned, whether they were fractured or dislocated.  The procedure is as described.  If a finger is fractured, realign and straighten it according to the instructions for straightening finger fractures.
Then make a splint for the fractured finger, the length of the finger, and slightly wider than the finger itself.  Then place the board, as described, under the foot, and secure it tightly; if two, three, or more bones are fractured.
Place a splint on each finger, sized according to the finger and wrapped in soft cloth.  Secure the board under the foot; each end of the board should extend beyond the foot to ensure proper support.
You should not forget to treat all fractures with the symptoms we have described, and address each symptom appropriately.
Chapter Eighteen.
Fractures of the woman's pubic bone and the man's pubic bone.
When a woman's vagina is fractured, place her in a squatting position and gradually lean her back slightly. Have her supported from behind, then have the midwife pack her vagina with cotton until it is full and appears like a ball.
Then, the woman should be rocked and her hips raised slightly. This will cause the cotton to be expelled until it resembles a ball at the vaginal opening, and the bone fracture will be healed.  A pad should be placed on her back, and when she wishes to urinate, the cotton should be gently removed so that she can urinate.
Then return it to its original position, as it was initially inserted, and return to the first pad.  Do this for seven days or so, and it will heal. If you wish, take a sheep's bladder, attach a short tube to its opening, and insert the entire bladder into the vagina.
Then blow forcefully into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vagina; the fracture will be healed. Afterward, pack it with cotton and continue the treatment as described previously until it heals.  Similarly, if the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, these methods are employed for its repair.
Its treatment is the same as described for the hip bone.  The correct approach to these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, will be evident to someone with some experience and who understands this book.  Indeed, the fracture itself often provides guidance.
Follow the correct method for its repair and strengthening.  If a man's penis is fractured, take the windpipe of a goose, insert the penis into it, then place a bandage of cloth over it and bind it. Leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On the setting of bone fractures if there is a wound.
If a fracture is accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large, such as the femur or humerus, or similar, one should promptly perform bloodletting if the conditions for bloodletting, as previously discussed, permit.
If the wound is bleeding, then one should quickly cauterize it by applying powdered alum if nothing else is available. Then, begin setting the fracture on that same day and do not delay.
If no hot swelling occurs, then if a hot swelling does occur, postpone setting the fracture until the ninth day, until the hot swelling subsides. Do not approach it at all on the third and fourth day, as it will be exposed to undesirable symptoms.
If the fractured bone is protruding and exposed, one should gently attempt to return and align it with one's hands and a slight extension.  If you cannot return and align it with your hands, return it using this instrument, which is made of iron, seven or eight fingers long. And its width should match the size of the wound.
Therefore, the physician should take three or four of them, as needed for treatment in each type of fracture.  Let them be slightly rounded and thick so as not to bend when prodded during use.
It should have a sharp tip with a bend at the tip, thicker at the top and significantly thinner from the middle downwards.  This is its image:.
It is called in Greek *permos*, meaning a small lever.  The sharp, bent tip should be placed on the protruding bone end, and pushed once until the bone is returned to a level alignment, and the fractured ends are aligned.
If the fractured end is thin and the instrument does not grasp it well, then the end of that bone should be cut to allow the instrument to hold it properly.  If the bone cannot be realigned as described, then cut it with a similar incision to those previously mentioned.
Or saw it with one of the saws, as best you can.  Then remove any remaining roughness and thin layers from the bone. If, after aligning the bone, the patient experiences severe, unbearable pain, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can return it to its natural position, do so, as you will greatly benefit the patient. Once the bone is properly set, dip a cloth in a dark, astringent drink, especially if it is summer, and do not place any ointments or anything greasy on the wound.
So that no putrefaction or decay occurs. Then, utilize splints when finished setting the bone. Leave the wound uncovered by cutting an opening in the dressings with scissors, sized to match the wound.
Beware, with utmost caution, of tightening the wound with the fracture. Often, the ignorant physicians do this, and bring about either death, or ulceration or discharge to their patients.  Let your tightening be gentle and loose, unlike the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or large, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we described, and if the patient experiences troublesome pain in the area, do not apply splints. Instead, make dressings of strong cloths, in the position of the splints, and secure them tightly.
If, after a day or two, you see pus beginning to form in the wound, remove the cloth soaked in the liquid. Then use the bandages and ointments with which we customarily treat wounds, such as the four-fold ointment, and the like.  You should also loosen the bandages.
Examine the wound each evening and morning until it heals and is cured.  The limb should be positioned so that pus drains easily downwards. If the wound takes many days to close and the pus does not stop, know that there are small pieces of bone fragments.
Then you should probe the wound.  Remove any detached fragments, and any fragments that remain unseparated, and pierce the limb, causing pain, then cut and remove them in any way you can.
If the wound shows signs of cold, ulceration, or other forms of corruption or putrefaction, you should treat each symptom with the corresponding remedy described in its respective section.
If a large, prominent bone such as the femur or humerus, or similar large bones, is fractured, do not attempt to pull or remove it. Often death results from this; instead, leave it until it rots.  Perhaps it will fall out on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
On treating the suppuration which occurs after some fractures.
This suppuration often occurs after the fracture has healed, especially near the joints.  It disfigures the limb and may prevent the limb from its natural function.
If the suppuration is fresh, use astringent medicines such as frankincense, myrrh,  henna, acacia, and the like, taking some or all of these and mixing them with a styptic liquid, egg white, or vinegar.
Apply this to the suppuration with meticulousness, firmly bandaging it, leaving the bandage on for many days, then removing it and repeating the application until the suppuration is gone, God willing, or by applying a tight lead plate.
Lead has the property of removing all that which is suppurative in the limbs.  If the suppuration has hardened and become severe, and necessity dictates its removal, make an incision at the top and cut or scrape away the excess pus until it is gone. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In treating a fracture, if the limb is subsequently slender and not in its original form.
If a fractured bone is set and the limb remains thin and weak afterward, this can have many causes. One cause may be excessive loosening and improper binding of the ligaments; or excessive tightening of the ligaments, preventing nourishment from reaching the limb; or excessive and improper manipulation; or excessive movements at inappropriate times; or a deficiency and weakness of blood in the patient's body.
The treatment for this is to nourish the patient and strengthen their body so that their blood increases, using baths, bringing joy and happiness, and similar measures.  Then, apply asphalt to the limb to draw much nourishment to it, or continue its manipulation with lukewarm water. Until nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural shape.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of fractured bones that have been rejoined crookedly and prevented from functioning properly.
If a limb, after being set, shows crookedness or a protuberance of the broken bone, or entanglement, and the appearance of the limb is thus unsightly. However, if the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then one should not accept the claim of those who say to break the limb from the head, and many ignorant physicians and setters do this in our country.
This action is highly reprehensible and leads to great risk, and the least of which is injury.  But if the crookedness and entanglement are recent, it is appropriate to bathe with water that has cooked herbs that relax, such as mallow leaves and root, and crown flower and the like.
And apply relaxing bandages, such as carefully made diachylon, or take the sap of the mallow root, mix it with chicken fat and butter, and apply as a dressing, or take ripe figs, grind them with pigeon droppings and similar remedies known as resolvents of inflammation.
And the entanglement may be resolved with constant gentle massage by hand, and the limb is moved in all directions at all times. If the crookedness is old, severe, and hardened, and treatment with iron is necessary.
Then it is necessary to cut above it, release the bone connection, and cut any excess entanglement or bone with finer cuts, using gentleness and care. Then treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jaw is a dislocation of a joint from its position, hindering movement, disfiguring the limb, and causing the patient severe pain and suffering.  Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, one must promptly attempt to restore it.
Do not delay it at all; if delayed, the area swells and becomes a mass, making restoration of the jaw difficult.  Therefore, it should not be delayed, nor moved, nor stretched while it is swollen, for it often causes the patient painful spasms and suffering.
However, if this occurs, the patient should first be bled, then allowed to rest until the swelling subsides somewhat.  Then, the limb should be bathed with warm water and ointment, and the jaw gently restored.  Each limb should be treated as described in its respective section.
The chapters on the jaw are also arranged, as was done with fractures, from the upper part of the body to the lower part.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of the lower jaw.
The jaws dislocate rarely.  Their dislocation is of two types: either they move slightly from their place, becoming somewhat relaxed, or they completely and entirely dislocate until they are relaxed. To the chest, until the patient's saliva flows and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his jaw, and his tongue is unable to speak.  If the dislocation is slight, however, it will often return to its place quite easily.
If the dislocation is complete, the repositioning must be expedited and not delayed.  A servant should hold the patient's head, and the physician should insert the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the patient's mouth, if the jaw is dislocated on one side.
Or he should insert both thumbs if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, and the rest of his fingers should be used externally to align it.  He should instruct the patient to relax his jaw and allow it to move in any direction.  The physician should then align the jaw and push it until it returns to its place.
If repositioning is difficult, especially if both jaws are involved, apply a warm water compress and ointment to ease the repositioning.  Do not delay repositioning, as we have said.  Once the jaws have returned to their proper position, and the patient's mouth is closed and no longer relaxed, then apply bandages made from linen with a plaster composed of wax, ointment, and rose petals.
Then gently bind it with a loose bandage. The patient should sleep on their back with their head propped up between two pillows, so as not to move it to the right or left, or to chew anything. Instead, their food should be soft and easily consumed.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw has healed, the patient may eat as they please.  They should use gentle care and not force their mouth open while eating, drinking, or yawning, until the jaw has healed and the injury is completely recovered.
If the jaws, having been dislocated at some point, do not return to their proper position, frequent fevers, constant headaches, and possibly abdominal discomfort and repeated vomiting may occur.  If you see this, know that the condition is severe.
Often, those who experience this condition for ten days die.
Chapter Twenty-five.
On the reduction of the clavicle and shoulder joint.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate from the inside due to its connection to the chest. It may dislocate outward, which is apparent to the touch.  To reduce it, the patient should lie on their back with their arms outstretched.
Then press firmly on the area with your hand; it will return. Then apply a bandage and splints, and secure them.  As for its end near the shoulder, to which it is connected, it does not usually dislocate. If it does, it should be reduced and aligned as described, as best you can.
Then apply a bandage, splints, and a tight wrap, and instruct the patient to remain still and quiet until healed.  With this same treatment, the shoulder joint can be reduced if it also dislocates.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In reducing the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: one is toward the armpit, downward; the second is toward the chest; and the third, rarely, is upward, away from the shoulder.
It does not dislocate behind the shoulder blade or in front of the nerve.  Most often, it dislocates and comes out downward toward the armpit, particularly in those with little muscle because it comes out and goes in quickly in them.
However, in those with a lot of muscle, it is the opposite; that is, it comes out with difficulty and goes in with difficulty.  Sometimes, a person may experience a blow or fall, causing the shoulder to swell and become hot.  They may think it is dislocated.  It is necessary to examine this carefully to determine its true condition.
Then, in treating it, if the dislocation is downward toward the armpit, when you place the dislocated shoulder next to the healthy one, you will find a clear difference.  You will find a distortion in the head of the dislocated shoulder.  And under the armpit, when touching, the head of the dislocated shoulder feels like an egg.
And the patient cannot raise their arm to their ear, nor perform any other movements.  Similarly, if it dislocated toward the chest or upward, you will find this obvious by touching, and it is not hidden.
This dislocation may be easily reduced if it is fresh or if the patient is a child.  To reduce it, have an attendant raise the arm upwards.  Then, place your thumbs under the armpit and raise the joint firmly upwards to its proper place.  The attendant should raise and extend the arm upwards.
Then, lower it downwards.  It will return quickly. If it does not return by the method described, and the dislocation has been present for many days, the patient should bathe in warm water and use remedies that loosen and soften, such as boiled  *al-khatmi* (marshmallow root), fenugreek, and *iklil al-malik* (king's crown) in water, and apply these.
Then, the patient lies on their back, and a ball of wool of moderate firmness is placed under their armpit.  The physician then places their heel on the ball, and holds the patient's hand and extends it downwards.
And the physician pushes the ball with their foot, and it returns to its place.  Or, if you wish, reduce it in this way: have a man taller than the patient stand on the side.  He places his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raises their armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. Another attendant pulls the patient's hand downwards towards their stomach. If the patient is light, something should be attached to weigh them down.  This will cause the joint to return immediately.  Other methods of reduction may also be employed.
Another method is to place a long, round-headed wooden board on the ground, similar to a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin.  A soft cloth is placed on the head of the board, and the patient stands along the board's length, and the board is placed under the patient's armpit.
Then, the patient extends their hand downwards on the opposite side, and also extends their body forcefully on the other side.  The joint will return to its proper place quickly.  If reduction is difficult using all the methods previously described, use this treatment: take a piece of wood approximately two cubits long. Its width should be about four fingers and its thickness about two fingers.  It should have a round head to easily enter the depth of the armpit, like this:
Then, a soft cloth is tied to the round head to prevent the board from injuring the patient. The board is placed under the armpit. The entire hand and arm are extended downward along the board. The board is fastened to the upper arm, forearm, and hand.
Then, the arm is placed on a crossbar. The arm is extended downward, and the rest of the body is left hanging from the opposite side. The joint will then enter its place immediately.  If it has entered in any way possible, a moderately sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then, a dressing prepared from mill dust, frankincense, and egg white is applied to the entire shoulder, moving from the top downward.  The ball is tightly secured under the armpit.  A bandage is wrapped around the dressing from above. The hand is then suspended by tying it to the neck.
The hand should remain immobile for seven days.  The patient's diet should be kept light to strengthen the affected limb, for this hastens recovery.  Then, after seven or five days, the bandage is removed and the patient is encouraged to move the arm. If it remains firm and does not relax, the injury is healed.
If the joint frequently dislocates due to moisture or another cause, cauterization with three small cauterizing instruments should be applied, as described in the chapter on cauterization. If all this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days, and the joint still does not hold,
If the bandage and pressure are repeated many times, and the joint still does not hold, dislocating and relaxing, and the arm cannot be raised, know that the nerve in the shoulder area is severed or stretched or relaxed.  Then know that the joint will never return to its proper position.
For a dislocated jaw, which is near the chest and breast, and behind the head, return it by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns to its place.  Use all other methods of bandaging and treatment until it heals.  If after healing, the limb feels numb or its movement is slow,
The patient should use a hot bath frequently to soften the numbness and restore it to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the treatment of a dislocated elbow.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and also returns with difficulty.  It dislocates in all directions, especially forward or backward. Its dislocation is not hidden from you, as it is under observation and touch, so you can tell precisely how it has dislocated.
If you compare the dislocated elbow to the healthy one, the dislocation will be clearly apparent by the hollowing of the joint.  The arm cannot be bent, nor can the shoulder be placed against it.  You should act quickly to relocate the dislocation before a hot swelling appears.
If a hot swelling appears, its relocation will be difficult, and it may not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backward.  This is the worst of all dislocations, the most painful, and often leads to death.
If it is possible to relocate it, a helper should extend his arms, and the physician's hands should be positioned above and below the elbow. The helper pushes on the joint with both thumbs or the base of his palms until it returns to its proper position.
If the dislocation is forward, the hand may be bent once until its palm strikes the shoulder opposite it.  If the dislocation does not return, use extremely strong traction.  Two helpers should pull on the arm.
Two helpers also hold the patient to prevent displacement during traction.  Then the arm is rotated in every direction after wrapping a folded cloth or wide bandage around it.  If the physician is working with his hands on the joint, he should lubricate it with ointment to facilitate the smooth repositioning of the joint.
Then the joint is pushed forcefully until it returns.  After it returns, a bandage with a compressive and drying agent (like egg white) should be applied tightly, and the arm should be suspended from the patient's neck and left for several days.
Then it is removed.  If the joint is firmly in place, remove the bandage and leave it.  If you see the joint is not yet firm, reapply the bandage and support, and leave it for several more days until it firms up.  Then remove it. If swelling occurs after the joint has returned and the bandage is removed.
If movement is difficult, use moist heat in a bath, gentle massage, and rubbing until it softens.  Or, place a thick, fatty sheep's fleece on the joint, bandage it, and leave it in place for a day and a night.  Then remove it and put him in a bath.  If he sweats, massage the joint gently.
Then repeat the sheep's fleece application one, two, and three times, along with baths, until it softens.  If you prefer, apply dampened, finely ground cow dung mixed with butter and bandage it.  Do this several times, and it will soften and return to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-eight.
On the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist frequently dislocates, and its reduction is easier than other joints. However, it is necessary to promptly reduce the dislocation the moment it occurs, before inflammation or a hot swelling develops in the area.
To reduce the dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a board.  A servant should extend their hand, and the physician should place their hand on the prominence of the joint and push until it returns.  However, one must check whether the dislocation has moved to the palm side of the hand.
Let the patient place the back of their hand on the board during extension and reduction. If the dislocation is prominent on the back of the hand, then let the palm of their hand rest on the board so that the physician's hand is on the same prominence of the joint.  If it returns immediately, then fine. Otherwise, apply a bandage that relieves inflammation.
Leave it be; do not attempt to reduce it again.  After a few days, you cannot reduce it, and the joint will remain crooked, without harming the patient, except that the hand will become weak and unable to grasp anything.
At that point, you will know that the nerve is severed or compressed; there is no remedy except to tie it, perhaps it will help, or perhaps not.  Once the wrist is reduced, apply the bandage we described, then tie it tightly, and leave it for five days.
Then, the bandage is removed, and the hand is exercised.  If its movement is difficult, and some stiffness is apparent, soften it with warm water and massage repeatedly until it softens.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may dislocate in any direction.  If a finger dislocates to the back or palm of the hand, straighten the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumb until it returns to place, then bandage the finger head and support it in the direction it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and stretch it until it stands upright that day. If it is in the evening, bandage it in the same manner. Continue to loosen the bandage during the day and exercise the movement, and bandage it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
And do the same if it dislocates to the palm of the hand, bandaging it towards that same direction.  Do the same as your initial treatment until it heals; do likewise if it dislocates in any other direction.
Chapter Thirty.
In treating the dislocation of the vertebrae.
When a vertebra in the back or neck is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no treatment because death quickly comes to the afflicted person.
A sign of this is that the afflicted person's stool is expelled involuntarily; they cannot retain it.  Often, some of their limbs, either their legs or arms or one of them, become relaxed.  If only one vertebra is displaced, it often slips further.
Its displacement can occur in four directions.  That which displaces backward is called a hump.  There is no treatment for a hump that developed from childhood, and no cure at all.  However, for one that resulted from a fall, a blow, or something similar...
The ancients have described various treatments with lengthy explanations, most of which are of little use.  I have condensed this into a smaller amount, sufficient for a considerable portion of what they presented, clarifying the meaning and illustrating the mechanics differently than they described and explained.
I say that a hump that develops in the front of the chest is untreatable and incurable, as is one to the sides.  However, treatment is possible for those that occur in the back, specifically by the method I describe:  the afflicted person should be placed on their face.
On a level platform near a wall, a moist mat should be spread beneath to prevent the chest from being injured. Then a vertical board, embedded in a hole in the ground, is placed near his head, and another board near his feet, at the other end of the platform.
A servant holds the board, not fastened in the hole, and another servant holds the other, in the same manner.  Then a soft, tight bandage is wrapped around the patient's chest and under their armpits. The end of the bandage is extended to the board near the head and tied to it.
Then another bandage is tightened over his hips, knees, and ankles.  Then all the bandages are gathered and tied to the other board near his feet.  Each servant pulls the board in their direction with the bandages, and the boards remain in their embedded positions.
However, the boards are not fastened, as we said. The physician places his hands firmly on the hump to restore it, or he places a board on it, then leans on the board with his feet until it returns.  If this treatment does not work, take a board about three cubits long.
A cavity is dug in the wall, near the patient, where the end of the board fits. Then the middle of the board is placed on the hump. The physician places his feet on the other end and pulls firmly to compress the hump.
And it returns to its place.  If you wish, you can do this with a hand-wound coil, by planting two boards a cubit long in the ground at the patient's head and another two at their feet, at the ends of the shop.
Place a span between each board. A hole is made in each board for the coil to pass through.  All the boards are firmly anchored to the ground to prevent movement. A rounded stick, the winding coil, is placed inside the holes in the boards, then the bandage is wound around it.
At the end of it [the coil] is a hole where a stick a span long, to be wound, is secured.  The same is done for the other two boards. Then, the bandage tied around the patient's chest is tightened on the coil by the head, and the bandages on the legs are tightened on the coil by the feet.
Then, a servant stands by each coil, twisting the coil with the winding tool.  The physician adjusts the hump as previously described.  This is a picture of the coil, the shop, and the patient.
Then, after the protrusion returns and the area is aligned, the dried bandage should be applied, followed by a poultice.  Then, a splint made of wood, whose width is about three fingers or so and whose length covers the hump, should be placed over the bandage.
And place some sound beads upon it.  Then tie with the necessary bandage, and the patient should eat soft food until cured.  If some protuberance remains in the area at the end of the healing process, then one should use the softening and relaxing remedies, along with the splint described, for a considerable time.
A lead plate may also be used in this case.  If a protrusion appears at the end of the back beads, it may be mistaken for dislocation.  This might be an extra bone that has protruded.  Do not treat it with this therapy, for it might cause death.
Chapter Thirty-one.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and not to the slight displacement and concavity that affects other joints.  The hip joint dislocates in four ways.
This is because it dislocates inwards, outwards, forwards, and backwards.  It dislocates inwards most often, and rarely forwards or backwards.  A sign of dislocation inwards is that if you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the affected leg will be longer.
The knee will be more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient will not be able to bend their leg at the groin.  The area next to the groin will be swollen and distinctly swollen, showing that the head of the thigh bone has moved there.
The signs of dislocation outwards are the opposite of these signs.  The sign of dislocation forward is that the patient can fully extend the leg, but cannot bend it without pain in the knee.  If they try to walk, they cannot do so forward.
The patient will have difficulty urinating and the groin will be painful.  When walking, the patient will place their weight on the heel.  A sign of dislocation backward is that the patient cannot straighten the knee or bend it before bending the groin, and the leg will also be shorter than the other.
The groin will be relaxed, and the head of the thigh bone will be clearly visible near the hip area.  As for the treatment of these dislocations, if the dislocation has been present for some time and the patient has not attempted to reduce it, and it remains in its current state, then there is no cure.
Therefore, it should not be allowed to occur.  For a recently dislocated joint, regardless of whether it's one of the four types of dislocation, attempt to rotate the joint, extending it inward and outward, and moving it right and left, for it may...
If it returns without further treatment, then if it doesn't return this way, one should prepare a strong assistant who will extend the leg from below, either with their hands or with a bandage tied above the knee, and another assistant will extend it from above.
By placing their hands under his armpits, then tightening a soft bandage around the upper thigh, a third assistant will hold the end of the bandage, and the extension will be either in front from the groin towards the collarbone, or from behind towards the back.
All of them will pull simultaneously until the patient's body is lifted from the ground and remains suspended.  This type of extension is common to all four types of dislocation. If the dislocation returns as we have described, then otherwise, each type requires its own specific treatment.
For its specific return if the dislocation is inward, the patient should lie on their sound side, then a bandage should be placed on the upper thigh, between the head of the thigh and the area below the groin.
Then the bandage is pulled upward from the groin toward the upper body, towards the collarbone.  Then a strong assistant, with both arms, grasps the thick part of the affected thigh and pulls outwards with great force, and it will return to its place.
This type is easier than other methods of returning this limb.  If you are unable to achieve its re-entry with this treatment method, then you should tie both of the patient's legs with a strong, soft bandage around both ankles and knees.
Each bandage should be approximately four finger-widths apart.  The affected leg should be extended further than the other by two finger-widths.  The patient should be suspended from the head by a wooden beam in the house, with the beam positioned at a height of approximately two arm-lengths from the ground.
Then a strong boy should grasp the head of the thigh, and another boy should hold onto the patient.  The boy holding the thigh should push with force, and the joint will quickly return to its proper place.  And for its specific return, if the dislocation is outward...
The patient should lie on a bed, as described for the patient with a hump, and the bandage should be tightened around his affected leg and chest.  Then, two beams should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head.  A further beam should be placed firmly in the middle of the bed.
Damp cloths should be wrapped around it to prevent harm to the patient.  The beam should be placed between the patient's thighs, to prevent downward displacement during stretching.  Then each attendant should pull in their direction, and the physician should align the hip with his hands.  If it returns to its place...
Otherwise, place the board upon him and compress it, as we described for the humpback, except that the patient must lie on his healthy side.  If the dislocation is forward, the patient's leg must be stretched while in this same position on the bed.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected groin, then presses it with his other hand.  At the same time, the pressure should be extended downward towards the knee.  If the dislocation is backward, the patient should not be stretched downward.
He should not be elevated from the ground, but rather placed on a firm surface.  Also, his hip and thigh should be rotated outward, as we described from his lying on the bed on his face with the bandages tight.
As we said before, compression with the board should also be applied to the area where the joint has dislocated.  When the hip joint returns properly, a clear sign, which you should know, is that the patient's legs can be stretched.
If you see both legs are even, and the patient can flex and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the limb has returned to its proper place.  Then, align the thighs, apply the bandage, and secure it with a turban.  Make sure the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient should remain at rest for three or four days.  Then, remove the bandage and dressing, and compare the legs. If you find that they are of equal size, know that the fracture has healed. Allow the patient to walk. However, if you see any sign of weakness, [do not allow walking yet].
Return and bandage it again, tightening it as you did initially.  Leave it for another three days.  Then, remove it and have the patient walk slowly for a few days until the limb is strong again, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On the treatment of a fractured knee.
The knee can fracture in three ways: inwards, outwards, and downwards (i.e., backwards). It does not fracture forwards. A sign of a fractured knee is to instruct the patient to press their leg against their thigh.
If the leg does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is fractured. To treat all types of fractures: the patient should sit, extending their legs if able, with an assistant behind them holding their middle and slightly bending them backwards. Then, you should sit on their thighs.
Lean your back against their body, place their leg between your legs, then grasp their knee with both hands, interlocking your fingers on it.  Press firmly with both hands on the sides of the knee. A second assistant should extend the leg until the knee returns to its position.
A sign that it has returned to its place is that the leg will adhere to the thigh, softly and without force. Then bandage them together, and secure the leg to the thigh with a bandage for three or four days, then remove it. Only allow light walking for a few days until strength returns.
If you cannot return it with the method described, then use strong traction with the bandages previously described for treating the hip until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the treatment of a dislocated heel.
The heel may dislocate slightly or completely.  Its dislocation can be either inward or outward.  A sign of dislocation is that the heel appears swollen and prominent on the side to which it has dislocated.
As for treating a slight displacement, it is easy to return it by gently pulling and aligning it until it returns.  However, if it is completely dislocated, the patient should sit, and a strong servant should hold him from behind in the middle of his back.
Then, using your right hand, grasp the top of his foot, and with your left hand, grasp the bottom of the foot at the ankle.  Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with your left, twice.  Then, push the front of the foot towards the leg with your right hand, and extend it with your left, without force.
Repeat this twice as described.  Then, in the third time, push the front of the foot towards the leg while pulling the heel.  If it returns in one or two attempts and you see the foot is aligned, then stop.  Otherwise, repeat the procedure, as it will return.
If it does not return with the method described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground.  Securely place a firmly planted wooden stake between his thighs, wrapped in cloth to prevent injury.  Then have a servant secure the patient's thigh.
Then another servant extends the leg, either with their hands or with a bandage tied around the patient's neck.  Each servant should pull in the opposite direction.  The wooden stake remains positioned between the patient's thighs, holding him steady to prevent his body from falling during the extension.  Then the physician aligns the joint with their hands.
Another servant holds the healthy leg down, for if the joint returns quickly, it is correct.  If the joint returns and its return is confirmed, then take the bandage and splint and secure them tightly with bandages.  Tie the foot with the bandage downward.  Be mindful of the nerve that lies above the heel from behind.
So that the bandage is not too tight and causes pain, then leave it for two or three days.  If the bandage loosens, tighten it, then release it on the third or fourth day.  The patient should refrain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this period, there is a risk that the joint will dislocate and be irreparably damaged, and subsequent treatment will not be successful.  If a hot swelling appears, use the treatments described previously in other parts of the treatment and healing, until it disappears.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the treatment of dislocated toes.
One should gently realign the dislocated toes by a slight stretch, without force, which is easily accomplished.  If the dislocation is in one of the toes of the foot, the patient should place their foot on a level surface.
Or, while standing like a walker, place your foot on the protruding joint and press it with your foot firmly until it returns to its proper position, and you see no protrusion at the site.
Then, place a board under the sole of their foot that encompasses the entire foot, having two ends.  Tie it firmly for three days.  Then loosen it and keep the patient from walking for many days until it strengthens, and a recurrence is avoided, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On the types of dislocation that occur with a wound, or with a fracture, or with both.
When such a thing occurs, and you attempt to treat and manipulate it, death often follows.
Therefore, only a skilled surgeon, experienced, gentle, compassionate, careful, and not rash or reckless, should undertake the treatment of such a case.  Initially, they should use only medications to alleviate the inflammatory swelling and allow the patient to recover.
By God's grace, except for cases where you hope for the patient's recovery from harm with mild illness and some hope appears, then return [the treatment] at once, in the beginning, before the inflammatory swelling occurs.
If the member returns as desired, use the treatment that relieves inflammatory swellings and treat the wound with appropriate drying ointments. If the dislocation is associated with a fracture and detached fragments have occurred in the bone, then remove them. Follow in this regard what we have mentioned regarding simple diseases in the preceding sections.
Strive diligently and purify yourself from entering the path of uncertainty, as previously advised.  That will preserve your reputation and safeguard your presentation, God willing.
